Fact	JUDGMENT <NAME>, C.J.
Fact	1.
Fact	These three writ petitions raise the important question of the validity of the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964.
Fact	2.
Fact	Writ Petition No.
Fact	153 of 1966, is filed by the petitioners therein against the State of Punjab and the Financial Commissioner, Punjab.
Fact	The petitioners are the son, daughter and grand-daughters of one <NAME>, who died on July 30, 1953.
Fact	The Financial Commissioner, in revision against the order made by the Additional Commissioner, Jullundur Division, held by an order dated January 22, 1962 that an area of 418 standard acres and 9 1/4 units was surplus in the hands of the petitioners under the provisions of the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act X of 1953, read with s. 10-B thereof.
Fact	The petitioners, alleging that the relevant provisions of the said Act whereunder the said area was declared surplus were void on the ground that they infringed their rights under cls.
Fact	(f) and (g) of Art.
Fact	19 and Art.
Fact	14 of the Constitution, filed a writ in this Court  under Art.
Fact	32 of the Constitution for a direction that the Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951, Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955, Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, insofar as they affected their fundamental rights were unconstitutional and inoperative and for a direction that s. 10-B of the said Act X of 1953 was void as violative of Arts.
Fact	14 and 19(1)(f) and (g) of the Constitution.
Fact	3.
Fact	Writ Petitions Nos.
Fact	202 and 203 of 1966 were filed by different petitioners under Art.
Fact	32 of the Constitution for a declaration that the Mysore Land Reforms Act (Act 10 of 1962) as amended by Act 14 of 1965, which fixed ceilings on land holdings and conferred ownership of surplus lands on tenants infringed Arts.
Fact	14, 19 and 31 of the Constitution and, therefore, was unconstitutional and void.
Fact	4.
Fact	The States of Punjab and Mysore, inter alia, contended that the said Acts were saved from attack on the ground that they infringed the fundamental rights of the petitioners by reason of the Constitution (Se venteenth Amendment) Act, 1964, which, by amending Art.
Fact	31-A of the Constitution and including the said two Acts in the 9th Schedule thereto, had placed them beyond attack.
Fact	5.
Fact	In Writ Petition No.
Fact	153 of 1966, 7 parties intervened.
Fact	In Writ Petition No.
Fact	202 of 1966 one party intervened.
Fact	In addition, in the first petition, notice was given to the Advocates General of various States.
Fact	All the learned counsel appearing for the parties, the Advocates General appearing for the States and the learned counsel for the interveners have placed their respective viewpoints exhaustively before us.
Fact	We are indebted to all of them for their thorough preparation and clear exposition of the difficult questions of law that were raised in the said petitions.
Argument	6.
Statute	At the outset it would be convenient to place briefly the respective contentions under different heads : (1) The Constitution is intended to be permanent and, therefore, it cannot be amended in a way which would injure, maim or destroy its indestructible character.
Statute	(2) Th e word "amendment" implies such an addition or change within the lines of the original instrument as will effect an improvement or better carry out the purpose for which it was framed and it cannot be so construed as to enable the <ORG> to destroy the permanent character of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) The fundamental rights are a part of the basic structure of the Constitution and, therefore, the said power can be exercised only to preserve rather than destroy the essence of those rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) The limits on the power to amend are implied in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , for the expression "amend" has a limited meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	The wide phraseology used in the Constitution in other Articles, such as "repeal" and "re-enact" indicates that art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 only enables a modification of the Articles within the framework of the Constitution and not a destruction of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	(5) The debates in <ORG>, particularly the speech of Mr. <NAME>, the first Prime Minister of India, and the reply of Dr. <NAME>, who piloted the Bill  disclose clearly that it was never the intention of the makers of the Constitution by putting in Art.
Precedent	368 to enable the <ORG> to repeal the fundamental rights; the circumstances under which the amendment moved by Mr. <NAME>, one of the members of <ORG>, was withdrawn and Art.
Precedent	368 was finally adopted, support the contention that amendment of Part III is outside the scope of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	(6) Part III of the Constitution is a self-contained Code and its provisions are elastic enough to meet all reasonable requirements of changing situations.
Precedent	(7) The power to amend is sought to be derived from three sources, namely, (i) by implication under Art.
Precedent	368 itself; the procedure to amend culminating in the amendment of the Constitution necessarily implies that power, (ii) the power and the limits of the power to amend are implied in the Articles sought to be amended, and (iii) Art.
Precedent	368 only lays down the procedure to amend, but the power to amend is only the legislative power conferred on the Parl iament under Arts.
Precedent	245, 246 and 248 of the Constitution.
Precedent	(8) The definition of "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) of the Constitution includes every branch of law, statutory, constitutional, etc., and therefore, the power to amend in whichever branch it may be classified, if it takes away or abridges fundamental rights would be void thereunder.
Precedent	(9) The impugned amendment detracts from the jurisdiction of <ORG> under Art.
Precedent	226 of the Constitution and also the legislative powers of the States and therefore it falls within the scope of the proviso to Art.
Precedent	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	7.
RatioOfTheDecision	The said summary, though not exhaustive, broadly gives the various nuances of the contentions raised by the learned counsel, who question the validity of the 17th Amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have not noticed the other arguments of Mr. <NAME>, which are peculiar to the Writ Petition No.
RatioOfTheDecision	153 of 1966 as those questions do not arise for decision, in the view we are taking on the common questions.
RatioOfTheDecision	8.
RatioOfTheDecision	On behalf of the Union and the States the following points were pressed : ( 1) A Constitutional amendment is made is exercise of the sovereign power and not legislative power of <ORG> and, therefore, it partakes the quality and character of the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) The real distinction is between a rigid and a flexible Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The distinction is based upon the express limits of the amending power.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) The provisions of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 are clear and unequivocal and there is no scope for invoking implied limitations on that power : further the doctrine of implied power has been rejected by the American courts and jurists.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) The object of the amending clause in a flexible Constitution is to enable the <ORG> to amend the Constitution in order to express the will of the people according to the changing course of events and if amending power is restricted by implied limitations, the Constitution itself might be destroyed by revolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, it is a safety valve and an alternative for a violent change by revolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	(5) There are no basic and non-basic features of the Cons titution; everything in the Constitution is basic and it can be amended in order to help the future growth and progress of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	(6) Debates in <ORG> cannot be relied upon for construing Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 of the Constitution and even if they can be, there is nothing in the debates to prove positively that fundamental rights were excluded from amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	(7) Most of the amendments are made out of political necessity : they involve questions, such as, how to exercise power, how to make the lot of the citizens better and the like and, therefore, not being judicial questions, they are outside the court's jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	(8) The language of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is clear, categorical, imperative and universal; on the other hand, the language of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) is such as to admit qualifications or limitations and, therefore, the <ORG> must construe them in such a manner as that Article could not control Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	(9) In order to enforce the Directive Principles the Constitution was amended from time to time and the g reat fabric of <ORG> has been built since 1950 on the basis that the Constitution could be amended and, therefore, any reversal of the previous decisions would introduce economic chaos in our country and that, therefore, the burden is very heavy upon the petitioners to establish that the fundamental rights cannot be amended under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	(10) Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and the 9th Schedule do not affect the power of <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 or the legislative power of the States though the area of their operation is limited and, therefore, they do not fall within the scope of the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	9.
RatioOfTheDecision	The aforesaid contentions only represent a brief summary of the elaborate arguments advanced by learned counsel.
RatioOfTheDecision	We shall deal in appropriate context with the other points mooted before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	10.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be convenient to read the material provisions of the Constitution at this stage.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 13(1) .
RatioOfTheDecision	.

RatioOfTheDecision	.
RatioOfTheDecision	.

Statute	(3) In this article, unless the context otherwise requires, -  (a) "law" includes any Ordinance, order, bye-law, rule regulation, notification, custom or usage having in the territory of India the force of law.
Statute	Article 31-A(1).

Statute	Article 31-B.
Statute	Without prejudice to the generality of the provisions contained in article 31-A , none of the Acts and Regulations specified in the Ninth Schedule nor any of the provisions thereof shall be deemed to be void, or ever to have become, void, on the ground that such Act, Regulation or provision is inconsistent with, or takes away or abridges any of the rights conferred by, any provisions of this Part, and notwithstanding any judgment decree or order of any court or tribunal to the contrary, each of the said Acts and Regulations shall, subject to the power of any competent <ORG> to repeal or amend it, continue in force.
Statute	11.
Statute	In the Ninth Schedule to the Constitution the Mysore Land Reforms Act, 1961, (Mysore Act 10 of 1962) is included as item 51 and the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act, 1953 (Punjab Act 10 of 1953) is included as item 54.
Statute	The definition of "estate" was amended and the Ninth Schedu le was amended by including therein the said two <ORG> by the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964.
Statute	12.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result of the said amendments is that both the said <ORG> dealing with estates, within their wide definition introduced by the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, having been included in the Ninth Schedule, are placed beyond any attack on the ground that their provisions are inconsistent with or take away or abridge any of the rights conferred by Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is common case that if the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, was constitutionally valid, the said <ORG> could not be impugned on any of the said grounds.
RatioOfTheDecision	13.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question of the amendability of the fundamental rights was considered by this <ORG> earlier in two decisions, namely, <CITATION_1706770> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89, 105) and in <CITATION_1308308> .
Precedent	14.
Precedent	In the former the validity of the Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951, which inserted, inter alia, Arts.
Precedent	31-A and 31-B in the Constitution, was  questioned.
Precedent	That amendment was made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 of the Constitution by the Provisional <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> held that <ORG> had power to amend Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> came to that conclusion on two grounds, namely, (1) the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) was one made in exercise of legislative power and not constitutional law made in exercise of constituent power; and (ii) there were two articles (Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) and 368) each of which was widely phrased and, therefore, harmonious construction required that one should be so read as to be controlled and qualified by the other, and having regard to the circumstances mentioned in the judgment Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13 must be read subject to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	A careful perusal of the judgment indicates that the whole decision turned upon an assumption that the expression "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) does not include constitutional law and on that assumption an attempt was made to harmonise Article 13(2) and 368 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	15.
RatioOfTheDecision	The decision in <NAME> case , was giv en in the context of the question of the validity of the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964.
Precedent	Two questions arose in that case : (1) Whether the amendment Act insofar it purported to take away or abridge the rights conferred by Part III of the Constitution fell within the prohibition of Art.
Precedent	13(2) and (2).
Precedent	Whether Articles 31-A and 31-B sought to make changes in Arts.
Precedent	132, 136 or 226 or in any of the lists in the Seventh Schedule and therefore the requirements of the proviso to Article 368 had to be satisfied.
Precedent	Both the Chief Justice and <NAME>, J., made it clear that the first contention was not raised before the <ORG>.
Precedent	The learned counsel appearing for both the parties accepted the correctness of the decision in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89, 105), in that regard.
Precedent	Yet <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	speaking for the majority agreed with the reasons given in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), on the first question and <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	expressed their dissent from the said view.
Precedent	But all  of them agreed, though for different reasons on the second question.
Precedent	<NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	speaking for himself, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	rejected the contention that Art.
Precedent	368 did not confer power on <ORG> to take away the fundamental rights guaranteed by Part III.
Precedent	When a suggestion was made that the decision in the aforesaid case should be reconsidered and reviewed, the learned Chief Justice though he conceded that in a case where a decision had a significant impact on the fundamental rights of citizens, the <ORG> would be inclined to review its earlier decision in the interests of the public good, he did not find considerations of substantial and compelling character to do so in that case.
Precedent	But after referring to the reasoning given in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), the learned Chief Justice observed :  "In our opinion, the expression "amendment of the Constitution" plainly and unambiguously means amendment of all the provisions of the Constitution".
Precedent	16.
Precedent	Referring to Art.
Precedent	13(2 ), he restated  the same reasoning found in the earlier decision and added that if it was the intention of the Constitution-makers to save fundamental rights from the amending process they should have taken the precaution of making a clear provision in that regard.
Precedent	In short, the majority, speaking through <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	agreed that no case had been made out for reviewing the earlier decision and practically accepted the reasons given in the earlier decision.
Precedent	<NAME>, speaking for himself, observed :  "But I make it clear that I must not be understood to have subscribed to the view that the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) does not control constitutional amendments.
Precedent	I reserve my opinion on that case for I apprehend that it depends on how wide is the word "law" in that Article".
Precedent	17.
Precedent	After giving his reasons for doubting the correctness of the reasoning given in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), the learned Judge concluded thus :  "I would require stronger reasons than those given in <NAME> case ([1952 ] S.C.R 89), to make me accept the view that Fundamental Rights were not really fundamental but were intended to be within the powers of amendment in common with the other parts of the Constitution and without the concurrence of the States".
Precedent	18.
Precedent	The learned Judge continued :  "The Constitution gives so many assurances in Part III that it would be difficult to think that they were the playthings of a special majority".
Precedent	19.
Precedent	<NAME>, J. was positive that the result of a legislative action of a legislature could not be other than "law" and, therefore, it seemed to him that the fact that the legislation dealt with the amendment of a provision of the Constitution would not make its results anytheless a "law".
Precedent	He further pointed out that Art.
Precedent	368 did not say that whenever <ORG> made an amendment to the Constitution it assumed a different capacity from that of a constituent body.
Precedent	He also brought out other defects in the line of reasoning adopted in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89).
RatioOfTheDecision	It will, therefore, be s een that the correctness of the decision in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), was not questioned in <NAME> case .
RatioOfTheDecision	Though it was not questioned, three of the learned Judges agreed with the view expressed therein, but two learned Judges were inclined to take a different view.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, as that question was not raised, the minority agreed with the conclusion arrived at by the majority on the question whether the Seventeenth Amendment Act was covered by the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The conflict between the majority and the minority in <NAME> case , falls to be resolved in this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The said conflict and the great importance of the question raised is the justification for the Constitution of the larger <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The decision in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), was assumed to be correct in subsequent decisions of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1879676> (sic).
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_184660633> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	284, 366) and <CITATION_761967> ( Supp.
Precedent	1 S.C.R.
Precedent	528, 563).
Precedent	But nothing turns upon that fact, as the correc tness of the decision was not questioned in those cases.
Precedent	20.
RatioOfTheDecision	A correct appreciation of the scope and the place of fundamental rights in our Constitution will give us the right perspective for solving the problem presented before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its scope cannot be appreciated unless we have a conspectus of the Constitution, its objects and its machinery to achieve those objects.
RatioOfTheDecision	The objective sought to be achieved by the Constitution is declared in sonorous terms in its preamble which reads :  "We the people of India having solemnly resolved to constitute India into a Sovereign, Democratic, Republic and secure to all its citizens justice.... liberty.... equality..... and fraternity...."  21.
RatioOfTheDecision	It contains in a nutshell, its ideals and its aspirations.
RatioOfTheDecision	The preamble is not a platitude but the mode of its realisation is worked out in detail in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution brings into existence different constitutional entities, namely, the <ORG>, the States and the <ORG> Territories.
RatioOfTheDecision	It creates three major instruments  of power, namely, the <ORG>, the <ORG> and the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	I demarcates their jurisdiction minutely and expects them to exercise their respective powers without overstepping their limits.
RatioOfTheDecision	They should function with the spheres allotted to them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some powers overlap and some are superseded during emergencies.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mode of resolution of conflicts and conditions for supersession are also prescribed.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, the scope of the power and the manner of its exercise are regulated by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	No authority created under the Constitution is supreme; the Constitution is supreme; and all the authorities function under the supreme law of the land.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule of law under the Constitution has a glorious content.
RatioOfTheDecision	It embodies the modern concept of law evolved over the centuries.
RatioOfTheDecision	It empowers the <ORG>s to make laws in respect of matters enumerated in the 3 Lists annexed to Schedule VII.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Part IV of the Constitution, the Directive Principles of State Policy are laid down.
RatioOfTheDecision	It enjoins it to bring about a social order in  which justice, social, economic and political - shall inform all the institutions of national life.
RatioOfTheDecision	It directs it to work for an egalitarian society where there is no concentration of wealth, where there is plenty, where there is equal opportunity for all, to education, to work, to livelihood, and where there is social justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	But having regard to the past history of our country, it could not implicitly believe the representatives of the people, for uncontrolled and unrestricted power might lead to an authoritarian <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It, therefore, preserves the natural rights against the <ORG> encroachment and constitutes the higher judiciary of the <ORG> as the sentinel of the said rights and the balancing wheel between the rights, subject to social control.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, the fundamental rights, subject to social control, have been incorporated in the rule of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is brought about by an interesting process.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the implementation of the Directive Principles, <ORG> or the Legislature of a <ORG> makes laws in respe ct of matter or matters allotted to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the higher <ORG> tests their validity on certain objective criteria, namely, (i) whether the appropriate <ORG> has the legislative competency to make the law; (ii) whether the said law infringes any of the fundamental rights; (iii) even if it infringes the freedoms under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 , whether the infringement only amounts to "reasonable restriction" on such rights in "public interest".
RatioOfTheDecision	By this process of scrutiny, the court maintains the validity of only such laws as keep a just balance between freedoms and social control.
RatioOfTheDecision	The duty of reconciling fundamental rights in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 and the laws of social control is cast upon the courts and the touchstone or the standard is contained in the said two expressions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The standard is an elastic one; it varies with time, space and condition.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is reasonable under certain circumstances may not be so under different circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constitutional philosophy of law is reflected in Parts III and IV of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Th e rule of law under the Constitution serves the needs of the people without unduly infringing their rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	It recognizes the social reality and tries to adjust itself to it from time to time avoiding the authoritarian path.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every institution or political party that functions under the Constitution must accept it; otherwise it has no place under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	22.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, what are the fundamental rights ?
RatioOfTheDecision	They are embodied in Part III of the Constitution and they may be classified thus : (i) right to equality, (ii) right to freedom, (iii) right against exploitation, (iv) right to freedom of religion, (v) cultural and educational rights, (vi) right to property, and (vii) right to constitutional remedies.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are the rights of the people preserved by our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Fundamental rights" are the modern name for what have been traditionally known as "natural rights".
RatioOfTheDecision	As one author puts : "they are moral rights which every human being everywhere at all times ought to have simply because of the fact that in con tradistinction with other beings, he is rational and moral".
RatioOfTheDecision	They are the primordial rights necessary for the development of human personality.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are the rights which enable a man to chalk out his own life in the manner he likes best.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution, in addition to the well-known fundamental rights, also included the rights of the minorities, untouchables and other backward communities, in such rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	23.
RatioOfTheDecision	After having declared the fundamental rights, our Constitution says that all laws in force in the territory of India immediately before the commencement of the Constitution, insofar as they are inconsistent with the said rights, are, to the extent of such inconsistency, void.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution also enjoins the <ORG> not to make any law which takes away or abridges the said rights and declares such laws, to the extent of such inconsistency, to be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	As we have stated earlier, the only limitation on the freedom enshrined in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 of the Constitution is that imposed by a valid law operating as a reas onable restriction in the interests of the public.
RatioOfTheDecision	24.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will, therefore, be seen that fundamental rights are given a transcendental position under our Constitution and are kept beyond the reach of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time Parts III and IV constituted an integrated scheme forming a self-contained code.
RatioOfTheDecision	The scheme is made so elastic that all the Directive Principles of State Policy can reasonably be enforced without taking away or abridging the fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	While recognizing the immutability of fundamental rights, subject to social control, the Constitution itself provides for the suspension or the modification of fundamental rights under specific circumstances, for instance, Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	33 empowers <ORG> to modify the rights conferred by Part III in their application to the members of the armed forces, Art.
Precedent	34 enables it to impose restrictions on the rights conferred by the said parts while martial law is in force in an area, Art.
Precedent	35 confers the power on it to make laws with respect to any of the m atters which under clause (3) of Art.
Precedent	16 , Clause (3) of Art.
Precedent	32 , Art.
Precedent	33 and Art.
Precedent	34 may be provided for by law.
Precedent	The non-obstante clause with which the last article opens makes it clear that all the other provisions of the Constitution are subject to this provision.
Precedent	Article 32 makes the right to move <ORG> by appropriate proceedings for the enforcement of the rights conferred by the said Parts a guaranteed right.
Precedent	Even during grave emergencies Art.
Precedent	358 only suspends the provision of Art.
Precedent	19; and Art.
Precedent	359 enables the President by order to declare the right to move any court for the enforcement of such of the rights conferred by Part III as may be mentioned in that order to be suspended; that is to say, even during emergency, only Art.
Precedent	19 is suspended temporarily and all other rights are untouched except those specifically suspended by the President.
Precedent	25.
Precedent	In the Book "Indian Constitution - Cornerstone of a Nation" by <NAME>, the scope, origin and the object of fundamental rights have been  graphically stated.
Precedent	Therein the learned author says :  "..... the core of the commitment to the social revolution lies in Parts III and IV, in the Fundamental Rights and in the Directive Principles of State Policy.
Precedent	These are the conscience of the Constitution".
Precedent	26.
Precedent	Adverting to the necessity for incorporating fundamental rights in a Constitution, the learned author says :  "That a declaration of rights had assumed such importance was not surprising; India was a land of communities, of minorities, racial, religious, linguistic, social and caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	For India to become a state, these minorities had to agree to be governed both at the center and in the provinces by fellow Indian-members, perhaps, of another minority - and not by a mediatory third power, the British.
RatioOfTheDecision	On both psychological and political grounds, therefore, the demand for written rights - since rights would provide tangible safeguards, against oppression - proved overwhelming".
Precedent	27.
Precedent	<NAME>, who presided over the <ORG> called for by the Ma dras <ORG> resolution, in May, 1928 observed in his report :  "It is obvious that our first care should be to have our Fundamental Rights guaranteed in a manner which will not permit their withdrawal under any circumstances..... Another reason why great importance attached to a Declaration of Rights is the unfortunate existence of communal differences in the country.
Precedent	Certain safeguards are necessary to create and establish a sense of security among those who look upon each other with distrust and suspicion.
Precedent	We could not, better secure the full enjoyment of religious and communal rights to all communities than by including them among the basic principles of the Constitution".
Precedent	28.
Precedent	Pandit <NAME>, on April 30, 1947 in proposing for the adoption of the Interim Report on Fundamental Rights, said thus :  "A fundamental right should be looked upon, not from the point of view of any particular difficulty of the moment, but as something that you want to make permanent in the Constitution.
Precedent	The other matter  should be looked upon - however important it might be - not from this permanent and fundamental point of view, but from the more temporary point of view".
Precedent	29.
Precedent	<NAME>, who was Prime Minister at that time and who must have had an effective voice in the framing of the Constitution, made this distinction between fundamental rights and other provisions of the Constitution, namely, the former were permanent and the latter were amendable.
Precedent	On September 18, 1949 Dr. <NAME> in speaking on the amendment proposed by Mr. <NAME> to Art.
Precedent	304 of the Draft Constitution corresponding to the present Art.
Precedent	368 , namely, "Any provision of this Constitution may be amended, whether by way of variation, addition or repeal, in the manner provided in this article", said thus :  "Now, what is it we do ?
Precedent	We divide the articles of the Constitution under three categories.
Precedent	The first category is the one which consists of articles which can be amended by <ORG> by a bare majority.
Precedent	The second set of articles are articl es which require two-thirds majority.
Precedent	If the future <ORG> wishes to amend any particular article which is not mentioned in Part III or article 304 , all that is necessary for them is to have two-thirds majority.
Precedent	Then they can amend it".
Precedent	30.
Precedent	Therefore, in Dr. <NAME>'s view the fundamental rights were so important that they could not be amended in the manner provided by Art.
Precedent	304 of the Draft Constitution, which corresponds to the present Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	31.
Precedent	We have referred to the speeches of Pandit <NAME> and Dr. <NAME> not with a view to interpret the provisions of Art.
Precedent	368 , which we propose to do on its own terms, but only to notice the transcendental character given to the fundamental rights by two of the important architects of the Constitution.
Precedent	32.
Precedent	This <ORG> also noticed the paramountcy of the fundamental rights in many decisions.
Precedent	<CITATION_1857950> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	88, 198), they are described as "paramount", in <CITATION_149321> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	525), as "sacro-sanci", in <CITATION_944601> ( Supp.
Precedent	1  S.C.R.
Precedent	806), as "rights reserved by the people", in <CITATION_673012> as "inalienable and inviolable" and in other cases as "transcendental".
RatioOfTheDecision	The minorities regarded them as the bedrock of their political existence and the majority considered them as a guarantee for their way of life.
RatioOfTheDecision	This, however, does not mean that the problem is one of mere dialectics.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution has given by its scheme a place of permanence to the fundamental freedoms.
RatioOfTheDecision	In giving to themselves the Constitution, the people have reserved the fundamental freedoms to themselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 13 merely incorporates that reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	That Article is however not the source of the protection of fundamental rights but the expression of the reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The importance attached to the fundamental freedoms is so transcendental that a bill enacted by a unanimous vote of all the members of both the <ORG> is ineffective to derogate from its guaranteed exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not what the <ORG> regards at a given moment as conducive to the public benefit,  but what Part III declares protected, which determines the ambit of the freedom.
RatioOfTheDecision	The incapacity of the <ORG> therefore in exercise of its amending power to modify, restrict or impair fundamental freedoms in Part III arises from the scheme of the Constitution and the nature of the freedoms.
RatioOfTheDecision	33.
RatioOfTheDecision	Briefly stated, the Constitution declares certain rights as fundamental rights, makes all the laws infringing the said rights void, preserves only the laws of social control infringing the said rights and expressly confers power on <ORG> and the President to amend or suspend them in specified circumstances; if the decisions in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89, 105) and <NAME> case ([1965] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	933), laid down the correct law, it enables the same <ORG> to abrogate them with one stroke, provided the party in power singly or in combination with other parties commands the necessary majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	While articles of less significance would require consent of the majority of the States, fundamental  rights can be dropped without such consent.
RatioOfTheDecision	While a single fundamental right cannot be abridged or taken away by the entire <ORG> unanimously voting to that effect, a two-thirds' majority can do away with all the fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The entire super structure built with precision and high ideals may crumble at one false step.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a conclusion would attribute unreasonableness to the makers of the Constitution, for, in that event would be speaking in two voices.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an intention cannot be attributed to the makers of the Constitution unless the provisions of the Constitution compel us to do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	34.
RatioOfTheDecision	With this background let us proceed to consider the provisions of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , vis-a-vis Art.
Precedent	13(2) of the Constitution.
Precedent	35.
Precedent	The first question is whether amendment of the Constitution under Art.
Precedent	368 is "law" within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	The marginal note to Art.
Precedent	368 describes that article as one prescribing the procedure for amendment.
Precedent	The article in terms only prescribes various procedural steps in the  matter of amendment : it shall be initiated by the introduction of a bill in either <ORG>; it shall be passed by the prescribed majority in both the <ORG>; it shall then be presented to the President for his assent; and upon such assent the Constitution shall stand amended.
Precedent	The article assumes the power to amend found elsewhere and says that it shall be exercised in the manner laid down therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument that the completion of the procedural steps culminates in the exercise of the power to amend may be subtle but does not carry conviction.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that was the intention of the provisions, nothing prevented the makers of the Constitution from stating that the Constitution may be amended in the manner suggested.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, whenever the Constitution sought to confer a special power to amend on any authority it expressly said so : (See Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	4 and 392).
RatioOfTheDecision	The alternative contention that the said power shall be implied either from Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 or from the nature of the articles sought to be amended cannot be  accepted, for the simple reason that the doctrine of necessary implication cannot be invoked if there is an express provision or unless but for such implication the article will become otiose or nugatory.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no necessity to imply any such power, as <ORG> has the plenary power to make any law, including the law to amend the Constitution subject to the limitations laid down therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	36.
RatioOfTheDecision	Uninfluenced by any foreign doctrines let us look at the provisions of our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 , "subject to the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> may make laws for the whole or any part of the territory of India...." Article 246 demarcates the matters in respect of which <ORG> and State <ORG> may make laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the field reserved for <ORG> there is Entry 97 which empowers it to make laws in respect of "any other matter not enumerated in Lists II and III including any tax not mentioned in either of those lists".
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 248 expressly states that <ORG> has exclusive power to make  any law with respect to any matter not enumerated in the Concurrent List or State List.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, therefore, clear that the residuary power of legislation is vested in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Subject to the argument based upon the alleged nature of the amending power as understood by jurists in other countries, which we shall consider at a later stage, it cannot be contended, and indeed, it was not contended, that <ORG>, if it were so minded, could not have conferred an express legislative power on <ORG> to amend the Constitution by ordinary legislative process.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 4 and 169, and para 7 of the 5th Schedule and para 21 of the 6th Schedule have expressly conferred such power.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is, therefore, no inherent inconsistency between legislative process and the amending one.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether in the field of a constitutional law or statutory law amendment can be brought about only by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The residuary power of <ORG>, unless there is anything contrary in the Constitution, certainly takes in the power to  amend the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that two Articles indicate the contrary intention.
RatioOfTheDecision	As Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 , the argument proceeds, is subject to the provisions of the Constitution, every law of amendment will necessarily be inconsistent with the articles sought to be amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is an argument in a circle.
RatioOfTheDecision	Can it be said reasonably that a law amending an article is inconsistent with the article amended ?
RatioOfTheDecision	If an article of the Constitution expressly says that it cannot be amended, a law cannot be made amending it, as the power of <ORG> to make a law is subject to the said Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may well be that in a given case such a limitation may also necessarily be implied.
Precedent	The limitation in Art.
Precedent	245 is in respect of the power to make a law and not of the content of the law made within the scope of its power.
Precedent	The second criticism is based upon Art.
Precedent	392 of the Constitution.
Precedent	That provision confers power on the President to remove difficulties; in the circumstances mentioned in that provision, he can by order direct that t he Constitution shall during such period as may be specified in that order have effect subject to such adaptations, whether by way of modification, addition or omission, as he may deem to be necessary or expedient.
Precedent	The argument is that the President's power, though confined to a temporary period, is co-extensive with legislative power and if the power to amend is a legislative power it would have to be held that the President can amend the Constitution in terms of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	Apart from the limited scope of Art.
Precedent	392 , which is intended only for the purpose of removing difficulties and for bringing about a smooth transition, an order made by the President cannot attract Act.
Precedent	368, as the amendment contemplated by that provision can be initiated only by the introduction of a bill in the <ORG>.
Precedent	There is no force in either of the two criticisms.
Precedent	37.
Precedent	Further, there is internal evidence in the Constitution itself which indicates that amendment to the Constitution is a "law" within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	245.
Precedent	Now, w hat is "law" under the Constitution ?
Precedent	It is not denied that in its comprehensive sense it includes constitutional law and the law amending the Constitution is constitutional law.
Precedent	But Art.
Precedent	13(2) for the purpose of that Article gives an inclusive definition.
Precedent	It does not exclude Constitutional law.
Precedent	It prima facie takes in constitutional law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 368 itself gives the necessary clue to the problem.
RatioOfTheDecision	The amendment can be initiated by the introduction of a bill; it shall be passed by the two <ORG>; it shall receive the assent of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	These are well-known procedural steps in the process of law-making : Indeed this <ORG> in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), brought out this idea in clear terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	It said in the first place, it is provided that the amendment must be initiated by the introduction of a "bill in either <ORG> of <ORG>" a familiar feature of <ORG> procedure (of Article 107(1) which says "A bill may originate in either <ORG> of <ORG>").
RatioOfTheDecision	Then, the bill must be "passed in each Hous e," - just what <ORG> does when it is called upon to exercise its normal legislative function [ Article 107(2)]; and finally, the bill thus passed must be "presented to the President" for his "assent", again a parliamentary process through which every bill must pass before it can reach the statute-book, ( Article 111 ).
RatioOfTheDecision	We thus find that each of the component units of <ORG> is to play its allotted part in bringing about an amendment to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have already seen that <ORG> effects amendments of the first class mentioned above by going through the same three-fold procedure but with a simple majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact that a different majority in the same body is required for effecting the second and third categories of amendments make the amending agency a different body".
RatioOfTheDecision	38.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the same decision it is pointed out that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is not a complete code in respect of the procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Court said "There are gaps in the procedure as to how and after what notice a bill is to be introduced, how  it is to be passed by each <ORG> and how the President's assent is to be obtained.
RatioOfTheDecision	Having provided for the Constitution of a <ORG> and prescribed a certain procedure for the conduct of its ordinary legislative business to be supplemented by rules made by each <ORG> ( Article 118 ), the makers of the Constitution must be taken to have intended <ORG> to follow that procedure, so far as they may be applicable consistently with the express provision of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , when they have entrusted to it the power of amending the Constitution".
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> of the People made rules providing procedure for amendments, the same as for other Bills with the addition of certain special provisions viz., Rules 155, 156, 157 and 158.
RatioOfTheDecision	If amendment is intended to be something other than law, the constitutional insistence on the said legislative process is unnecessary.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, amendment cannot be made otherwise than by following the legislative process.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact that there are other conditions, such as, a larger majority and  in the case of articles mentioned in the proviso a ratification by <ORG> is provided, does not make the amendment anytheless a law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The imposition of further conditions is only a safeguard against hasty action or a protection to the States, but does not change the Legislative character of the amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	39.
RatioOfTheDecision	This conclusion is reinforced by the other articles of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 3 enables <ORG> by law to form new States and alter areas, boundaries or the names of existing States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proviso to that Article imposed two further conditions, namely, (i) the recommendation of the President, and (ii) in the circumstances mentioned therein, the views expressed by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Notwithstanding the said conditions it cannot be suggested that the expression "law" under the said Article is not one made by the Legislative process.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	4 , such a law can contain provisions for amendment of Schedules I and IV indicating thereby that amendments are only made by Legislative process.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is  more, clause (2) thereof introduces a fiction to the affect that such a law shall not be deemed to be an amendment to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This shows that the amendment is law and that but for the fiction it would be an amendment within the meaning of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 169 which empowers <ORG> by law to abolish or create Legislative Councils in States, para 7 of the 5th Schedule and para 21 of the 6th Schedule which enable <ORG> by law to amend the said Schedules, also bring out the two ideas that the amendment is law made by legislative process and that but for the fiction introduced it would attract Article 368.
RatioOfTheDecision	That apart amendments under the said provisions can be made by the Union <ORG> by simple majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	That an amendment is made only by legislative process with or without conditions will be clear if two decisions of <ORG> are considered in juxta-position.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are <NAME> v. The King ( A.C. 691) and <ORG> v. <NAME> ( 2 W.L.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	1301).
Precedent	40.
Precedent	The fac ts in <NAME> v. The King ([1920] A.C. 691) were these : In 1859 Queensland had been granted a Constitution in the terms of an Order in <ORG> made on June 6 of that year under powers derived by <NAME> from the Imperial Statute, 18 & 19 Vict.
Precedent	c. 54.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Order in <ORG> had set up a legislature for the territory, consisting of the Queen, <ORG> and <ORG>, and the law-making power was vested in <NAME> acting with the advice and consent of the <ORG> and <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any laws could be made for the "peace, welfare and good government of the Colony".
RatioOfTheDecision	The said legislature of Queensland in the year 1867 passed the Constitution Act of that year.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under that Act power was given to the said legislature to make laws for "peace, welfare and good Government of the Colony in all cases whatsoever".
RatioOfTheDecision	But, under s. 9 thereof a two-thirds majority of the <ORG> and of the <ORG> was required as a condition precedent to the validity of legislation altering the constitution of <ORG> cil.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG>, therefore, had, except in the case covered by s. 9 of the Act, an unrestricted power to make laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> passed a law which conflicted with one of the existing terms of the Constitution Act .
Precedent	Lord Birkenhead, L.C., upheld the law, as the Constitution Act conferred an absolute power upon the legislature to pass any law by majority even though it, in substance, amended the terms of the Constitution Act .
Precedent	41.
Precedent	In <ORG> ([1964] 2 W.L.R.
Statute	1301), the facts are these : By section 29 of the Ceylon (Constitution) Order in Council, 1946, <ORG> shall have power to make laws for the "peace, order and good government" of the Island and in the exercise of its power under the said section it may amend or repeal any of the provisions of the Order in its application to the Island.
Statute	The proviso to that section says that no Bill for the amendment or repeal of any of the provisions of the Order shall be presented for the Royal assent unless it has endors ed on it a certificate under the hand of the Speaker that the number of votes cast in favour thereof in <ORG> amounted to not less than two-thirds of the whole number of members of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under s. 55 of the said Order the appointment of Judicial Officers was vested in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the <ORG> under s. 41 of the Bribery Amendment Act, 1958, provided for the appointment of the personnel of <ORG> by the Governor-General on the advice of the Minister of Justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The said Amendment Act was in conflict with the said s. 55 of the Order and it was passed without complying with the terms of the proviso to s. 29 of the Order.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> held that the amendment Act was void.
Precedent	Lord <NAME>, after considering <NAME>'s case ( A.C. 691), made the following observations, at p. 1310 :  "........ a legislature has no power to ignore the conditions of law-making that are imposed by the instrument which itself regulates its power to make law.
RatioOfTheDecision	This restr iction exists independently of the question whether the legislature is sovereign, as is the legislature of Ceylon, or whether the Constitution is "uncontrolled", as the Board held the Constitution of Queensland to be.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a Constitution can, indeed, be altered or amended by the legislature, if the regulating instrument so provides and if the terms of those provisions are complied with .........."  42.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be seen from the said judgments that an amendment of the Constitution is made only by legislative process with ordinary majority or with special majority, as the case may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, amendments either under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 or under other Articles are made only by <ORG> by following the legislative process adopted by it in making other law.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the premises, an amendment of the Constitution can be nothing but "law".
RatioOfTheDecision	43.
RatioOfTheDecision	A comparative study of other Constitutions indicates that no particular pattern is followed.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the Constitutions confer an express power to amend, most of them provide for legislative  procedure with special majority, referendum, convention, etc., and a few with simple majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, <ORG>, which is a supreme body, can amend the constitution like any other statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	As none of the Constitutions contains provisions similar to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2 ), neither the said Constitutions nor the decisions given by courts thereon would be of any assistance in construing the scope of Art.
Precedent	368 of our Constitution.
Precedent	44.
Precedent	A brief survey of the nature of the amending process adopted by various constitutions will bring out the futility of any attempt to draw inspiration from the said opinions or decisions on the said constitutions.
Precedent	The nature of the amending power in different constitutions generally depends on the nature of the polity created by the constitution, namely, whether it is federal or unitary constitution or on the fact whether it is a written or an unwritten constitution or on the circumstances whether it is a rigid or a flexible constitution.
Precedent	Particularly the difference can  be traced to the "spirit and genius of the nation in which a particular constitution has its birth".
Precedent	The following articles of the Constitution of the different countries are brought to our notice by one or other of the counsel that appeared before us.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	5 of the Constitution of the united States of America, Arts.
Precedent	125 and 128 of the Commonwealth of Australia Constitution Act, Art.
Fact	92(1) of the British North American Act, s. 152 of the South African Act, Art.
Fact	217 of the Constitution of the United States of Brazil, Section 46 of the Constitution of Ireland, 1937, Arts.
Fact	207, 208 and 209 of the Constitution of the Union of Burma, Art.
Fact	88 of the Constitution of the Kingdom of Denmark Act, Art.
Fact	90 of the Constitution of the French Republic, 1954, Art.
Fact	135 of the united States of Mexico, Art.
Precedent	96 of the Constitution of Japan, Art.
Precedent	112 of the Constitution of Norway, Art.
Precedent	85 of the Constitution of the Kingdom of Sweden, Arts.
Precedent	118, 119, 120, 121, 122 and 123 of the Constitution of the Swiss Federation, Arts.
Precedent	140, 14 1 and 142 of the Constitution of Venezuela, and Art.
Precedent	146 of the Constitution of the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics, 1936 and s. 29(4) of Ceylon Constitution Order in Council, 1946.
Precedent	45.
Fact	Broadly speaking amendments can be made by four methods : (i) by ordinary legislative process with or without restriction, (ii) by the people through referendum, (iii) by majority of all the units of a federal State; and (iv) by a special convention.
Fact	The first method can be in four different ways, namely, (i) by the ordinary course of legislation by absolute majority or by special majority, (See Section 92(1) of the British North America Act, sub-section 152 South African Act, whereunder except sections 35 , 137 and 152 , other provisions could be amended by ordinary legislative process by absolute majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many constitutions provide for special majorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	); (ii) by a fixed quorum of members for the consideration of the proposed amendment and a special majority for its passage; (see the defunct Constitution of Rumania),  (iii) by dissolution and general election on a particular issue; (see the Constitutions of Belgium, Holland, Denmark and Norway), and (iv) by a majority of two <ORG> in joint session as in the Constitution of the South Africa.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second method demands a popular vote, referendum or plebiscite as in Switzerland, Australia, Ireland, Italy, France and Denmark.
RatioOfTheDecision	The third method is by an agreement in some form or other of either of the majority or of all the federating units as in Switzerland, Australia and the United States of America.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fourth method is generally by creation of a special body ad hoc for the purpose of constitution revision as in Latin America.
Precedent	Lastly, some constitutions impose express limitation on the power to amend.
Precedent	(See Art.
Precedent	5 of the United States Constitution and the Constitution of the Fourth French Republic).
Precedent	A more elaborate discussion of this topic may be found in the American political Constitution by strong.
Precedent	It will, therefore, be seen that the power to amend and the  procedure to amend radically differ from <ORG> to <ORG>; it is left to the constitution-makers to prescribe the scope of the power and the method of amendment having regard to the requirements of the particular <ORG>.
Precedent	There is no article in any of the constitutions referred to us similar to article 13(2) of our Constitution.
Precedent	India adopted a different system altogether : it empowered the <ORG> to amend the Constitution by the legislative process subject to fundamental rights.
Precedent	The Indian Constitution has made the amending process comparatively flexible, but it is made subject to fundamental rights.
Precedent	46.
Precedent	Now let us consider the argument that the power to amend is a sovereign power, that the said power is supreme to the legislative power, that it does not permit any implied limitations and that amendments made in exercise of that power involve political questions and that, therefore, they are outside judicial review.
Precedent	The wide proposition is sought to be supported on the basis of opinions of jurists and judic ial decisions.
Precedent	Long extracts have been read to us from the book "The Amending of the Federal Constitution (1942)" by <NAME>, and particular reference was made to the following passages :  "At the point it may be well to note that when the <ORG> is engaged in the amending process it is not legislating.
Precedent	It is exercising a peculiar power bestowed upon it by Article Five.
Precedent	This Article for the most part controls the process; and other provisions of the Constitution, such as those relating to the passage of legislation, having but little bearing".
Precedent	47.
RatioOfTheDecision	Adverting to the Bill of Rights, the learned author remarks that they may be repealed just as any other amendment and that they are no more sacred from a legal standpoint than any other part of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Dealing with the doctrine of implied limitations, he says that it is clearly untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Posing the question "Is there a law about the amending power of the Constitution ?
RatioOfTheDecision	", he answers, "there is none".
RatioOfTheDecision	He would even go to the extent of sa ying that the sovereignty, if it can be said to exist at all, is located in the amending body.
RatioOfTheDecision	The author is certainly a strong advocate of the supremacy of the amending power and an oponent of the doctrine of implied limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	His opinion is based upon the terms of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	5 of the Constitution of the United States of America and his interpretation of the decisions of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even such an extreme exponent of the doctrine does not say that a particular constitution cannot expressly impore restrictions on the power to amend or that a court cannot reconcile the articles couched in unlimited phraseology.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	5 of the American Constitution imposes express limitations on the amending power.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some passages from the book "Political Science and Government" by <NAME> are cited.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> points out :  "An unamendable constitution, said <NAME>, is the "worst tyranny of time, or rather the very tyranny of time"."
RatioOfTheDecision	But he also notices :  "The provision for amendment should be neit her so rigid as to make needed changes practically impossible nor so flexible as to encourage frequent and unnecessary changes and thereby lower the authority of the Constitution".
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "The Government of the United States", 5th Edition, uses strong words when he says :  "........ it is impossible to conceive of an unamendable constitution as anything but a contradiction in terms".
RatioOfTheDecision	48.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned author says that such a constitution would constitute "government by the graveyards".
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "Constitutional Law of the United States" avers that the doctrine of amendability of the Constitution is grounded in the doctrine of the sovereignty of the people and that it has no such implied limitations as that an amendment shall not contain a new grant of power or change the dual form of government or change the protection of the Bill of Rights, or make any other change in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "The Theory and Practice of Modern Government" defines "constituti on" as its process of amendment, for, in his view, to amend is to deconstitute and reconstitute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned author concludes that the amending clause is so fundamental to a constitution that he is tempted to call it the constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the learned author recognizes that difficulty in amendment certainly products circumstances and makes impossible the surreptitious abrogation of rights guaranteed in the constitution.
Precedent	<NAME> in "Federalism and Constitutional Change" says :  "The formal procedure of amendment is of greater importance than the informal processes, because it constitutes a higher authority to which appeal lies on any question that may arise".
Precedent	49.
Precedent	But there are equally eminent authors who express a different view.
Precedent	In "American Jurisprudence", 2nd Edition, Vol.
RatioOfTheDecision	16, it is stated that a statute and a constitution though of unequal dignity are both laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another calls the constitution of a <ORG> as one of the laws of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book on "Constitutional Law" opines  that changes in the fundamental laws of the <ORG> must be indicated by the people themselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	He further implies limitations to the amending power from the belief in the constitution itself, such as, the republican form of Government cannot be abolished as it would be revolutionary in its character.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the same book it is further said that the power to amend the constitution by legislative action does not confer the power to break it any more than it confers the power to legislate on any other subject contrary to the prohibitions.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "Modern Political Constitutions", 1963 edition, does not accept the theory of absolute sovereignty of the amending power which does not brook any limitations, for he says :  "In short, it attempts to arrange for the re-creation of a constituent assembly whenever such matters are in future to be considered, even though that assembly be nothing more than the ordinary legislature acting under certain restrictions.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, there may be some elements of  the constitution which the constituent assembly wants to remain unalterable by the action of any authority whatsoever.
RatioOfTheDecision	These elements are to be distinguished from the rest, and generally come under the heading of fundamental law.
Precedent	Thus, for example, the American Constitution, the oldest of the existing Constitutions, asserts that by no process of amendment shall any <ORG>, without its own consent, be deprived of its equal suffrage in the <ORG>, while among the Constitutions more recently promulgated, those of the Republics of France and Italy, each containing a clause stating that the republican form of government cannot be the subject of an amending proposal".
Precedent	50.
Precedent	It is not necessary to multiply citations from text-books.
Precedent	51.
Precedent	A catena of American decisions have been cited before us in support of the contention that the amending power is a supreme power or that it involves political issues which are not justiciable.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would be futile to consider them at length, for after going through them carefully we fi nd that there are no considered judgments of the American <ORG>, which would have a persuasive effect in that regard.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the Constitution of the United States of America, prepared by <NAME>, <ORG>, <ORG>, (1953 edn.
RatioOfTheDecision	), the following summary under the heading "Judicial Review under Article V" is given :  "Prior to 1939, <ORG> had taken cognizance of a number of diverse objections to the validity of specific amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart from holding that official notice of ratification by the several States was conclusive upon the <ORG>, it had treated these questions as justiciable, although it had uniformly rejected them on the merits.
Precedent	In that year, however, the whole subject was thrown into confusion by the inconclusive decision in <NAME> v. <NAME>.
Precedent	This case came up on a writ of certiorari to <ORG> of Kansas to review the denial of a writ of mandamus to compel the Secretary of the Kansas <ORG> to erase an endorsement on a resolution ratifying  the proposed child labour amendment to the Constitution to the effect that it had been adopted by <ORG>.
Fact	The attempted ratification was assailed on three grounds : (1) that the amendment had been previously rejected by <ORG>; (2) that it was no longer open to ratification because an unreasonable period of time, thirteen years, had elapsed since its submission to the States, and (3) that the lieutenant governor had no right to cast the deciding vote in the <ORG> in favour of ratification.
Fact	Four opinions were written in <ORG>, no one of which commanded the support of more than four members of the <ORG>.
Fact	The majority ruled that the plaintiffs, members of the Kansas State <ORG>, had a sufficient interest in the controversy to give the federal courts jurisdiction to review the case.
Fact	Without agreement as to the grounds for their decision, a different majority affirmed the judgment of the Kansas court denying the relief sought.
Precedent	Four members who concurred in the result had vot ed to dismiss the writ on the ground that the amending process "is political" in its entirety, from submission until an amendment becomes part of the Constitution, and is not subject to judicial guidance, control or interference at any point".
Precedent	Whether the contention that the lieutenant governor should have been permitted to cast the deciding vote in favour of ratification presented a justiciable controversy was left undecided, the court being equally divided on the point.
Precedent	In an opinion reported as "the opinion of the <ORG>" but in which it appears that only three Justices concurred, Chief Justice <NAME> declared that the writ of mandamus was properly denied because the question as to the effect of the previous rejection of the amendment and the lapse of time since it was submitted to the States were political questions which should be left to <ORG>.
Precedent	On the same day, the <ORG> dismissed a writ of certiorari to review a decision of the Kentucky <ORG> of Appeals declaring the action of the Kentucky General As sembly purporting to ratifying the child labour amendment illegal and void.
Precedent	Inasmuch as the governor had forwarded the certified copy of the resolution to the Secretary of <ORG> before being served with a copy of the restraining order issued by the <ORG> Court, <ORG> found that there was no longer a controversy susceptible of judicial determination".
Precedent	52.
Precedent	This passage, in our view, correctly summarises the American law on the subject.
Precedent	It will be clear therefrom that prior to 1939 <ORG> of America had treated the objections to the validity of specific amendments as justiciable and that only in 1939 it rejected them in an inconclusive judgment without discussion.
Precedent	In this state of affairs we cannot usefully draw much from the judicial wisdom of the Judges of <ORG> of America.
Precedent	53.
RatioOfTheDecision	One need not cavil at the description of an amending power as sovereign power, for it is sovereign only within the scope of the power conferred by a particular constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the authors also agre e, that a particular constitution can expressly limit the power of amendment, both substantive and procedural.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only conflict lies in the fact that some authors do not permit implied limitations when the power of amendment is expressed in general words.
RatioOfTheDecision	But others countenance such limitations by construction or otherwise.
RatioOfTheDecision	But none of the authors goes to the extent of saying, which is the problem before us, that when there are conflicting articles couched in widest terms, the court has no jurisdiction to construe and harmonize them.
RatioOfTheDecision	If some of the authors meant to say that - in our view, they did not - we cannot agree with them, for, in that event this <ORG> would not be discharging its duty.
Precedent	54.
Precedent	Nor can we appreciate the arguments repeated before us by learned counsel for the respondents that the amending process involves political questions which are, outside the scope of judicial review.
Precedent	When a matter comes before the <ORG>, its jurisdiction does not depend upon the nature of the question raised but on  the question whether the said matter is expressly or by necessary implication excluded from its jurisdiction.
Precedent	Secondly, it is not possible to define what is a political question and what is not.
Precedent	The character of a question depends upon the circumstances and the nature of a political society.
Precedent	To put it differently, the court does not decide any political question at all in the ordinary sense of the term, but only ascertains whether <ORG> is acting within the scope of the amending power.
Precedent	It may be that <ORG> seeks to amend the Constitution for political reasons, but the court in denying that power will not be deciding on political questions, but will only be holding that <ORG> has no power to amend particular articles of the Constitution for any purpose whatsoever, be it political or otherwise.
Precedent	We, therefore, hold that there is nothing in the nature of the amending power which enables <ORG> to override all the express or implied limitations imposed on that power.
Precedent	As we have pointed out <NAME> er, our Constitution adopted a novel method in the sense that <ORG> makes the amendment by legislative process subject to certain restrictions and that the amendment so made being "law" is subject to Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	55.
Precedent	The next argument is based upon the expression "amendment" in Art.
Precedent	368 of the Constitution and it is contended that the said expression has a positive and a negative content and that in exercise of the power of amendment <ORG> cannot destroy the structure of the Constitution, but it can only modify the provisions thereof within the framework of the original instrument for its better effectuation.
Precedent	If the fundamentals would be amenable to the ordinary process of amendment with a special majority, the argument proceeds, the institutions of the President can be abolished, the parliamentary executive can be removed, the fundamental rights can be abrogated, the concept of federalism can be obliterated and in short the sovereign democratic republic can be converted into a totalitarian system  of government.
Precedent	There is considerable force in this argument.
Precedent	Learned and lengthy arguments are advanced to sustain it or to reject it.
Precedent	But we are relieved of the necessity to express out opinion on this all important question as, so far as the fundamental rights are concerned, the question raised can be answered on a narrower basis.
Precedent	This question may arise for consideration only if <ORG> seeks to destroy the structure of the Constitution embodied in the provisions other than in Part III of the Constitution.
Precedent	We do not, therefore, propose to express our opinion in that regard.
Precedent	56.
Precedent	In the view we have taken on the scope of Art.
Precedent	368 vis-a-vis the fundamental rights, it is also unnecessary to express our opinion on the question whether the amendment of the fundamental rights is covered by the proviso to Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	57.
Precedent	The result is that the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, inasmuch as it takes away or abridges the fundamental rights is void under Art.
Precedent	13(2) of the Constitution.
Precedent	58.
Precedent	The next  question is whether our decision should be given retrospective operation.
Precedent	During the period between 1950 and 1967 i.e., 17 years, as many as 20 amendments were made in our Constitution.
Precedent	But in the context of the present petitions it would be enough if we notice the amendments affecting fundamental right to property.
Precedent	The Constitution came into force on January 26, 1950.
Precedent	The Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951, amended Arts.
Precedent	15 and 19, and Arts.
Precedent	31-A and 31-B were inserted with retrospective effect.
Precedent	The object of the amendment was said to be to validate the acquisition of zamindaries or the abolition of permanent settlement without interference from courts.
Precedent	The occasion for the amendment was that <ORG> in <CITATION_219225> (A.I.R.
Precedent	1951 Patna 91), held that the Bihar Land Reforms Act  passed by the State of Bihar was unconstitutional, while <ORG> of Allahabad and Nagpur upheld the validity of corresponding legislations in Uttar Pradesh and Madhya Pradesh respectively.
Precedent	The amendme nt was made when the appeals from those decisions were pending in <ORG>.
Precedent	In <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89, 105), the constitutionality of the said amendment was questioned but the amendment was upheld.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be noticed that the said amendment was not made on the basis of the power to amend fundamental rights recognized by this Court, but only in view of the conflicting decisions of <ORG> and without waiting for the final decision from this Court.
Statute	Article 31-A was again amended by the Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955.
Statute	Under that amendment clause (2) of Art.
Statute	31 was amended and clause (2-A) was inserted therein.
Statute	While in the original article 31-A the general expression "any provisions of his Part" was found, in the amended article the scope was restricted only to the violation of Arts.
Statute	14, 19 and 31 and 4 other clauses were included, namely, clauses providing for (a) taking over the management of any property by the <ORG> for a limited period; (b) amalgamation of two or more corpo rations; (c) extinguishment or modification of rights of persons interested in corporations; and (d) extinguishment or modification of rights accruing under any agreement, lease or licence relating to minerals, and the definition of "estate" was enlarged in order to include the interests of raiyats and under-raiyats.
Statute	The expressed object of the amendment was to carry out important social welfare legislations on the desired lines, to improve the national economy of the <ORG> and to avoid serious difficulties raised by courts in that regard.
Statute	Article 31-A had further been amended by the Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955.
Statute	By the said amendment in the Ninth Schedule to the Constitution entries 14 to 20 were added.
Fact	The main objects of this amending Act was to distinguish the power of compulsory acquisition or requisitioning of private property and the deprivation of property and to extend the scope of Art.
Fact	31-A to cover different categories of social welfare legislations and to enable monopolies in particul ar trade or business to be created in favour of the <ORG>.
Fact	Amended Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	31(2) makes the adequacy of compensation not justiciable.
Fact	It may be said that the Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955 was made by <ORG> as this <ORG> recognized the power of <ORG> to amend Part III of the Constitution; but it can also be said with some plausibility that, as <ORG> had exercised the power even before the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Fact	89, 105), it would have amended the Constitution even if the said decision was not given by this <ORG>.
Fact	The Seventeenth Amendment Act was made on June 20, 1964.
Fact	The occasion for this amendment was the decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_9285> ( Supp.
Fact	1 S.C.R.
Fact	829), which struck down the Kerala Agrarian Relations Act IV of 1961 relating to ryotwari lands.
Fact	Under that amendment the definition of the expression "estate" was enlarged so as to take in any land under ryotwari settlement and any held or let for purposes of agriculture or for purposes anci llary thereto, including waste land, forest land, land for pasture or sites of buildings and other structures occupied by cultivators of land, agricultural labourers and village artisans.
Fact	In the Ninth Schedule the amendment included items 21 to 65.
Fact	In the objects and reasons it was stated that the definition "estate" was not wide enough, that the courts had struck down many land reform Acts and that, therefore, in order to give them protection the amendment was made.
Fact	The validity of the Seventeenth Amendment Act was questioned in this Court and was held to be valid in <NAME> case .
RatioOfTheDecision	From the history of these amendments, two things appear, namely unconstitutional laws were made and they were protected by the amendment of the Constitution or the amendments were made in order to protect the future laws which would be void but for the amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the fact remains that this Court held as early as in 1951 that <ORG> had power to amend the fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may, therefore, be said that the Const itution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955, and the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, were based upon the scope of the power to amend recognized by this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further the Seventeenth Amendment Act was also approved by this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	59.
RatioOfTheDecision	Between 1950 and 1967 the <ORG> of various States made laws bringing about an agrarian revolution in our country - zamindaries, inams and other intermediary estates were abolished, vested rights were created in tenants, consolidation of holdings of villages was made, ceilings were fixed and the surplus lands transferred to tenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	All these were done on the basis of the correctness of the decisions in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R 89) and <NAME> case , namely, that <ORG> had the power to amend the fundamental rights and that Acts in regard to estates were outside judicial scrutiny on the ground they infringed the said rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The agrarian structure of our country has been revolutionised on the basis of the said laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	Should we now give retrosp ectivity to our decision, it would introduce chaos and unsettle the conditions in our country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Should we hold that because of the said consequences <ORG> had power to take away fundamental rights, a time might come when we would gradually and imperceptibly pass under a totalitarian rule.
