RatioOfTheDecision	Learned counsel for the petitioners as well as those for the respondents placed us on the horns of this dilemma, for they have taken extreme positions - learned counsel for the petitioners want us to reach the logical position by holding that all the said laws are void and the learned counsel for the respondents persuade us to hold that <ORG> has unlimited power and, if it chooses, it can do away with fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not think that this Court is so helpless.
RatioOfTheDecision	As the highest Court in the land we must evolve some reasonable principle to meet this extraordinary situation.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is an essential distinction between Constitution and statutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Comparatively speaking, Constitution is permanent; it is an organic sta tute; it grows by its own inherent force.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constitutional concepts are couched in elastic terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG>s are expected to and indeed should interpret, its terms without doing violence to the language to suit the expanding needs of the society.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this process and in a real sense they make laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though it is not admitted, the said role of this <ORG> is effective and cannot be ignored.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in the realm of ordinary statutes, the subtle working of the process is apparent though the approach is more conservative and inhibitive.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the constitutional field therefore, to meet the present extraordinary situation that may be caused by our decision, we must evolve some doctrine which has roots in reason and precedents so that the past may be preserved and the future protected.
RatioOfTheDecision	60.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are two doctrines familiar to American Jurisprudence, one is described as Blackstonian theory and the other as "prospective over-ruling", which may have some relevance to the present enquiry.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his Commentaries, 69 (15th  edn., 1809) stated the common law rule that the duty of the <ORG> was "not to pronounce a new rule but to maintain and expound the old one".
RatioOfTheDecision	It means the Judge does not make law but only discovers or finds the true law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law has always been the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a subsequent decision changes the earlier one, the latter decision does not make law but only discovers the correct principle of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result of this view is that it is necessarily retrospective operation.
RatioOfTheDecision	But Jurists, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, have expounded the doctrine of "prospective over-ruling" and suggested it as "a useful judicial tool".
Precedent	In the words of <NAME> the said expression means :  "........ a court should recognize a duty to announce a new and better rule for future transactions whenever the court has reached the conviction that an old rule (as established by the precedents) is unsound even though feeling compelled by stare decisis to apply the old and condemned rule to the instant case and  to transactions which had already taken place".
Precedent	61.
Precedent	<NAME>, before he became a Judge of <ORG> of the United States of America, when he was the Chief Justice of New York State addressing <ORG> said thus :  62.
Precedent	The rule (the Blackstonian rule) that we are asked to apply is out of tune with the life about us.
Precedent	It has been made discordant by the forces that generate a living law.
Precedent	We apply it to this case because the repeal might work hardship to those who have trusted to its existence.
Precedent	We hive notice however that any one trusting to it hereafter will do at his peril".
Precedent	63.
Precedent	<ORG> of the United States of America, in the year 1932, after <NAME> became an Associate Justice of that <ORG> in <ORG> v. <ORG>, , applied the said doctrine to the facts of that case.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that case the Montana <ORG> had adhered to its previous construction of the statute in question but had announced that interpretation would not be followed in the future.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was conte nded before <ORG> of the United States of America that a decision of a court over-ruling earlier decision and not giving its ruling retro-active operation violated the due process clause of the 14th Amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rejecting that plea, <NAME> said :  "This is not a case where a <ORG> in overruling an earlier decision has come to the new ruling of retro-active dealing and thereby has made invalid what was followed in the doing.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even that may often be done though litigants not infrequently have argued to the contrary........
RatioOfTheDecision	This is a case where a <ORG> has refused to make its ruling retro-active, and the novel stand is taken that the Constitution of the United States is infringed by the refusal.
RatioOfTheDecision	We think that the Federal Constitution has no voice upon the subject.
RatioOfTheDecision	A state in defining the elements of adherence to precedent may make a choice for itself between the principle of forward operation and that of relation backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be so that the decision of the highest courts, though later over-ruled, w as law nonetheless for intermediate transactions........ On the other hand, it may hold to the ancient dogma that the law declared by its <ORG> had a platonic or ideal existence before the act of declaration, in which event, the discredited declaration will be viewed as if it had never been and to reconsider declaration as law from the beginning......
Precedent	The choice for any state may be determined by the juristic philosophy of the Judges of her <ORG>, their considerations of law, its origin and nature".
Precedent	64.
Precedent	The opinion of <NAME> tried to harmonize the doctrine of prospective over-ruling with that of stare decisis.
Precedent	65.
Precedent	In 1940, <NAME>, C.J., in Chicot County Drainage District v. <ORG> ([1940] 308 U.S. 371), sated thus :  "The law prior to the determination of unconstitutionality is an operative fact and may have consequences which cannot justly be ignored.
Precedent	The past cannot always be erased by a new judicial declaration".
Precedent	66.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME> ([1956] 351 U.S. 12, 2), <ORG> ica reaffirmed the doctrine laid down in <NAME>'s case (sic).
Precedent	There, a statute required defendants to submit bills of exceptions as a pre-requisite to an appeal from a conviction; the Act was held unconstitutional in that it provided no means whereby indigent defendants could secure a copy of the record for this purpose.
Precedent	<NAME>, J., in that context observed :  "......... in arriving at a new principle, the judicial process is not important to define its scope and limits.
Precedent	Adjudication is not a mechanical exercise nor does it compel 'either/or' determination".
Precedent	67.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. Colorado , a majority of <ORG> held that in a prosecution in <ORG> for a state crime, the 14th Amendment did not forbid the admission of evidence obtained by an unreasonable search and seizure.
Precedent	But in <NAME>.
Precedent	v. Ohio , <ORG> reversed that decision and held that all evidence obtained by searches and seizure in violation of the 4th Amendment of the Federal Constitution was, by virtue of the due process cl ause of the 14th Amendment guaranteeing the right to privacy free from unreasonable <ORG> instrusion, inadmissible in a <ORG> Court.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME> ([1965] 381 U.S. 618), the question arose whether the exclusion of the rule enunciated in <NAME> v. Ohio , did not apply to <ORG> Court convictions which had become final before the date of that judgment.
Precedent	Mr. Justice <NAME>, speaking for the majority observed :  "We believe that the existence of the <NAME> doctrine prior to <NAME> is 'an operative' fact and may have consequences which cannot justly be ignored.
Precedent	The past cannot always be erased by a new judicial declaration".
Precedent	"<NAME> had as its prima purpose the enforcement of the Fourth Amendment through the inclusion of the exclusionary rule within its rights ..................  We cannot say that this purpose would be advanced by making the rule retrospective.
Precedent	The misconduct of the police prior to <NAME> has already occurred and will not be corrected by releasing the prisoners involved......... On the other hand,  the States relied on <NAME> and followed its command.
Precedent	Final judgments of conviction were entered prior to <NAME>.
Precedent	Again and again the <ORG> refused to reconsider <NAME> and gave its implicit approval to hundreds of cases in their application of its rule.
Precedent	In rejecting the <NAME> doctrine as to the exclusionary rule the purpose was to deter the lawless action of the police and to effectively enforce the Fourth Amendment.
Precedent	That purpose will not at this late date be served by the wholesale release of the guilty victims".
Precedent	"Finally, there are interests in the administration of justice and the integrity of the judicial process to consider.
Precedent	To make the rule of <NAME> retrospective would tax the administration of justice to the utmost.
Precedent	Hearings would have to be held on the excludability of evidence long since destroyed, misplaced or deteriorated.
Precedent	If it is excluded, the witness available at the time of the original trial will not be available or if located their memory will be dimmed.
Precedent	To thus legitimate such an extraordinary pr ocedural weapon that has no bearing on guilt would seriously disrupt the administration of justice".
Precedent	68.
Precedent	This case has reaffirmed the doctrine of prospective overruling and has taken a pragmatic approach in refusing to give it retroactivity.
Precedent	In short, in America the doctrine of prospective overruling is now accepted in all branches of law, including constitutional law.
Precedent	But the carving of the limits of retrospectivity of the new rule is left to courts to be done, having regard to the requirements of justice.
Precedent	Even in England the Blackstonian theory was criticized by <NAME> and <NAME>.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s Jurisprudence, 4th Ed., at page 65, the learned author says :  "What hindered <NAME> was 'the childish fiction' employed by our judges, that judiciary or common law is not made by them, but is a miraculous something made, by nobody, existing, I suppose, from eternity, and merely declared from time to time by the Judges".
Precedent	69.
Precedent	Though <ORG> in the past-accepted the Blackstonian theory and though the <ORG>  of Lords strictly adhered to the doctrine of 'precedent' in the earlier years, both the doctrines were practically given up by the "Practice Statement (Judicial Precedent)" issued by <ORG> recorded in (1966) 1 W.L.R.
Precedent	1234.
Precedent	Lord <NAME>, speaking for <ORG> made the following observations :  "Their Lordships nevertheless recognise that too rigid adherence to precedent may lead to injustice in a particular case and also unduly restrict the proper development of the law.
Precedent	They propose, therefore, to modify their present practice and, while treating former decisions of this <ORG> as normally binding, to depart from a previous decision when it appears right to do so".
Precedent	70.
Precedent	In this connection they will bear in mind the danger of disturbing retrospectively the basis on which contracts, settlements of property and fiscal arrangements have been entered into and also the especial need for certainty as to the criminal law".
Precedent	This announcement is not intended to affect the use of precedent e lsewhere than in this <ORG>".
Precedent	71.
Precedent	It will be seen from this passage that the <ORG> of Lords hereafter in appropriate cases may depart from its previous decision when it appears right to do so and in so departing will bear in mind the danger of giving effect to the said decision retroactivity.
Precedent	We consider that what the <ORG> of Lords means by this statement is that in differing from the precedents it will do so only without interefering with the transactions that had taken place on the basis of earlier decisions.
Precedent	This decision, to a large extent, modifies the Blackstonian theory and accepts, though not expressly but by necessary implication the doctrine of "prospective overruling".
Precedent	72.
Precedent	Let us now consider some of the objections to this doctrine.
Precedent	The objections are : (1) the doctrine involved legislation by courts; (2) it would not encourage parties to prefer appeals as they would not get any benefit therefrom; (3) the declaration for the future would only be obiter; (4) it is not a desirable change; and (5)  the doctrine of retroactivity serves as a brake on courts which otherwise might be tempted to be so fascile in overruling.
Precedent	But in our view, these objections are not insurmountable.
Precedent	If a court can over-rule its earlier decision - there cannot be any dispute now that the court can do so - there cannot be any valid reason why it should not restrict its ruling to the future and not to the past.
Precedent	Even if the party filing an appeal may not be benefited by it, in similar appeals which he may file after the change in the law he will have the benefit.
Precedent	The decision cannot be obiter for what the court in effect does is to declare the law but on the basis of another doctrine restricts its scope.
Precedent	Stability in law does not mean that injustice shall be perpetuated.
Precedent	An illuminating article on the subject is found in <ORG> [Vol.
Precedent	110 p. 650].
Precedent	73.
Precedent	It is a modern doctrine suitable for a fast moving society.
Precedent	It does not do away with the doctrine of stare decisis, but confines it to past transactions.
Precedent	It is true  that in one sense the court only declares the law, either customary or statutory or personal law.
Precedent	While in strict theory it may be said that the doctrine involves making of law, what the court really does is to declare the law but refuses to give retroactivity to it.
Precedent	It is really a pragmatic solution reconciling the two conflicting doctrines, namely, that a court finds law and that it does make law.
Precedent	It finds law but restricts its operation to the future.
Precedent	It enables the court to bring about a smooth transition by correcting its errors without disturbing the impact of those errors on the past transactions.
Precedent	It is left to the discretion of the court to prescribe the limits of the retroactivity and thereby it enables it to mould the relief to meet the ends of justice.
Precedent	74.
Precedent	In India there is no statutory prohibition against the court refusing to give retroactivity to the law declared by it.
Precedent	Indeed, the doctrine of res judicata precludes any scope for retroactivity in respect of a subject-matter that has been finall y decided between the parties.
Precedent	Further, Indian court by interpretation reject retroactivity to statutory provisions though couched in general terms on the ground that they affect vested rights.
Precedent	The present case only attempts a further extension of the said rule against retroactivity.
Precedent	75.
Precedent	Our Constitution does not expressly or by necessary implication speak against the doctrine of prospective over-ruling.
Precedent	Indeed, Arts.
Precedent	32, 141 and 142 are couched in such wide and elastic terms as to enable this <ORG> to formulate legal doctrines to meet the ends of justice.
Precedent	The only limitation thereon is reason, restraint and injustice.
Precedent	Under Art.
Precedent	32 , for the enforcement of the fundamental rights the Supreme <ORG> has the power to issue suitable directions or orders or writs.
Precedent	Article 141 says that the law declared by the Supreme <ORG> shall be binding on all courts; and Art.
Precedent	142 enables it in the exercise of its jurisdiction to pass such decree or make such order as is necessary for doing complete justice in any cause or mat ter pending before it.
Precedent	These articles are designedly made comprehensive to enable <ORG> to declare law and to give such directions or pass such orders as are necessary to do complete justice.
Precedent	The expression "declared" is wider than the words "found or made".
Precedent	To declare is to announce opinion.
Precedent	Indeed, the latter involves the process, while the former expresses result.
Precedent	Interpretation, ascertainment and evolution are parts of the process, while that interpreted, ascertained or evolved is declared as law.
Precedent	The law declared by <ORG> is the law of the land.
Precedent	If so, we do not see any acceptable reason why it, in declaring the law in supersession of the law declared by it earlier, could not restrict the operation of the law as declared to future and save the transactions, whether statutory or otherwise that were effected on the basis of the earlier law.
Precedent	To deny this power to <ORG> on the basis of some outmoded theory that the <ORG> only finds law but does to make it is to make ineffe ctive the powerful instrument of justice placed in the hands of the highest judiciary of this country.
Precedent	76.
Precedent	As this Court for the first time has been called upon to apply the doctrine evolved in a different country under different circumstances, we would like to move warily in the beginning.
Precedent	We would lay down the following propositions : (1) The doctrine of prospective over-ruling can be invoked only in matters arising under our Constitution; (2) it can be applied only by the highest court of the country, i.e., <ORG> as it has the constitutional jurisdiction to declare law binding on all the courts in India; (3) the scope of the retroactive operation of the law declared by <ORG> superseding its earlier decisions is left to its discretion to be moulded in accordance with the justice of the cause or matter before it.
Precedent	77.
Precedent	We have arrived at two conclusions, namely, (1) <ORG> has no power to amend Part III of the Constitution so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights; and  (2) this is a fit case to invoke and apply the doctrine of prospective overruling.
Precedent	What then is the effect of our conclusion on the instant case ?
Precedent	Having regard to the history of the amendments, their impact on the social and economic affairs of our country and the chaotic situation that may be brought about by the sudden withdrawal at this stage of the amendments from the Constitution, we think that considerable judicial restraint is called for.
Precedent	We, therefore, declare that our decisions will not affect the validity of the constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, or other amendments made to the Constitution taking away or abridging the fundamental rights.
Precedent	We further declare that in future <ORG> will have no power to amend Part III of the Constitution so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights.
Precedent	In this case we do not propose to express our opinion on the question of the scope of the amendability of the provisions of the Constitution other than the fundamental rights, as it does not arise  for consideration before us.
Precedent	Nor are we called upon to express out opinion on the question regarding the scope of the amendability of Part III of the Constitution otherwise than by taking away or abridging the fundamental rights.
Precedent	We will not also indicate our view one way or other whether any of the Acts questioned can be sustained under the provisions of the Constitution without the aid of Arts.
Precedent	31A, 31B and the 9th Schedule.
Precedent	78.
Precedent	The aforesaid discussion leads to the following results :  (1) The power of the <ORG> to amend the Constitution is derived from Arts.
Precedent	245, 246 and 248 of the Constitution and not from Art.
Precedent	368 thereof which only deals with procedure.
Precedent	Amendment is a legislative process.
Precedent	(2) Amendment is 'law' within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	13 of the Constitution and, therefore, if it takes away or abridges the rights conferred by Part III thereof, it is void.
Precedent	(3) The Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951, Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955, and the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) A ct, 1964, abridge the scope of the fundamental rights.
Precedent	But, on the basis of earlier decisions of this <ORG>, they were valid.
Precedent	(4) On the application of the doctrine of 'prospective over-ruling', as explained by us earlier, our decision will have only prospective operation and, therefore, the said amendments will continue to be valid.
Precedent	(5) We declare that the <ORG> will have no power from the date of this decision to amend any of the provisions of Part III of the Constitution so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights enshrined therein.
Precedent	(6) As the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act holds the filed, the validity of the two impugned Acts, namely, the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act X of 1953, and the Mysore Land Reforms Act X of 1962, as amended by Act XIV of 1965, cannot be questioned on the ground that they offend Arts.
Precedent	13, 14, or 31 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Before we close, it would be necessary to advert to an argument advanced on emotional plane.
Precedent	It was said that if the provisions of  the Constitution could not be amended it would lead to revolution.
Precedent	We have not said that the provisions of the Constitution cannot be amended but what we have said is that they cannot be amended so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights.
Precedent	Nor can we appreciate the argument that all the agrarian reforms which the <ORG> in power wants to effectuate cannot be brought about without amending the fundamental rights.
Precedent	It was exactly to prevent this attitude and to project the rights of the people that the fundamental rights were inserted in the Constitution.
Precedent	If it is the duty of the <ORG> to enforce the directive principles, it is equally its duty to enforce them without infringing the fundamental rights.
Precedent	The Constitution-makers thought that it could be done and we also think that the directive principles can reasonably be enforced with the self-regulatory machinery provided by Part III.
Precedent	Indeed both Parts III and IV of the Constitution form an integrated scheme and is elastic enough to respond to  the changing needs of the society.
Precedent	The verdict of the <ORG> on the scope of the law of social control of fundamental rights is not final, but justiciable.
Precedent	If not so, the whole scheme of the Constitution will break.
Precedent	What we cannot understand is how the enforcement of the provisions of the Constitution can bring about a revolution.
Precedent	History shows that revolutions are brought about not by the majorities but by the minorities and some time by military coups.
Precedent	The existence of an all comprehensive amending power cannot prevent revolutions, if there is chaos in the country brought about by mis-rule or abuse of power.
Precedent	On the other hand, such a restrictive power gives stability to the country and prevents it from passing under a totalitarian or dictatorial regime.
Precedent	We cannot obviously base our decision on such hypothetical or extraordinary situations which may be brought about with or without amendments.
Precedent	Indeed, a Constitution is only permanent and not eternal.
Precedent	There is nothing to choose between destruction by ame ndment or by revolution, the former is brought about by totalitarian rule, which cannot brook constitutional checks and the other by the discontentment brought about by mis-rule.
Precedent	If either happens, the constitution will be a scrap of paper.
Precedent	Such considerations are out of place in construing the provisions of the Constitution by a court of law.
Precedent	79.
Precedent	Nor are we impressed by the argument that if the power of amendment is not all comprehensive there will be no way to change the structure of our Constitution or abridge the fundamental rights even if the whole country demands for such a change.
Precedent	Firstly, this visualizes an extremely unforeseeable and extravagant demand; but even if such a contingency arises, the residuary power of the parliament may be relied upon to call for <ORG> for making a new Constitution of radically changing it.
Precedent	The recent Act providing for a poll in Goa, Daman and Diu is an instance of analogous exercise of such residuary power by the <ORG>.
Precedent	We do not express our final  opinion on this important question.
Precedent	80.
Precedent	A final appeal is made to us that we shall not take a different view as the decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89, 105), held the field for many years.
Precedent	While ordinarily this <ORG> will be reluctant to reverse its previous decision, it is its duty in the constitutional field to correct itself as early as possible, for otherwise the future progress of the country and the happiness of the people will be at stake.
Precedent	As we are convinced that the decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89, 105) is wrong, it is pre-eminently a typical case where this <ORG> should over-rule it.
Precedent	The longer it holds the field the greater will be the scope for erosion of fundamental rights.
Precedent	As it contains the seeds of destruction of the cherished rights of the people the sooner it is over-ruled the better for the country.
Precedent	81.
Precedent	This argument is answered by the remarks made by this <ORG> in the recent judgment in <CITATION_25576> .
Precedent	"The third contention need not detain us for  it has been rejected by this <ORG> in <CITATION_608874> .
Precedent	There <ORG> 7 Judges unanimously held that there was nothing in the Constitution that prevented the Supreme <ORG> from departing from a previous decision of its own it was satisfied of its error and of its baneful effect on the general interest of the public.
Precedent	If the aforesaid rule of construction accepted by this <ORG> is inconsistent with the legal philosophy of our Constitution, it is our duty to correct ourselves and lay down the right rule.
Precedent	In constitutional matters which affect the evolution of our polity, we must more readily do so than in other branches of law, as perpetuation of a mistake will be harmful to public interests.
Precedent	While continuity and consistency are conducive to the smooth evolution of the rule of law, hesitancy to set right deviation will retard its growth.
Precedent	In this case, as we are satisfied that the said rule of construction is inconsistent with our republican polity and, if accepted, bristles with anomalies, we have no hesit ation to reconsider our earlier decision".
Precedent	82.
Precedent	In the result the petitions are dismissed, but in the circumstances without costs.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	83.
Precedent	This Special Bench of eleven Judges of this Court has been constituted to consider the correctness of the decision of this Court in <CITATION_1706770> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), which was accepted as correct by the majority in <CITATION_1308308> .
Precedent	84.
Fact	The reference has been made in three petitions challenging the constitutionality of the Seventeenth Amendment to the Constitution.
Fact	In one of the petitions, the inclusion of the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act, (No.
Fact	X of 1953) in the Ninth Schedule, which makes it immune from attack under any provisions contained in Part III of the Constitution, has been attacked on the ground that the Seventeenth Amendment is in itself unconstitutional.
Fact	In the other two petitions, the inclusion of the Mysore Land Reforms Act.
Fact	(No.
Fact	10 of 1962) has been attacked on the same grounds.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not necessary to set out the facts in the three petiti ons for present purposes.
Fact	The main argument in all the three petitions has been as to the scope and effect of Art.
Precedent	368 of the Constitution and the power conferred thereby to amend the Constitution.
Precedent	85.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before we come to the specific points raised in the present petitions, we may indicate the circumstances in which <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), as well as <NAME> case , came to be decided and what they actually decided.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution came into force on January 26, 1950.
Statute	It provides in Part III for certain fundamental rights.
Statute	Article 31 which is in Part III, as it originally stood, provided for compulsory acquisition of property.
Statute	By clause (1) it provided that "no person shall be deprived of his property save by authority of law".
Statute	Clause (2) thereof provided that any law authorising taking of possession or acquisition of property must provide for compensation therefore and either fix the amount of compensation or specify the principles on which, and the manner in which, the compensation w as to be determined and paid.
Statute	Clause (4) made a special provision to the effect that if any Bill pending at the commencement of the Constitution on the <ORG> of a State had, after it had been passed by such <ORG>, been reserved for the consideration of the President and had received his assent, then such law would not be called in question though it contravened the provisions of clause (2) relating to compensation.
Statute	Clause (6) provided that any law of the State enacted not more than eighteen months before the Constitution might be submitted to the President for his certification, and if so certified, it could not be called in question on the ground that it contravened the provisions of clause (2) of Art.
Statute	31 relating to compensation.
RatioOfTheDecision	86.
RatioOfTheDecision	These two clauses of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31 were meant to safeguard legislation which either had been passed by Provincial or State legislatures or which was on the anvil of State legislatures for the purpose of agrarian reforms.
Precedent	One such piece of legislation was the Bihar Land R eforms Act, which was passed in 1950.
Precedent	That Act received the assent of the President as required under clause (6) of Art.
Precedent	31.
Precedent	It was however challenged before <ORG> and was struck down by that court on the ground that it violated Art.
Precedent	14 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Then there was an appeal before this <ORG>, but while that appeal was pending, the First Amendment to the Constitution was made.
Precedent	87.
Precedent	We may briefly refer to what the First Amendment provided for.
Precedent	It was the First Amendment which was challenged and was upheld in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89).
Precedent	The First Amendment contained a number of provisions; but it is necessary for present purposes only to refer to those provisions which made changes in Part III of the Constitution.
Precedent	These changes related to Arts.
Precedent	15 and 19 and in addition, provided for insertion of two Articles numbered 31-A and 31-B in Part III.
Precedent	Article 31-A provided that no law providing for the acquisition by the <ORG> of any estate or of any rights therein or the extinguis hment or modification of any such rights shall be deemed to be void on the ground that it was inconsistent with, or took away or abridged any of the rights conferred by any provision in part III.
Precedent	The word "estate" was also defined for the purpose of Art.
Precedent	31-A.
Precedent	Further Article 31-B , provided for validation of certain Acts and Regulations and specified such Acts and Regulations in the Ninth Schedule, which was for the first time added to the Constitution.
Precedent	The ninth Schedule then contained 13 Acts, all relating to estates, passed by various legislatures of the Provinces or States.
Precedent	It laid down that those Acts and Regulations would not be deemed to be void or ever to have become void, on the ground that they were inconsistent with or took away or abridged any of the rights conferred by any provision of Part III.
Precedent	It further provided that notwithstanding any judgment, decree or order of any court or tribunal to the contrary, all such Acts and Regulations subject to the power of any competent legislature to repeal  or amend them, continue in force.
Precedent	88.
Precedent	This amendment, and in particular Arts.
Precedent	31-A and 31-B were immediately challenged by various writ petitions in this <ORG> and these came to be decided on October 5, 1951 in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89).
Precedent	The attack on the validity of the First Amendment was made on various grounds; but three main grounds which were taken were, firstly, that amendments to the Constitution made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 were liable to be tested under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2); secondly that in any case as Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B inserted in the Constitution by the First Amendment affected the power of the High <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and of this <ORG> under Articles 132 and 136, the amendment required ratification under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368; and, thirdly, that Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B were invalid on the ground that they related to matters covered by the State List, namely, item 18 of List II, and could not therefore be passed by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> rejected all the three contentions.
RatioOfTheDecision	It held that although "la w" would ordinarily include constitutional law, there was a clear demarcation between ordinary law made in the exercise of legislative power and constitutional law made in the exercise of constituent power, and in the context of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13 , "law" must be taken to mean rules or regulations made in exercise of ordinary legislative power and not amendments to the Constitution made in the exercise of constituent power; in consequence Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) did not affect amendments made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	It further held that Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B did not curtail the power of <ORG> under Art.
Fact	226 or of this court under Arts.
Fact	132 and 136 and did not require ratification under the proviso contained in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	Finally, it was held that Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B were essentially amendments to the Constitution and <ORG> as such had the power to enact such amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	In consequence, the First Amendment to the Constitution was upheld as valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	89.
Fact	After this decision, there followed sixteen more amendments to the Constitution  till we come to the Seventeenth Amendment, which was passed on June 20, 1964.
Fact	There does not seem to have been challenge to any amendment up to the Sixteenth Amendment, even though two of them, namely, the Fourth Amendment and the Sixteenth Amendment, contained changes in the provisions of Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further the nature of these amendments was to add to, or alter or delete various other provisions of the Constitution contained in Part III thereof.
RatioOfTheDecision	On December 5, 1961, came the decision of this Court by which the Kerala Agrarain Reforms Act (No.
Precedent	4 of 1961), passed by the Kerala legislature, was struck down, among other grounds, for the reason that ryotwari lands in South India were not estates within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	31-A and therefore acquisition of ryotwari land was not protected under Art.
Precedent	31-A of the Constitution : [see <CITATION_9285> ( Supp.
Precedent	1 S.C.R.
Precedent	829)].
Fact	This decision was followed by the Seventeenth Amendment on June 20, 1964.
Fact	By this amendment, changes were made in Art.
Fact	31-A of t he Constitution and 44 Acts were included in the Ninth Schedule to give them complete protection from attack under any provision of Part III of the Constitution.
Fact	Practically all these Acts related to land tenures and were concerned with agrarian reforms.
Fact	This amendment was challenged before this <ORG> in <NAME> case .
Fact	The points then urged were that as Art.
Fact	226 was likely to be affected by the Seventeenth Amendment, it required ratification under the proviso to Art.
Fact	368 and that the decision in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Fact	89), which had negatived this contention required reconsideration.
Fact	It was also urged that the Seventeenth Amendment was legislation with respect to land and <ORG> had no right to legislate in that behalf, and further that as the Seventeenth Amendment provided that the Acts put in the Ninth Schedule would be valid in spite of the decision of the <ORG>, it was unconstitutional.
RulingByLowerCourt	This <ORG> by majority of 3 to 2 upheld the correctness of the decision in <NAME> case ( S .C.R 89).
RulingByLowerCourt	It further held unanimously that the Seventeenth Amendment did not require ratification under the proviso to Art.
Precedent	368 because of its indirect effect on Art.
Precedent	226 , and that <ORG> in enacting the Amendment was not legislating with respect to land and that it was open to <ORG> to validate legislation which had been declared invalid by courts.
Precedent	Finally this <ORG> held by majority that the power conferred by Art.
Precedent	368 included the power to take away fundamental rights guaranteed by Part III and that the power to amend was a very wide power and could not be controlled by the literal dictionary meaning of the word "amend", and that the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) did not include an amendment of the Constitution made in pursuance of Art.
Precedent	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	The minority however doubted the correctness of the view taken in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), to the effect that the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) did not include amendment to the Constitution made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and therefore doubted the competence of <NAME> nt to make any amendment to Part III of the Constitution.
Precedent	One of the learned Judges further doubted whether making a change in the basic features of the Constitution could be regarded merely as an amendment or would, in effect, be re-writing a part of the Constitution, and if so, whether it could be done under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It was because of this doubt thrown on the correctness of the view taken in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), that the present reference has been made to this Special Bench.
Precedent	90.
Precedent	As the question referred to this Bench is of great constitutional importance and affected legislation passed by various States, notice was issued to the Advocates General of all States and they have appeared and intervened before us.
Precedent	Further a number of persons who were also affected by the Seventeenth Amendment have been permitted to intervene.
Precedent	The arguments on behalf of the petitioners and the interveners who support them may now be briefly summarised.
Precedent	91.
Precedent	It is urged that Art.
Precedent	368 when it provides for the amendm ent of the Constitution merely contains the procedure for doing so and that the power to make amendment has to be found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is further urged that the word "amendment" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 means that the provisions in the Constitution can be changed so as to improve upon them and that this power is of a limited character and does not authorise <ORG> to make any addition to, alteration of or deletion of any provision of the Constitution, including the provision contained in Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	So Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 authorise only those amendments which have the effect of improving the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then it is urged that amendment permissible under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is subject to certain implied limitations and these limitations are that basic features of the Constitution cannot be amended at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	An attempt was made to indicate some of these basic features, as, for example, the provisions in Part III, the federal structure, the republican character of the <ORG>, elected <ORG> and <ORG> Legislatures  on the basis of adult suffrage, control by the judiciary and so on, and it is said that an amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is subject to the implied limitations that these basic features and others of the kind cannot be changed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus in effect the argument is that there is a very limited power of amendment under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	92.
Statute	It is further urged that apart from these implied limitations, there is an express limitation under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) and the word "law" in that Article includes an amendment of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument thus in the alternative is that as the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) includes a constitutional amendment, no amendment can be made in Part III under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 which would actually take away or abridge the rights guaranteed under that Part.
RatioOfTheDecision	In effect, it is said that even if there are no implied limitations to amend the Constitution under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) is an express limitation insofar as the power to amend Part III is concerned and by virtue of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) the rights guaranteed under Par t III cannot be taken away or abridged under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , though it is conceded that Part III may be amended by way of enlarging the rights contained therein.
Precedent	93.
Precedent	Another line of argument is that in any case it was necessary to take action under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and as that was not done the Seventeenth Amendment is not valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 is seriously affected by the provisions contained in the Seventeenth Amendment and that amounts to an amendment of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and in consequence action under the proviso was necessary.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also urged that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 was equally affected by the addition of a number of Acts in the Ninth Schedule read with Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) and therefore also it was necessary to take action under the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is further urged that it was not competent for <ORG> to amend the Constitution by putting a large number of Acts in the Ninth Schedule as the power to legislate with respect to land is solely within the competence of State Legislatures and that is another reason why the a ddition to the Ninth Schedule read with Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B should be struck down.
RatioOfTheDecision	94.
RatioOfTheDecision	Lastly an argument had been advanced, which we may call the argument of fear.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is held to confer full power to amend each and every part of the Constitution as has been held in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89).
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> may do all kinds of things, which were never intended, under this unfettered power and may, for example, abolish elected legislatures, abolish the President or change the present form of Government into a Presidential type like the United States Constitution or do away with the federal structure altogether.
RatioOfTheDecision	So it is urged that we should interpret Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 in such a way that <ORG> may not be able to do all these things.
RatioOfTheDecision	In effect this argument of fear has been put forward to reinforce the contention that this <ORG> should hold that there are some implied limitations on the amending power and these implied limitations should be that there is no power any where in the Constitution to  change the basic features of the Constitution to which reference has already been made.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is in brief the submission on behalf of the petitioners and the interveners who support them.
Precedent	95.
Precedent	The submission on behalf of the Union of India and the States may now be summarised.
Precedent	It is urged that Art.
Precedent	368 not only provides procedure for amendment but also contains in it the power to amend the Constitution.
Precedent	It is further urged that the word "amendment" in law does not merely mean making such changes in the Constitution as would improve it but includes the power to make any addition to the Constitution, any alteration of any of the existing provisions and its substitution by another provision, and any deletion of any particular provision of the Constitution.
Precedent	In effect, it is urged that even if the word "amendment" used in Art.
Precedent	368 does not take in the power to abrogate the entire Constitution and replace it by another new Constitution, it certainly means that any provisions of the Constitution may be changed and th is change can be in the form of addition to, alteration of or deletion of any provision of the Constitution.
Precedent	So long therefore as the Constitution is not entirely abrogated and replaced by a new Constitution at one stroke, the power of amendment would enable <ORG> to make all changes in the existing Constitution by addition, alteration or deletion.
Precedent	Subject only to complete repeal being not possible, the power of amendment contained in Art.
Precedent	368 is unfettered.
Precedent	It is further urged that there can be no implied limitations on the power to amend and the limitations if any on this power must be found in express terms in the Article providing for amendment.
Precedent	It is conceded that there may be an express limitation not merely in the Article providing for amendment but in some other part of the Constitution.
Precedent	But it is said that if that is so, there must be a clear provision to that effect.
Precedent	In the absence of express limitations, therefore, there can be no implied limitations on the power to amend the Constitution cont ained in Art.
Precedent	368 and that power will take in all changes whether by way of addition, alteration or deletion, subject only to this that the power of amendment may not contain the power to abrogate and repeal the entire Constitution and substitute it with a new one.
Precedent	96.
Precedent	It is then urged that there is no express provision in Art.
Precedent	368 itself so far as any amendment relating to the substance of the amending power is concerned; the only limitations in Art.
Precedent	368 are as to procedure and courts can only see that the procedure as indicated in Art.
Precedent	368 is followed before an amendment can be said to be valid.
Precedent	It is further urged that the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13 does not include an amendment of the Constitution and only means law as made under the legislative provisions contained in Chapter I of Part XI read with Chapters II and III of Part V of the Constitution and Chapters III and V of Part VI thereof.
Precedent	In effect it is a law which is made under the Constitution which is included in the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) and not an  amendment to the Constitution under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	97.
RatioOfTheDecision	As to Articles 226 and 245 and the necessity of taking action under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it is urged that there is no change in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and 245 on account of any provision in the Seventeenth Amendment and therefore no action under the proviso was necessary.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only direct change in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and 245 which would require following the procedure as to ratification or at any rate such change in other Articles which would have the effect of directly compelling change in Arts.
Precedent	226 and 245 and that in the present case no such direct compulsion arises.
Precedent	98.
Precedent	Lastly as to the argument of fear it is urged that there is always a provision with respect to amendment in written federal Constitutions.
Precedent	Such a provision may be rigid or flexible.
Precedent	In our Constitution Art.
Precedent	368 provides for a comparatively flexible provision for amendment and there is no reason to make it rigid by implying any limitations on that power.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further there is no reason to suppose that all those  things will be done by <ORG> which are being urged to deny the power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 which flows naturally from its terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	99.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides the above, reliance is also placed on behalf of the Union of India and the States on the doctrine of stare decisis.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that since the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), sixteen further amendments have been made by <ORG> on the faith of that decision involving over 200 Articles of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The amendments relating to Part III have been mainly with respect to agrarian reforms resulting in transfers of title of millions of acres of land in favour of millions of people.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, even though <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), has stood only for fifteen years there has been a vast agrarian revolution effected on the faith of that decision and this <ORG> should not now go back on what was decided in that case.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, besides the argument based on state decisis, it is urged on the basis of certain decisions of thi s Court that the unanimous decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), which had stood practically unchallenged for about 15 years till the decision in <NAME> case , should not be over-ruled unless it is found to be incorrect by a large majority of the Judges constituting this Special Bench.
Precedent	It is urged that if the present Bench is more or less evenly divided it should not over-rule, the unanimous decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), by a majority of one.
Precedent	100.
Precedent	We shall first take Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It is found in Part XX of the Constitution which is headed "Amendment of the Constitution" and is the only Article in that Part.
Precedent	That Part thus provides specifically for the amendment of the Constitution, and the first question that arises is whether it provides power for the amendment of the Constitution as well as the procedure for doing so.
Precedent	It is not disputed that the procedure for amendment of the Constitution is to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 , but what is in dispute is whether Art.
Precedent	368 con fers power also in that behalf.
Precedent	Now the procedure for the amendment of the Constitution is this.
Precedent	The amendment is initiated by the introduction of a Bill in either <ORG>.
Precedent	The Bill has to be passed in each <ORG> by a majority of the total membership of that <ORG> and by a majority of not less two-third of the members of that <ORG> present and voting.
Precedent	After it is so passed, it has to be presented to the President for his assent.
Precedent	On such presentation if the President assents to the Bill, Art.
Precedent	368 provides that the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further there is a proviso for ratification with respect to certain Articles and other provisions of the Constitution including Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , and those matters can only be amended if the Bill passed by the two <ORG>s by necessary majority is ratified by the legislatures of not less than one-half of the States by resolutions to that effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a case the Bill cannot be presented for his assent to the President unt il necessary ratification is available.
RatioOfTheDecision	But when the necessary ratification has been made, the Bill with respect to these matters is then presented to the President and on his assent being given, the Constitution stands amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	101.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument is that there is no express provision in terms in Art.
Precedent	368 conferring power on <ORG> to amend the Constitution, and in this connection our attention has been invited to an analogous provision in the Constitution of Ireland in Art.
Precedent	46 , where clause 1 provides that any provision of the Constitution may be amended in the manner provided in that Article, and then follows the procedure for amendment in clauses 2 to 5.
Precedent	Reference is also made to similar provisions in other constitution, but it is unnecessary to refer to them.
Precedent	It is urged that as Art.
Precedent	368 has nothing comparable to clause 1 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution, the power to amend the Constitution is not in Art.
Precedent	368 and must be found elsewhere.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are prepared to  accept this argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is not in two parts, the first part indicating that the Constitution shall be amended in the manner provided thereafter, and the second part indicating the procedure for amendment, does not mean that the power to amend the Constitution is not contained in Art.
Precedent	368 itself.
Precedent	The very fact that a separate Part has been devoted in the Constitution for amendment thereof and there is only one Article in that Part shows that both the power to amend and the procedure for amendment are to be found in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	Besides, the words "the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill" in Art.
Precedent	368 clearly in our opinion provide for the power to amend after the procedure has been followed.
Precedent	It appears that our Constitution-makers were apparently thinking of economy of words and elegance of language in enacting Art.
Precedent	368 in the terms in which it appears and that is why it is not in two parts on the model of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution.
Precedent	But there can  in our opinion be no doubt, when a separate Part was provided headed "Amendment of the Constitution" that the power to amend the Constitution must also be contained in Art.
Precedent	368 which is the only Article in that Part.
Precedent	If there was any doubt about the matter, that doubt in our opinion is resolved by the words to which we have already referred, namely, "the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill".
Precedent	These words can only mean that the power is there to amend the Constitution after the procedure has been followed.
Precedent	102.
Precedent	It is however urged that the power to amend the Constitution is not to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 but is contained in the residuary power of <ORG> in Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of List I, insofar as it provides for residuary power of legislation, is very wide in its scope, and the argument that the power to amend the Constitution is contained in this provision appears prima facie attractive in view of the width of  the residuary power.
RatioOfTheDecision	But we fail to see why when there is a whole Part devoted to the amendment of the Constitution the power to amend should not be found in that Part, if it can be reasonably found there and why Art.
RulingByPresentCourt	368 should only be confined to providing for procedure for amendment.
RulingByPresentCourt	It is true that the marginal note to Art.
Precedent	368 is "procedure for amendment of the Constitution", but the marginal note cannot control the meaning of the words in the Article itself, and we have no doubt that the words "the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill" to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 confer the power of amendment.
Precedent	If we were to compare the language of cls.
Precedent	2 to 5 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution which prescribes the procedure for amendment, we find no words therein comparable to these words in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	These words clearly are comparable to clause 1 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution and must be read as conferring power on <ORG> to amend the Constitution.
Precedent	Besides it is remarkable  in contrast that Art.
Precedent	248 read with List I does not in terms mention the amendment of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	While therefore there is a whole Part devoted to the amendment of the Constitution, we do not find any specific mention of the amendment of the Constitution in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 or in any entry of List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would in the circumstances be more appropriate to read the power in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 in view of the words which we have already referred to than in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides it is a historical fact to which we can refer that originally the intention was to vest residuary power in States, and if that intention had been eventually carried out, it would have been impossible for any one to argue that the power to amend the Constitution was to be found in the residuary power if it had been vested in the States and not in the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mere fact that during the passage of the Constitution by <ORG>, residuary power was finally vested in the <ORG> would not therefore mean that it include s the power to amend the Constitution.
Precedent	On a comparison of the scheme of the words in Art.
Precedent	368 and the scheme of the words in Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List I, therefore, there is no doubt in our mind that both the procedure and power to amend the Constitution are to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 and they are not to be found in Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List I which provides for residuary legislative power of <ORG>.
Precedent	103.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is in our opinion another reason why the power to amend the Constitution cannot be found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is the fundamental law and no law passed under mere legislative power conferred by the Constitution can affect any change in the Constitution unless there is an express power to that effect given in the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	But subject to such express power given by the Constitution itself the fundamental law, namely the Constitution, cannot be changed by a law passed under the legislative provisions contained in the Constitution as all l egislative acts passed under the power conferred by the Constitution must conform to the Constitution can make no change therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are a number of Article in the Constitution, which expressly provide for amendment by law, as, for example, 3, 4, 10, 59(3), 65(3), 73(2), 97, 98(3), 106, 120(2), 135, 137, 142(1), 146(2), 148(3), 149, 169, 171(2), 186, 187(3), 189(3), 194(3), 195, 210(2), 221(2), 225, 229(2), 239(1), 241(3), 283(1) and (2), 285(2), 287, 300(1), 313, 345, 373, Sch.
RatioOfTheDecision	V. clause 7 and Sch.
RatioOfTheDecision	VI clause 21; and so far as these Articles are concerned they can be amended by parliament by ordinary law-making process.
RatioOfTheDecision	But so far as the other Articles are concerned they can only be amended by amendment of the Constitution under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 which gives power to make law for the whole or any part of the territory of India by <ORG> is "subject to the provisions of this Constitution" and any law made by <ORG> whether under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	246 read with List I or under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of L ist I must be subject to the provisions of the Constitution.
Precedent	If therefore the power to amend the Constitution is contained in Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 List I, that power has to be exercised subject to the provisions of the Constitution and cannot be used to change the fundamental law (namely, the Constitution) itself.
Precedent	But it is argued that Art.
Precedent	368 which provides a special procedure for amendment of the Constitution should be read along with Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 and 248, and so read it would be open to amend any provisions of the Constitution by law passed under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 on the ground that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 is subject to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and therefore the two together give power to <ORG> to pass a law under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 which will amend even those provisions of the Constitution which are not expressly made amendable by law passed under the legislative power of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This in our opinion is arguing in a circle.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the fundamental law (i.e.
Precedent	the Constitution) cannot be changed by any law passed under the legislative power contained  therein, for legislation so passed must conform to the fundamental law, we fail to see how a law passed under the residuary power, which is nothing more than legislative power conferred on <ORG> under the Constitution, can change the Constitution (namely, the fundamental law) itself.
Precedent	104.
Precedent	We may in this connection refer to the following passage in The Law and the Constitution by <NAME> (1933 Ed.)
Precedent	at p. 51 onwards :-  "A written constitution is thus the fundamental law of a country, the express embodiment of the doctrine of the reign of law.
Precedent	All public authorities - legislative administrative and judicial - take their powers directly or indirectly from it....... whatever the nature of the written constitution it is clear that there "is a fundamental distinction between constitutional law and the rest of the law......
Precedent	There is a clear separation, therefore, between the constitutional law and the rest of the law".
Precedent	105.
Precedent	It is because of this difference between the fundamental law (namely, the Co nstitution) and the law passed under the legislative provisions of the Constitution that it is not possible in the absence of an express provision to that effect in the fundamental law to change the fundamental law by ordinary legislation passed thereunder, for such ordinary legislation must always conform to the fundamental law (i.e.
Precedent	the Constitution).
Precedent	If the power to amend the Constitution is to be found in Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List I, it will mean that ordinary legislation passed under the fundamental law would amend that law and this cannot be done unless there is express provision as in Art.
Precedent	3 etc.
Precedent	to that effect.
Precedent	In the absence of such express provision any law passed under the legislative powers granted under the fundamental law cannot amend it.
Precedent	So if we were to hold that the power to amend the Constitution is comprised in Art.
Precedent	248 , that would mean that no amendment of the Constitution would be possible at all except to the extent expressly provided in various Articles to which we have referr ed already, for the power to legislate under Art.
Precedent	245 read with Art.
Precedent	248 is itself subject to the Constitution.
Precedent	Therefore, reading Art.
Precedent	368 and considering the scheme of the legislative powers conferred by Articles 245 and 248 read with item 97 of List I, this to our mind is clear, firstly that the power to amend the Constitution is to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 itself, and secondly, that the power to amend the Constitution can never reside in Art.
Precedent	245 and Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List I, for that would make any amendment of the Constitution impossible except with respect to express provisions contained in certain Articles thereof for amendment by law.
Precedent	106.
Precedent	We may in this connection add that all this argument that power to amend the Constitution is to be found in Art.
Precedent	245 and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of List I has been based on one accidental circumstance, and that accidental circumstance is that the procedure for amendment of the Constitution contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is more or less assimilated to the procedu re for making ordinary laws under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument is that constitutional amendment is also passed by the two <ORG>, and is assented to by the President like ordinary legislation, with this difference that a special majority is required for certain purposes and a special majority plus ratification is required for certain other purposes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be admitted that the procedure for amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is somewhat similar to the procedure for passing ordinary legislation under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even so, as pointed out by Sir <NAME> under in the passage already quoted, there is a clear separation between constitutional law and the rest of the law and that must never be forgotten.
RatioOfTheDecision	An amendment to the Constitution is a constitutional law and as observed in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), is in exercise of constituent power; passing of ordinary law is in exercise of ordinary legislative power and is clearly different from the power to amend the Constitution.
Precedent	We may in this  connection refer, for example, to Art.
Precedent	V of the U.S. Constitution, which provides for the amendment thereof.
Precedent	It will be clearly seen that the power contained in Art.
Precedent	V of the U.S. Constitution is not ordinary legislative power and no one can possibly call it ordinary legislative power, because the procedure provided for the amendment of the Constitution in Art.
Precedent	V differs radically from the procedure provided for ordinary legislation, for example, the President's assent is not required for constitutional amendment under Art.
Precedent	V of the U.S. Constitution.
Precedent	Now if Art.
Precedent	368 also had made a similar departure from the procedure provided for ordinary legislation, it could never have been said that Art.
Precedent	368 merely contained the procedure for amendment and that what emerges after that procedure is followed is ordinary law of the same quality and nature as emerges after following the procedure for passing ordinary law.
Precedent	If, for example, the assent of the President which is to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 had not been there and the  Constitution would have stood amended after the Bill had been passed by the two <ORG> by necessary majority and after ratification by not less than one-half of the States where so required, it could never have been argued that the power to amend the Constitution was contained in Arts.
Precedent	245 and 248 read with item 97 of List I and Art.
Precedent	368 merely contained the procedure.
Precedent	107.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are however of opinion that we should look at the quality and nature of what is done under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and not lay so much stress on the similarity of the procedure contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 with the procedure for ordinary law-making.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we thus look at the quality and nature of what is done under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , we find that it is the exercise of constituent power for the purpose of amending the Constitution itself and is very different from the exercise of ordinary legislative power for passing laws which must be in conformity with the Constitution and cannot go against any provision thereof, unless there is express provision to that effect to  which we have already referred.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we thus refer to the nature and quality of what is done under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , we immediately see that what emerges after the procedure in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is gone through is not ordinary law which emerges after the legislative procedure contained in the Constitution is gone through.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 provides for the coming into existence of what may be called the fundamental law in the form of an amendment of the Constitution and therefore what emerges after the procedure under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is gone through is not ordinary legislation but an amendment of the Constitution which becomes a part of the fundamental law itself, by virtue of the words contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 to the effect that the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	108.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged in this connection on behalf of the Union of India that even though the assent of the President is required under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , the President must assent thereto and cannot withhold his assent as is possible in the case of  ordinary law in view of Art.
Precedent	111 of the Constitution, for the word "that he withholds assent therefrom" found in Art.
Precedent	111 are not to be found in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It is however difficult to accept the argument on behalf of the <ORG> that the President cannot withhold his assent when a Bill for amendment of the Constitution is presented to him.
Precedent	Article 368 provides that a Bill for the amendment of the Constitution shall be presented to the President for his assent.
Precedent	It further provides that upon such assent by the President, the Constitution shall stand amended.
Precedent	That in our opinion postulates that if assent is not given, the Constitution cannot be amended.
Precedent	Whether a President will ever withhold his assent in our form of Government is a different matter altogether, but as we read Art.
Precedent	368 we cannot hold that the President is bound to assent and cannot withhold his assent when a Bill for amendment of the Constitution is presented to him.
Precedent	We are of opinion that the President can refuse to give his assent when a Bill for  amendment of the Constitution is presented to him, the result being that the Bill altogether falls, for there is no specific provision for anything further to be done about the Bill in Art.
Precedent	368 as there is in Art.
Precedent	111.
Precedent	We may in this connection refer to the different language used in clause 5 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution which says that "a Bill containing a proposal for the amendment of this Constitution shall be signed by the President forthwith upon his being satisfied that the provisions of this Article have been complied with in respect thereof".
Precedent	It will be seen therefore that if the intention under Art.
Precedent	368 had been that the President cannot withhold his assent, we would have found language similar in terms to that in clause 5 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution.
Precedent	109.
Precedent	We thus see that in one respect at any rate Art.
Precedent	368 even on its present terms differs from the power of the President in connection with ordinary legislation under the Constitution and that is if the President withholds his assen t the Bill for amendment of the Constitution immediately falls.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot accept that the procedure provided under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	111 can apply in such a case, for this much cannot be disputed that so far as the procedure provided for amendment of the Constitution is concerned we must look to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 only and nothing else.
RatioOfTheDecision	In any case the mere fact that the procedure in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is very much assimilated to the procedure for passing ordinary legislation is no reason for holding that what emerges after the procedure under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is followed is ordinary law and no more.
RatioOfTheDecision	We repeat that we must look at the quality and nature of what is done under Art.
Precedent	368 , and that is, the amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	If we look at that we must hold that what emerges is not ordinary law passed under the Constitution but something which has the effect of amending the fundamental law itself which could not be done by ordinary legislative process under the Constitution unless there is express provision to that effect.
Precedent	We h ave already referred to such express provisions in various Articles, but Art.
Precedent	368 cannot be treated as such an Article, for it deals specifically with the amendment of the Constitution as a whole.
Precedent	110.
Precedent	It is also remarkable to note in this connection that the word "law" which has been used in so many Articles of the Constitution has been avoided apparently with great care in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	We again refer to the concluding words of the main part of Art.
Precedent	368 which says that the "Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill".
RatioOfTheDecision	Now it is well-known that in the case of ordinary legislation as soon as the Bill is passed by both <ORG> and has received the assent of the President it becomes an Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	But Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 provides that as soon as the Bill for amendment of the Constitution has been passed in accordance with the procedure provided therein the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	These words in our opinion have significance of their own.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also rem arkable that these words clearly show the difference between the quality of what emerges after the procedure under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is followed and what happens when ordinary law-making procedure is followed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	111 , in the case of ordinary law-making when a Bill is passed by the two <ORG> it is presented to the President and the President shall declare either that he assents to the Bill or that he withholds assent therefrom.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it is remarkable that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	111 does not provide that when the Bill has been assented to by the President it becomes an Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason for this is that the Bill assented to by the President though it may become law is still not declared by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	111 to be a law, for such law is open to challenge in courts on various grounds, namely, on the ground that it violates any fundamental rights, or on the ground that parliament was not competent to pass it or on the ground that it is in breach of any provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand we find that when a Bill for  the amendment of the Constitution is passed by requisite majority and assented to by the President, the Constitution itself declares that the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thereafter what courts can see is whether the procedure provided in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 has been followed, for if that is not done, the Constitution cannot stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	But if the procedure has been followed, the Constitution stands amended, and there is no question of testing the amendment of the Constitution thereafter on the anvil of fundamental rights or in any other way as in the case of ordinary legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of all this we have no doubt that even though by accident the procedure provided in the Constitution for amendment thereof is very akin to the procedure for passing ordinary legislation, the power contained in Art.
Precedent	368 is still not ordinary legislative power but constituent power for the specific purpose of amendment of the Constitution; and it is  the quality of that power which determines the nature of what emerges after the procedure in Art.
Precedent	368 has been followed and what thus emerges is not ordinary legislation but fundamental law which cannot be tested, for example, under Art.
Precedent	13(2) of the Constitution or under any other provision of the Constitution.
Precedent	111.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may briefly refer to an argument on behalf of <ORG> that the amending power contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is the same sovereign power which was possessed by <ORG> when it made the Constitution and therefore it is not subject to any fetters of any kind.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not think it necessary to enter into the academic question as to where sovereignty resides and whether legal sovereignty is in the people and political sovereignty in the body which has the power to amend the Constitution and vice versa.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our view the words of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 clearly confer the power to amend the Constitution and also provide the procedure for doing so, and that in our opinion is enough for the purpose  of deciding whether the Seventeenth Amendment is valid or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further as we have already stated, the power conferred under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is constituent power to change the fundamental law i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	the Constitution, and is distinct and different from the ordinary legislative power conferred on <ORG> by various other provisions in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	So long as this distinction is kept in mind <ORG> would have the power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 to amend the Constitution and what <ORG> does under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is not ordinary law-making which is subject to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) or any other Article of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is the extent of the power conferred on <ORG> and whether there are any limitations on it - express or implied - will be considered by us presently.
RatioOfTheDecision	But we have no doubt, without entering into the question of sovereignty and of whether Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 confers the same sovereign power on <ORG> as <ORG> had when framing the Constitution, that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 does confer power on <ORG> subject t o the procedure provided therein for amendment of any provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	112.
RatioOfTheDecision	This bring us to the scope and extent of the power conferred for amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 only gives power to amend the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Recourse is had on behalf of the petitioners to the dictionary meaning of the word "amendment".
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that amendment implies and means improvement in detail and cannot take in any change in the basic features of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference in this connection may be made to the following meaning of the word "amend" in <ORG>, namely, "to make professed improvements in a measure before <ORG>; formally, to alter in detail, though practically it may be to alter its principle, so as to thwart it".
RatioOfTheDecision	This meaning at any rate does not support the case of the petitioners that amendment merely means such change as results in improvement in detail.
RatioOfTheDecision	It shows that in law, though amendment may professedly be intended to make improvements and to  alter only in detail, in reality, it may make a radical change in the provision which is amended.
