RatioOfTheDecision	In any case, as was pointed out in <NAME> case , the word "amend" or "amendment" is well understood in law and will certainly include any change whether by way of addition or alteration or deletion of any provision in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no reason to suppose that when the word "amendment" of the Constitution was being used in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , the intention was to give any meaning less than what we have stated above.
RatioOfTheDecision	To say that "amendment" in law only means a change which results in improvement would make amendments impossible, for what is improvement of an existing law is a matter of opinion and what, for example, the legislature may consider an improvement may not be so considered by others.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore in our opinion impossible to introduce in the concept of amendment as used in Art.
Precedent	368 any idea of improvement as to details of the Constitution.
Precedent	The word "amendment" used in Art.
Precedent	368 must therefor e be given its full meaning as used in law and that means that by amendment an existing Constitution or law can be changed, and this change can take the form either of addition to the existing provisions, or alteration of existing provisions and their substitution by others or deletion of certain provisions altogether.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection reference has been made to contrast certain other provisions of the Constitution, where, for example, the word "amend" has been followed by such words as "by way of addition, variance or repeal" (see Sixth Schedule, paragraph 21) and more or less similar expressions in other Articles of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is very difficult to say why this was done.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the fact that no such words appear in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 does not in our mind make any difference, for the meaning of the word "amendment" in law is clearly as indicated above by us and the presence or absence of explanatory words of the nature indicated above do not in our opinion make any difference.
RatioOfTheDecision	113.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question whether the po wer of amendment given by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 also includes the power to abrogate the Constitution completely and to replace it by an entire new Constitution, does not really arise in the present case, for the Seventeenth Amendment has not done any such thing and need not be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is enough to say that it may be open to doubt whether the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 goes to the extent of completely abrogating the present Constitution and substituting it by an entirely new one.
RatioOfTheDecision	But short of that, we are of opinion that the power to amend includes the power to add any provision to the Constitution, to alter any provision and substitute any other provision in its place and to delete any provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Seventeenth Amendment is merely in exercise of the power of amendment as indicated above and cannot be struck down on the ground that it goes beyond the power conferred on <ORG> to amend the Constitution by Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	114.
Precedent	The next question that arises is whether there is any limitation on the power  of amendment as explained by us above.
Precedent	Limitations may be of two kinds, namely, express or implied.
Precedent	So far as express limitations are concerned, there are none such in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	When it speaks of the "amendment of this Constitution" it obviously and clearly refers to amendment of any provision thereof, including the provisions contained in Part III relating to fundamental rights.
Precedent	Whether Art.
Precedent	13(2) is an express limitation on the power of amendment will be considered by us later, but so far as Art.
Precedent	368 is concerned there are no limitations whatsoever in the matter of substance on the amending power and any provision of the Constitution, be it in Part III and any other Part, can be amended under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	115.
Precedent	The next question is whether there are any implied limitations on the power of amendment contained in Art.
Precedent	368 , and this brings us to the argument and there are certain basic features of the Constitution which cannot be amended at all and there is an implied limitation on the power of amendment conta ined in Art.
Precedent	368 so far as these basic features are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may in this connection refer to the view prevailing amongst jurists in the United States of America as to whether there are any implied limitations on the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are two lines of thought in this matter in the United States.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some jurists take the view that there are certain implied limitations on the power to amend contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	These are said to be with respect to certain basic features, like, the republican character of Government, the federal structure etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, it appears that the more prevalent view amongst jurists in the United States is that there are no implied limitations on the scope of the amending power in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> on the Constitutional Law of the United States of America (1936 Edition) says that probably the correct position is that the amending power embraces everything; in other words there a re no legal limitations whatever on the power of amendment, except what is expressly provided in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V : (see discussion on pp.
RatioOfTheDecision	122 to 127).
RatioOfTheDecision	Even with respect to these express limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in The Government of the United States (Fifth Edition) at p. 77 says that even these express limitations can be removed and one of the ways of doing so is "to remove the exception by a preliminary amendment and thus clear the way for further action".
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides, as a matter of fact there is no decision of <ORG> holding that there are implied limitations on the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution and all amendments so far made in the United States have been upheld by <ORG> there in the few cases that have been taken to it for testing the validity of the amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	116.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have given careful consideration to the argument that certain basic features of our Constitution cannot be amended under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and have come to the conclusion that no limitati ons can be and should be implied upon the power of amendment under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	One reason for coming to this conclusion is that if we were to accept that certain basic features of the Constitution cannot be amended under Art.
Precedent	368 , it will lead to the position that any amendment made to any Article of the Constitution would be liable to challenge before courts on the ground that it amounts to amendment of a basic feature.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> would thus never be able to know what amendments it can make in the Constitution and what it cannot; for, till a complete catalogue of basic features of the Constitution is available, it would be impossible to make any amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 with any certainty that it would be upheld by courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	If such an implied limitation were to be put on the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it would only be the court which would have the power to decide what are basic features of the Constitution and then to declare whether a particular amendment is valid or not on the ground that it  amends a particular basic feature or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result would be that every amendment made in the Constitution would provide a harvest of legal wrangles so much so that <ORG> may never know what provisions can be amended and what cannot.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to amend being a constituent power cannot in our opinion for these reasons be held subject to any implied limitations thereon on the ground that certain basic features of the Constitution cannot be amended.
Precedent	We fail to see why if there was any intention to make any part of the Constitution unamendable, <ORG> failed to indicate it expressly in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	If, for example, the Constitution-makers intended certain provisions in the Constitution, and Part III in particular, to be not amendable, we can see no reason why it was not so stated in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	On the clear words of Art.
Precedent	368 which provides for amendment of the Constitution which means any provision thereof, we cannot infer any implied limitations on the power of amendment of any provision of t he Constitution, be it basic or otherwise.
Precedent	Our conclusion is that constituent power, like that contained in Art.
Precedent	368 , can only be subject to express limitations and not to any implied limitations so far as substance of the amendments are concerned and in the absence of anything in Art.
Precedent	368 making any provision of the Constitution unamendable, it must be held that the power to amend in Art.
Precedent	368 reaches every provision of the Constitution and can be used to amend any provision thereof, provided the procedure indicated in Art.
Precedent	368 is followed.
Precedent	117.
Precedent	Copious reference were made during the course of arguments to debates in <ORG> and it is urged that it is open to this <ORG> to look into the debates in order to interpret Art.
Precedent	368 to find out the intention of the Constitution-makers.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are of opinion that we cannot and should not look into the debates that took place in <ORG> to determine the interpretation of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and the scope and extent of the provision contained therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be c onceded that historical background and perhaps what was accepted or what was rejected by <ORG> while the Constitution was being framed, may be taken into account in finding out the scope and extent of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	But we have no doubt that what was spoken in the debates in <ORG> cannot and should not be looked into in order to interpret Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	Craies on Statute Law (Sixth Edition) at p. 128 says that "it is not permissible in discussing the meaning of an obscure enactment, to refer to 'parliamentary history' of a statute, in the sense of the debates which took place in <ORG> when the statute was under consideration", and support his view with reference to a large number of English cases.
Precedent	The same is the view in <NAME> on Interpretation of Statutes, (11th Edition) p. 26.
Precedent	<NAME> on Statutory Construction (1940 Edition) at p. 340 says that resort may not be had to debates to ascertain legislative intent, though historical background in which the legislation came to b e passed, can be taken into consideration.
Precedent	118.
Precedent	In Administrator General of Bengal v. <NAME> ([1895] 22 I.A.107), <ORG> held that "proceedings of the legislature cannot be referred to as legitimate aids to the construction of the Act in which they result".
Precedent	119.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. Commissioner of Taxation ([1907] 4 C.L.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	1087), it was said that reference to historical facts can be made in order to interpret a statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was however no reference to the debates in order to arrive at the meaning of a particular provision of the Constitution there in dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	120.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1857950> ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	88), <NAME> referring to the debates and reports of <ORG> in respect of the words of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 observed at p. 110 that they might not be read to control the meaning of the Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that case all that was accepted was that "due process of law" which was a term used in the U.S. Constitution, was not accepted for the purpose of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 which used the word " the procedure established by law".
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> (at p. 202) also refused to look at the debates and particularly the speeches made in order to determine the meaning of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21.
Precedent	<NAME> (at p. 158) was of opinion that the proceedings and discussions in <ORG> were not relevant for the purpose of construing the expressions used in Art.
Precedent	21.
Precedent	121.
Precedent	Again in <CITATION_304499> , this Court looked into the historical background but refused to look into the debates in order to determine the meaning of the provisions of the Constitution in dispute in that case.
Precedent	122.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are therefore of opinion that it is not possible to read the speeches made in <ORG> in order to interpret Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 or to define its extent and scope and to determine what it takes in and what it does not.
RatioOfTheDecision	As to the historical facts, namely, what was accepted or what was avoided in <ORG> in connection with Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it is enough to say that we have not been able to find any help from t he material relating to this.
RatioOfTheDecision	There were proposals for restricting the power of amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and making fundamental rights immune therefrom and there were counter proposals before <ORG> for making the power of amendment all-embracing.
RatioOfTheDecision	They were all either dropped or negatived and in the circumstances are of no help in determining the interpretation of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 which must be interpreted on the words thereof as they finally found place in the Constitution, and on those words we have no doubt that there are no implied limitations of any kind on the power to amend given therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	123.
RatioOfTheDecision	An argument is also raised that limitations on the power to amend the Constitution can be found in the preamble to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	As to that we may refer only to in re : the Berubari Union and Exchange of Enclaves , with respect to the value of the preamble to the Constitution and its importance therein.
Precedent	It was observed in that case unanimously by a Bench of nine Judges that "although it may be corr ect to describe the preamble as a key to the mind of the Constitution-makers, it forms no part of the Constitution and cannot be regarded as the source of any substantive power which the body of the Constitution alone can confer on the <ORG>, expressly or by implication.
Precedent	This is equally true to prohibitions and limitations".
Precedent	The Court there was considering whether the preamble could in any way limit the power of <ORG> to cede any part of the national territory and held that it was not correct to say that "the preamble could in any way limit the power of <ORG> to cede parts of the national territory".
Precedent	On a parity of reasoning we are of opinion that the preamble cannot prohibit or control in any way or impose any implied prohibitions or limitations on the power to amend the Constitution contained in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	124.
Precedent	This brings us to the question whether the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) includes an amendment of the Constitution, and therefore, there is an express provision in Art.
Precedent	13(2) which at leas t limits the power of amendment under Art.
Precedent	368 to this extent that by such amendment fundamental rights guaranteed by Part III cannot be taken away or abridged.
Precedent	We have already pointed out that in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), as well as <NAME> case , it has already been held, in one case unanimously and in the other by majority, that the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) does not include an amendment of the Constitution, and it is the correctness of this view which is being impugned before this Bench, Article 13 is in three parts.
Precedent	The first part lays down that "all laws in force in the territory of India immediately before the commencement of this Constitution, insofar as they are inconsistent with the provisions of this Part, shall, to the extent of such inconsistency, be void".
Precedent	Further all previous constitutional provisions were repealed by Art.
Precedent	395 which provided that "the Indian Independence Act, 1947, and the Government of India Act, 1935, together with all enactments amending or supplementing the  latter Act, but not including the Abolition of Privy Council Jurisdiction Act, 1949, are hereby repealed".
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus it is clear that the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(1 ), does not include any law in the nature of a constitutional provision, for no such law remained after the repeal in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	395.
RatioOfTheDecision	125.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then comes the second part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13 , which says that "the <ORG> shall not make any law which takes away or abridges the rights conferred by this Part and any law made in contravention of this clause shall, to the extent of the contravention, be void".
RatioOfTheDecision	The third part defines the word "law" for the purpose of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13; the definition is inclusive and not exhaustive.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is because of the definition in clause (3) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13 being inclusive that it is urged that the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) includes an amendment of the Constitution also.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now we see no reason why if the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(1) relating to past laws does not include any constitutional provision the word "law" in clause (2) would take in an amendment of the Con stitution, for it would be reasonable to read the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) includes an amendment of the 13.
RatioOfTheDecision	But apart from this consideration, we are of opinion that the word "law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) could never have been intended to take in an amendment of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	What Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) means is that a law made under the constitutional provisions would be tested on the anvil of Part III and if it takes away or abridges rights conferred by Part III it would be void to the extent of the contravention.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are many Articles in the Constitution which provides directly for making law in addition to Articles 245, 246, 248, etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	and the three Lists and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) prohibits the <ORG> from making any law under these provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	We see no difficulty in the circumstances in holding that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) when it talks of the <ORG> making any law, refers to the law made under the provisions contained in Ch.
RatioOfTheDecision	I of Part XI of the Constitution beginning with Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 and also other provisions already referred to earlier.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 2 46 provides that <ORG> may make laws for the whole or any part of the territory of India and the legislature of a <ORG> may make laws for the whole or any part of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 246(1) gives exclusive power to <ORG> to make laws with respect to subjects enumerated in List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 246(3) gives exclusive power to <ORG> legislatures to make laws with respect to List II.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 248(1) gives exclusive power to <ORG> to make laws with respect to any matter nor enumerated in the Concurrent List or the <ORG> List.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are referring to these provisions merely to show that the various provisions in Chapter I of Part XI provide for making laws, and these laws are all laws which are made under the legislative power conferred on <ORG> or on the <ORG> legislatures under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore when in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) it is said that the <ORG> shall not make any law (<ORG> there including <ORG> and legislature of each <ORG>), its meaning could only take in laws made by <ORG> and <ORG> leg islatures under the powers conferred under Chapter I Part XI and also other provisions already referred to earlier.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have already held that the power to amend the Constitution is to be found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 along with the procedure and that such power is not to be found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read item 97 of List I.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore an amendment of the Constitution is not an ordinary law made under the powers conferred under Chapter I of Part XI of the Constitution and cannot be subject to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) where the word "law" must be read as meaning law made under the ordinary legislative power.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have already referred to a larger number of Articles where <ORG> is given the power to make law with respect to those Articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as this power of <ORG> is concerned it is ordinary legislative power and it will certainly be subject to Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	But there can in our opinion be no doubt that when Art.
Precedent	13(2) prohibits the <ORG> from making any law which takes away or abridges rights conferred by Part III, it is only referring  to ordinary legislative power conferred on <ORG> and legislatures of States and cannot have any reference to the constituent power for amendment of the Constitution contained in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	126.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have already pointed out that there are no implied limitations on the power to amend under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and it is open to <ORG> under that Article to amend any part of the Constitution, including Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is worth remembering that a whole Part XX is devoted by the Constitution-makers to the subject of amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	If it was their intention that Part III of the Constitution will not be liable to amendment by way of abridgement or abrogation under the amending power contained in Art.
Precedent	368 we see no reason why an express provision to that effect was not made in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	We cannot see what prevented <ORG> from making that clear by an express provision in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It is however said that it was not necessary to say so in Art.
Precedent	368 , because the provision was already made i n Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	We are unable to accept this contention, for we have no doubt that Art.
Precedent	13(2 ), when it refers to making of laws is only referring to the ordinary legislative power and not to the constituent power which results in amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	In any case it seems to us somewhat contradictory that in Art.
Precedent	368 power should have been given to amend any provision of the Constitution without any limitations but indirectly that power is limited by using words of doubtful import in Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	It is remarkable that in Art.
Precedent	13(2) there is no express provision that amendment of the Constitution, under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , would be subject thereto.
RatioOfTheDecision	It seems strange indeed that no express provision was made in Part XX in this matter and even in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) no express provision is made to this effect, and in both places the matter is left in a state of uncertainty.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also remarkable that in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 the word "law", which we find so often used in so many Articles of the Constitution is conspicuously avoided, and  it is specifically provided that after the procedure has been gone through the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	This language of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is very significant and clearly makes a distinction between a constitutional Amendment and an ordinary law passed as an Amending Act .
RatioOfTheDecision	The validity of law has to be determined at the time when the Bill actually matures into an Act and not at the stage while it is still a Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provision in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 has the effect that when a Bill amending the Constitution receives the assent of the President, the Constitution stands amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution thus stands amended in terms of the Bill if the Bill has been introduced, passed and assented to by the President in accordance with the procedure laid down in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and not as a result of the Bill becoming an Amendment Act introducing amendment in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provision that the Constitution shall stand amended in terms of the Bill was t hus clearly intended to indicate that the amendment of the Constitution is not dependent on the Bill being treated as a law or an Act duly passed by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus it is clear that by indicating that the Constitution is to stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill, Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 clearly envisages that the power of amendment of the Constitution stands on an entirely different footing from an ordinary law made by <ORG> in exercise of its legislative power.
RatioOfTheDecision	127.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we keep in mind this difference between a constitutional amendment or constitutional law and an ordinary amending Act or law, it should not be difficult to hold that when Art 13(2) speaks of the <ORG> making a law, it is referring to ordinary law made under the powers conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	reads with various Lists and various provisions of the Constitution where express provision to that effect has been made and is not referring to the amendment of the Constitution which is made under the constituent power.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once it is held that th e power to amend is found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and is not to be found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 read with item 97 of List I, it must follow that the power to amend the Constitution under Art.
Precedent	368 is a different power (namely, constituent power) and when Art.
Precedent	13(2) speaks of making law, it can only refer to making ordinary law, particularly when we compare the words of Art.
Precedent	13(2) (namely , the <ORG> shall not make any law) and the words of Arts.
Precedent	245, 248, and 250 (which all speak of <ORG> making law, <ORG>-legislatures making law, and so on).
Precedent	128.
Precedent	Lastly, as the power to amend is in Art.
Precedent	368 and of the words, as they stand in that Article, that power is unfettered and includes the power to amend Part III, it is strange that power should be limited by putting an interpretation on the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2 ), which would include constitutional law also.
Precedent	There is nothing to suggest this even in the inclusive definition of the words "law" and "laws in force" in Art.
Precedent	13(3).
Precedent	Besides it is conceded on behalf of the petitioners that  Art.
Precedent	368 gives power to amend Part III, but that power is only to amend one way, namely, towards enlargement of the rights contained therein, and not the other way, namely, for abridging or taking away the rights contained therein.
Precedent	We must say that it would require a very clear provision in the Constitution to read the power to amend the Constitution relating to Part III in this manner.
Precedent	We cannot find that clear provision in Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	We repeat that what <ORG> was taking the trouble of providing a whole Part for amendment of the Constitution and when the words in Art.
Precedent	368 clearly give the power to amend the Constitution and are subject to no implied limitations and contain no express unitations, it is strange indeed that it should have omitted to provide in that very Article that Part III is not liable to amendment thereunder.
Precedent	In any case if the power of amendment conferred to the words of Art.
Precedent	368 is unfettered, we must avoid any inconsistency between that power and the provision contai ned in Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	We avoid that in keeping with the unfettered power in Art.
Precedent	368 by reading the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) as meaning law passed under ordinary legislative power and thus not including an amendment of the Constitution therein.
Precedent	The words in Art.
Precedent	13(2) are in our opinion not specific and clear enough to take in the power of amendment under Art.
Precedent	368 and must be confined only to the power of ordinary law-making contained in Arts.
Precedent	245 etc., and other provisions of the Constitution read with various Lists.
Precedent	We have therefore no hesitation in agreeing with the view taken in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), which was upheld by the majority in <NAME> case .
Precedent	129.
Precedent	The next argument is that action under the proviso to Art.
Precedent	368 is necessary as the Seventeenth Amendment affects the power of <ORG> contained in Art.
Precedent	226.
RulingByLowerCourt	It is said that by including various Acts in the Ninth Schedule and making them immune from challenge under the provisions contained in Part III, the power of <ORG> t under Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	226 is affected inasmuch as <ORG> cannot strike down any of the Acts included in the Ninth Schedule on the ground that they take away or abridge the rights conferred by Part III.
Precedent	So it is said that there has been a change in Art.
Precedent	226 and it was necessary that the Seventeenth Amendment should have been ratified by more than half the States under the proviso.
Precedent	A similar argument was raised in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), and was turned down unanimously.
Precedent	The same argument was again raised in <NAME> case , and was also turned down.
Precedent	Now ratification is required under the proviso if the amendment seeks to make any change in various provisions mentioned therein and one such provision is Art.
Precedent	226.
Precedent	The question therefore is whether the Seventeenth Amendment makes any change in Art.
Precedent	226 and whether this change has to be a direct change in the words of Art.
Precedent	226 or whether merely because there may be some effect by the Seventeenth Amendment on the content of the power in Art.
Precedent	226 it wi ll amount to change in Art.
Precedent	226.
Precedent	We are of opinion that when the proviso lays down that there must be ratification when there is any change in the entrenched provisions, including Art.
Precedent	226 , it means that there must be actual change in the terms of the provision concerned.
Precedent	If there is no actual change directly in the entrenched provision, no ratification is required, even if any amendment of any other provision of the Constitution may have some effect Indirectly on the entrenched provisions mentioned in the proviso.
Precedent	But it is urged that there may be such a change in some other provision as would seriously affect an entrenched provision, and in such a case ratification should be necessary.
Precedent	This argument was also dealt with in the majority judgment in <NAME> case , where the doctrine of pith and substance was applied and it was held that where the amendment in any other Article so affects the entrenched Article as to amount to an amendment therein, then ratification may be necessary, even though the ent renched Article may not be directly touched.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps the use of the doctrine of pith and substance in such a case is not quite apt.
RatioOfTheDecision	But what was meant in <NAME> case , was that if there is such an amendment of an unentrenched Article that it will directly affect an entrenched Article and necessitate a change therein, then recourse must be had to ratification under the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may illustrate this by two examples.
RulingByPresentCourt	Article 226 lays down inter alia that <ORG> shall have power to issue writs for the enforcement of any of the rights conferred by Part III and for any other purpose.
RulingByPresentCourt	Now assume that Part III is completely deleted by amendment of the Constitution.
RulingByPresentCourt	If that takes place, it will necessitate an amendment of Art.
RulingByPresentCourt	226 also and deletion therefrom of the words "for the enforcement of any of the rights conferred, by Part III".
RatioOfTheDecision	We have no doubt that if such a contingency ever happens and Part III is completely deleted, <ORG> will amend Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 also and that will necessitate ratificatio n under the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	But suppose <ORG> merely deletes Part III and does not make the necessary consequential amendment in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 , it can then be said that deletion of Part III necessitates change in Art.
Precedent	226 also, and therefore in such a case ratification is necessary, even though <ORG> may not have in fact provided for amendment of Art.
Precedent	226.
Precedent	130.
Precedent	Take another example.
Precedent	Article 54 is an entrenched Article and provides for the election of the President.
Precedent	So is Art.
Precedent	55 which provides for the manner of election.
Precedent	Article 52 which lays down that there shall be a President is on the other hand not an entrenched Article.
Precedent	It is said that Art.
Precedent	52 may be altered and something else may be substituted in its place and that would not require ratification in terms as Art.
Precedent	52 is not among the entrenched Articles.
Precedent	But we are of opinion that if <ORG> amends Art.
Precedent	52 , it is bound to make consequential amendments in Arts.
Precedent	54 and 55 which deal with the election of the President and the manner thereof and if it  is so the entire amendment must be submitted for ratification.
Precedent	But suppose <ORG> merely amends Art.
Precedent	52 and makes no change in Arts.
Precedent	54 and 55 (a supposition which is impossible to visualise).
Precedent	In that case it would in our opinion be right to hold that Art.
Precedent	52 could not be altered by abolition of the office of the President without necessitating a change in Arts.
Precedent	54 and 55 and in such a case if Art.
Precedent	52 alone is altered by <ORG>, to abolish the office of President, it will require ratification.
Precedent	131.
Precedent	These two examples will show where alteration or deletion of an unentrenched Article would necessitate amendment of an entrenched Article, and in such a case if <ORG> takes the incredible course of amending only the unentrenched Article and not amending the entrenched Article, courts can say that ratification is necessary even for amending the unentrenched Article, for it directly necessitates a change in an entrenched Article.
Precedent	But short of that we are of opinion that merely because there is some effe ct indirectly on an entrenched Article by amendment of an unentrenched Article it is not necessary that there should be ratification in such circumstances also.
Precedent	132.
Precedent	Besides, let us consider what would happen if the argument on behalf of the petitioners is accepted that ratification necessary whenever there is even indirect effect on an entrenched Article by amending an unentrenched Article.
Precedent	Take the case of Art.
Precedent	226 itself.
Precedent	It gives power to <ORG> not only to issue writs for the enforcement of fundamental rights but to issue them for any other purpose.
Precedent	Writs have thus been issued by <ORG> for enforcing other rights conferred by ordinary laws as well as under other provisions of the Constitution, like Arts.
Precedent	301 and 311.
Precedent	On this argument if any change is made in Arts.
Precedent	301 and 311 there is bound to be an effect on Art.
Precedent	226 and therefore ratification would be necessary, even though both Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	301 and 311 are not entrenched in the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, take an ordinary law which confers certain rights  and it is amended and those rights are taken away.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 226 would be clearly affected.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before the amendment those rights may be enforced through Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 while after the amendment the rights having disappeared there can be no enforcement thereof.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, on this argument even if there is amendment of ordinary law there would be an effect on Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and it must therefore be amended every time even when ordinary law is changed and the entire procedure under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 must be gone through including ratification under the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is however said that when ordinary law is amended, rights disappear and therefore there is no question of enforcement thereof; if that is correct with respect to ordinary law, it is in our opinion equally correct with respect to the amendment of an unentrenched provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The answer given in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), to this argument was that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 remained just the same as it was before, and only a certain class of cases had been excluded from  the purview of Part III and the courts could no longer interfere, not because their powers were curtailed in any manner or to any extent, but because there would be no occasion thereafter for the exercise of their power in such cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	We respectfully agree with these observations and are of opinion that merely because there is some indirect effect on Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 it was not necessary that the Seventeenth Amendment should have been ratified by more than one half of the States.
Precedent	It is only in the extreme case, the examples of which we have given above, that an amendment of an unentrenched Article without amendment of entrenched Article might be had for want of ratification, and this is what was intended by the majority judgment in <NAME> case , when it applied the doctrine of pith and substance in these circumstances.
Precedent	The argument that ratification is necessary as Art.
Precedent	226 is indirectly affected has therefore no force and must be rejected.
Precedent	This is equally true with respect to the power of this Court under A rts.
Precedent	132 and 136.
Precedent	133.
Precedent	Then it is urged that Art.
Precedent	245 is enlarged by the Seventeenth Amendment inasmuch as State legislatures and <ORG> were freed from the control of Part III in the matter of certain laws affecting, for example, ryotwari lands, and therefore as Art.
Precedent	245 is an entrenched Article there should have been ratification under the proviso.
Precedent	The argument in our opinion is of the same type as the argument with respect to the effect on Art.
Precedent	226 and our answer is the same, namely, that there is no direct effect on Art.
Precedent	245 by the amendment and the indirect effect, if any, does not require that there should have been ratification in the present case.
Precedent	134.
Precedent	It is then urged that ratification is necessary as Art.
Precedent	31-B deals with State legislation and in any case <ORG> cannot make any law with respect to Acts which were put in the Ninth Schedule and therefore <ORG> could not amend the Constitution in the manner in which it was done by making additions in the Ninth Schedule, both for want of r atification and for want of legislative competence.
Precedent	The answer to this argument was given in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), and it was observed there that -  " Article 31-A and 31-B really seek to save a certain class of laws and certain specified laws already passed from the combined operation of Art.
Precedent	13 read with other relevant Articles of Part III.
Precedent	The new Articles being thus essentially amendments of the Constitution, <ORG> had the power of enacting them.
Precedent	That laws thus saved relate to matters covered by List II does not in any way affect the position.
Precedent	It was said that <ORG> could not validate a law which it had no power to enact.
Precedent	The proposition holds good where the validity of the impugned provision turns on whether the subject-matter, falls within or without the jurisdiction of the legislature which passed it.
Precedent	But to make a law which contravenes the Constitution, constitutionally valid is a matter of constitutional amendment and as such it falls within the exclusive power of <ORG>".
Precedent	135.
Precedent	We respectfully agree with these observations.
Precedent	They succinctly put the legal and constitutional position with respect to the validity of Arts.
Precedent	31-A and 31-B.
Precedent	It seems to us that Art.
Precedent	31-B in particular is a legislative drafting device which compendiously puts in one place amendments which would otherwise have been added to the Constitution under various Articles in Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	The laws in the Ninth Schedule have by the device of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B been excepted from the various provisions in Part III, which affected them and this exception could only be made by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The infirmity in the Acts put in the Ninth Schedule was apprehended to be a constitutional infirmity on the ground that those laws might take away or abridge rights conferred by Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a constitutional infirmity could not be cured by State legislatures in any way and could only be cured by <ORG> by constitutional amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	What <ORG> in fact did by including various Acts in the Ninth Schedule read with Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B was to amend  the various provisions in Part III, which affected these <ORG> by making them an exception to those provisions in Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	This could only be done by <ORG> under the constituent power it had under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and there was no question of the application of the proviso in such a case, for <ORG> was amending Part III only with respect to these laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	The laws had already been passed by State legislatures and it was their constitutional infirmity, if any, which was being cured by the device adopted in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B read with the Ninth Schedule, the amendment being only of the relevant provisions of Part III which was compendiously put in one place in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> could alone do it under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and there was no necessity for any ratification under the proviso, for amendment of Part III is not entrenched in the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	136.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor is there any force in the argument that <ORG> could not validate those laws by curing the constitutional infirmity because they dealt with land which is in List II of th e Seventh Schedule to the Constitution over which <ORG> Legislatures have exclusive legislative power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The laws had already been passed by <ORG> legislatures under their exclusive powers; what has been done by the Seventeenth Amendment is to cure the constitutional infirmity, if any, in these laws in relation to Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	That could only be done by <ORG> and in so doing <ORG> was not encroaching on the exclusive legislative power of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG>s had already passed the laws and all that was done by the Seventeenth Amendment was to cure any constitutional infirmity in the laws by including them in the Ninth Schedule read with Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must therefore reject the argument that the Seventeenth Amendment required ratification because laws put in the Ninth Schedule were <ORG> laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must equally reject the argument that as these laws dealt with land, which in the exclusive legislative power of <ORG> legislature.
Precedent	<ORG> could not cure the constitutional infirmity, if any, in these laws by  putting them in the Ninth Schedule.
Precedent	137.
Precedent	We now come to what may be called the argument of fear.
Precedent	It is urged that if Art.
Precedent	368 confers complete power to amend each and every provision of the Constitution - as we have held that it does - frightful consequences will follow on such an interpretation.
Precedent	If <ORG> is clothed with such a power to amend the Constitution it may proceed to do away with fundamental rights altogether, it may abolish elected legislatures, it may change the present form of Government, it may do away with the federal structure and create a unitary state instead, and so on.
Precedent	It is therefore argued that we should give a limited interpretation to the power of amendment contained in Art.
Precedent	368 , as otherwise we shall be giving power to <ORG> to destroy the Constitution itself.
Precedent	138.
Precedent	This argument is really a political argument and cannot be taken into account in interpreting Art.
Precedent	368 when its meaning to our mind is clear.
RatioOfTheDecision	But as the argument was urged with a good deal of force on behalf o f the petitioners and was met with equal force on behalf of the Union and the States, we propose to deal with it briefly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, if this argument means that <ORG> may abuse its power of amendment conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , all that need be said in reply is that mere possibility of abuse cannot result in courts withholding the power if the Constitution grants it.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well-settled so far as ordinary laws are concerned that mere possibility of abuse will not induce courts to hold that the power is not there, if the law is valid and its terms clearly confer the power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same principle in our opinion applies to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution gives a certain power and its terms are clear, there is no reason why that power should be withheld simply because of possibility of abuse.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we may say so, possibility of abuse of any power granted to any authority is always there; and if possibility of abuse is a reason for withholding the power, no power whatever can ever be conferred on any authority, be it exe cutive, legislative or even judicial.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the so-called fear of frightful consequences, which has been urged on behalf of the petitioners (if we hold, as we do, that the power to amend the Constitution is unfettered by any implied limitations), is no ground for withholding the power, for we have no reason to suppose that <ORG> on whom such power is conferred will abuse it.
Precedent	Further even if it abuses the power of constitutional amendment under Art.
Precedent	368 the check in such circumstances is not in courts but is in the people who elect members of <ORG>.
Precedent	The argument for giving a limited meaning to Art.
Precedent	368 because of possibility of abuse must therefore be rejected.
Precedent	139.
Precedent	The other aspect of this argument of fear is that we should not make the Constitution too flexible so that it may be open to the requisite majority with the requisite ratification to make changes too frequently in the Constitution.
Precedent	It is said that the Constitution is an organic document for the governance of the country and it is ex pected to endure and give stability to the institution which it provides.
Precedent	That is undoubtedly so and this is very true of a written federal Constitution.
Precedent	But a perusal of various Constitutions of the world shows that there are usually provisions for amendment of the Constitution in the Constitution itself.
Precedent	This power to amend a Constitution may be rigid or flexible in varying degrees.
Precedent	Jurists have felt that where the power to amend the Constitution is made too rigid and the people outgrow a particular Constitution and feel that it should be amended but cannot do so because of the rigidity of the Constitution, they break the Constitution, and this breaking is more often than not by violent revolution.
Precedent	It is admitted by even those writers on the United States Constitution who are of the view that there are certain basic features which cannot be amended and who would thus make the U.S. Constitution even more rigid than it is, that howsoever rigid the Constitution may be its rigidity will not stop the people from  breaking it if they have outgrown it and this breaking is, generally speaking, by violent revolution.
Precedent	So, making our Constitution rigid by putting the interpretation which the petitioners want us to put on it will not stop the frightfulness which is conjured up before us on behalf of the petitioners.
Precedent	If any thing, an interpretation which will make our Constitution rigid in the manner in which the petitioner want the amending power in Art.
Precedent	368 to be interpreted will make a violent revolution, followed by frightfulness of which the petitioners are afraid, a nearer possibility than an interpretation which will make it flexible.
Precedent	140.
Precedent	It is clear that our Constitution-makers wanted to avoid making the Constitution too rigid.
Precedent	It is equally clear that they did not want to make an amendment of the Constitution too easy.
Precedent	They preferred an intermediate course which would make the Constitution flexible and would still not allow it to be amended too easily.
Precedent	That is why Art.
Precedent	368 provides for special majorities of the tw o <ORG> for the purpose of amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	Besides it also provides for ratification by more than half the States in case of entrenched provisions in the proviso.
Precedent	Subject to these limitations, the Constitution has been made moderately flexible to allow any change when the people feel that change is necessary.
Precedent	The necessity for special majorities in each <ORG> separately and the necessity for ratification by more than half the States in certain cases appear to us to be sufficient safeguards to prevent too easy change in the Constitution without making it too rigid.
Precedent	But it is said that in the last sixteen years, a large number of amendments have been made to the Constitution and that shows that the power to amend is much too easy and should be restricted by judicial interpretation.
Precedent	Now, judicial interpretation cannot restrict the power on the basis of a political argument.
Precedent	It has to interpret the Constitution as it finds it on the basis of well-known canons of construction and on the terms of Ar t. 368 in particular.
Precedent	If on those terms it is clear - as we think it is - that power to amend is subject to no limitations except those to be expressly found in the Constitution, courts must give effect to that.
Precedent	The fact that in the last sixteen years a large number of amendments could be made and have been made is in our opinion due to the accident that one party has been returned by electors in sufficient strength to be able to command the special majorities which are required under Art.
Precedent	368 , not only at the center but also in all the States.
Precedent	It is because of this circumstance that we have had so many amendments in the course of the last sixteen years.
Precedent	But that in our opinion is no ground for limiting the clear words of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	141.
Precedent	The power of amendment contained in a written federal Constitution is a safety valve which to a large extent provides for stable growth and makes violent revolution more or less unnecessary.
Precedent	It has been said by text-book writers that the power of amendment, though it allows  for change, also makes a Constitution long-lived and stable and serves the needs of the people from time to time.
Precedent	If this power to amend is made too rigid it loses its value as a safety valve.
Precedent	The more rigid a Constitution the more likely it is that people will outgrow it and throw it over-board violently.
Precedent	On the other hand, if the Constitution is flexible (though it may not be made too easy to modify it) the power of amendment provides for stability of the Constitution itself and for ordered progress of the nation.
Precedent	If therefore there had to be a choice between giving an interpretation to Art.
Precedent	368 which would make our Constitution rigid and giving an interpretation which would make it flexible, we would prefer to make it flexible, so that it may endure for a long period of time and may, if necessary, be amended from time to time in accordance with the progress in the ideas of the people for whom it is meant.
Precedent	But we feel that it is not necessary to go to this extent, for that would be entering into the field  of politics.
Precedent	As we see the terms of Art.
Precedent	368 , we are clearly of opinion that the Constitution-makers wanted to make our Constitution reasonably flexible and that the interpretation that we have given to Art.
Precedent	368 is in consonance with the terms thereof and the intention of those who made it.
Precedent	We therefore reject the argument of fear altogether.
Precedent	142.
Precedent	This brings us to the argument of stare decisis raised on behalf of the Union of India and the States.
Precedent	The argument is put thus.
Precedent	After the decision of <ORG> invalidating the Bihar Land Reforms Act, 1950, <ORG> passed the First Amendment to the Constitution.
Precedent	That Amendment was challenged in this Court by a number of writ petitions and was upheld in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), in 1951.
Fact	That case practically stood unchallenged till <NAME> case , in 1964 after the Seventeenth Amendment was passed.
Fact	Thus in the course of these fifteen years or so a large number of State Act s were passed on the basis of the First Amendment by wh ich in particular Arts.
Fact	31-A and 31-B were introduced in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that though <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), has stood for less than 15 years there have been so many laws dealing with agrarian reforms passed on the basis of the First Amendment which was upheld by this <ORG> that the short period for which that case has stood should not stand in the way of this <ORG> acting on the principle of stare decisis.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason for this is that an agrarian revolution has taken place all over the country after the First Amendment by <ORG> laws passed on the faith of the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89).
RatioOfTheDecision	This agrarian revolution has led to millions of acres of land having changed hands and millions of new titles having been created.
Precedent	So it is urged that the unanimous decision in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), which was challenged when the Seventeenth Amendment was passed and was upheld by majority in <NAME> case , should not now be disturbed as its disturba nce would create chaos in the country, particularly in the agrarian sector which constitutes the vast majority of the population in this country.
Precedent	143.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are of opinion that there is force in this argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the period for which <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), has stood unchallenged is not long, the effects which have followed in the passing of <ORG> laws on the faith of that decision, are so overwhelming that we should not disturb the decision in that case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not disputed that millions of acres of land have changed hands and millions of new titles in agricultural lands have been created and the <ORG> laws dealing with agricultural lands have been passed in the course of the last fifteen years after the decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), have brought about an agrarian revolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Agricultural population constitutes a vast majority of the population in this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	In these circumstances it would in our opinion be wrong to hold now that <NAME> case ([1952] S.C .R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), was not correctly decided and thus disturb all that has been done during the last fifteen years and create chaos into the lives of millions of our countrymen who have benefited by these laws relating to agrarian reforms.
RatioOfTheDecision	We would in the circumstances accept the argument on behalf of the Union of India and the States that this is the fittest possible case in which the principle of stare decisis should be applied.
Precedent	On this basis also, apart from our vies that <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), was in fact rightly decided, we would not interfere with that decision now.
Precedent	144.
Precedent	But it is urged that instead of following the principle of stare decisis which would make the decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), good for all times, we should follow the doctrine of prospective over-ruling, which has been evolved by some United States courts so that everything that has been done up to now, including the Seventeenth Amendment would be held good but in future it would not be open to <ORG>  to amend Part III by taking away or abridging any of the rights conferred thereby and, if the argument as to implied limitations on the power to amend is accepted, further limit the power of <ORG> to amend what may be called basic features of the Constitution.
Precedent	We must say that we are not prepared to accept the doctrine of prospective over-ruling.
Precedent	We do not know whether this doctrine which it is urged should be applied to constitutional amendment would also be applied to amendments of ordinary laws.
Precedent	We find it difficult to visualise what would be the effect of this doctrine if it is applied to amendment of ordinary laws.
Precedent	We have so far been following in this country the well-known doctrine that courts declare law and that a declaration made by a court is the law of the land and takes effect from the date the law came into force.
Precedent	We would on principle be loath to change that well-known doctrine and supersede it by the doctrine of prospective over-ruling.
Precedent	Further it seems to us that in view of the provisio ns of Art.
Precedent	13(2) it would be impossible to apply the doctrine of prospective over-ruling in our country, particularly where a law infringes fundamental rights.
Precedent	Article 13(2) lays down that all laws taking away or abridging fundamental rights would be void to the extent of contravention.
Precedent	It has been held by this <ORG> in <CITATION_1300072> ( Supp.
Precedent	2 S.C.R.
Precedent	8), that a law made after the Constitution came into force which infringes fundamental rights is a still-born law and that the prohibition contained in Art.
Precedent	13(2) went to the root of the <ORG> power of legislation and any law made in contravention of that provision was void ab initio.
Precedent	This case has been followed in <CITATION_1718426> ( Supp.
Precedent	1 S.C.R.
Precedent	912).
Precedent	In the face of these decisions it is impossible to apply the principle of prospective over-ruling in this country so far as ordinary laws are concerned.
Precedent	Further, if the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) includes an amendment of the Constitution, the same principle will apply, for that amendment would be still-born  if it infringes any fundamental rights contained in Part III.
Precedent	In these circumstances, it would be impossible to apply the principle of prospective over-ruling to constitutional amendments also.
Precedent	On the other hand, the word "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) does not include an amendment of the Constitution, then there is no necessity of applying the principle of prospective over-ruling, for in that case unless some limitations on the power of amendment of the Constitution are implied the amendment under Art.
Precedent	368 would not be liable to be tested under Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	We are therefore unable to apply the doctrine of prospective over-ruling in the circumstances.
Precedent	Further as we are of opinion that this is the fittest possible case in which the principle of stare decisis applies, we must uphold <NAME> case ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89), for this reason also.
Precedent	145.
Precedent	Lastly we would refer to the following observations in <NAME> case , (at pp.
Precedent	947-48) with respect to over-ruling earlier judgments of this <ORG> and specially those which a re unanimous, like <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89) :-  "It is true that the Constitution does not place any restriction on our powers to review our earlier decisions or even to depart from them and there can be no doubt that in matters relating to the decision of constitutional points which have a significant impact on the fundamental rights of citizens, we would be prepared to review our earlier decisions in the interest of public good..........
Precedent	Even so, the normal principle that "judgments pronounced by this <ORG> would be final, cannot be ignored and unless considerations of a substantial and compelling character make it necessary to do so, we should be slow to doubt the correctness of previous decisions or to depart from them.
Precedent	"It is universally recognised that in regard to a large number of constitutional problems which are brought before this <ORG> for its decision, complex and difficult questions arise and on many of such questions two views are possible.
Precedent	Therefore, if one view has been taken  by this <ORG> after mature deliberation, the fact that another <ORG> is inclined to take a different view may not justify the <ORG> in reconsidering the earlier decision or in departing from it......................
Precedent	Even so, the <ORG> should be reluctant to accede to the suggestion that its earlier decisions should be light-heartedly reviewed and departed from.
Precedent	In such a case the test should be : is it absolutely necessary and essential that the question already decided should be reopened ?
Precedent	The answer to this question would depend on the nature of the infirmity alleged in the earlier decision, its impact on public good, and the validity and compelling character of the considerations urged in support of the contrary view.
Precedent	If the said decision has been followed in a large number of cases, that again is a factor which must be taken into account".
Precedent	146.
Precedent	A similar view was taken in the <CITATION_1512520> , where it was observed that -  "...........before a previous decision is pronounced to be plainly erroneous,  the <ORG> must be satisfied with a fair amount of unanimity amongst its members that a revision of the said view is fully justified".
Precedent	147.
Precedent	These principles were applied in <NAME> case and it was observed that if <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R 89), were to be overruled, "it would lead to the inevitable consequence that the amendments made in the Constitution both in 1951 and 1955 would be rendered invalid and a large number of decisions dealing with the validity of the Acts included in the Ninth Schedule which have been pronounced by different <ORG> ever since the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), was declared, would also be exposed to serious jeopardy".
Precedent	148.
Precedent	The majority in that case therefore was not in favour of reviewing <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), even so in view of the argument raised and the importance of the question it considered the arguments against that decision and came to the conclusion itself that case was rightly decided.
RatioOfTheDecision	We m ay add that besides so many cases in <ORG> there have been a large number of cases in this <ORG> to which it is unnecessary to refer where on the faith of various amendments made in the Constitution, particularly the First, the Fourth and the Sixteenth, amending fundamental rights, this <ORG> has upheld the validity of various Acts on the basis of these amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further we would be very reluctant to over-rule the unanimous decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89), or any other unanimous decision by the slender majority of one in a larger <ORG> constituted for the purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	We say this with great respect and would hold that apart from the principle of stare decisis we should not say that the unanimous judgment in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), was wrongly decided by such a slender majority in this Special <ORG>.
Precedent	149.
Precedent	We therefore hold that <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), was correctly decided and that the majority in <NAME> case , was correct in following t hat decision.
Precedent	We would follow the decision in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89), even now as in our opinion it was correctly decided.
Precedent	Following that decision we hold that the Seventeenth Amendment is good.
Precedent	150.
Precedent	In view of this decision it is unnecessary to refer to other arguments raised with respect to the two petitions challenging the Mysore Land Reforms Act.
Precedent	151.
RulingByPresentCourt	In our view therefore all the three petitions should fail and we would dismiss them.
RulingByPresentCourt	In the circumstances we would pass no order as to costs.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	152.
Precedent	In these three writ petitions, the facts of which appear in the two judgments just delivered, the validity of the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act, 1953 and the Mysore Land Reforms Act, 1953, is principally involved.
Precedent	Since these Acts are protected by the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, the validity of the constitutional amendment is also questioned.
Precedent	Therefore, a much larger field must be traversed because of the claim of the <ORG> that no part of the Consti tution from the Preamble to the Ninth Schedule, is beyond the provision for amendment contained in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	The article forms the Twentieth Part of the Constitution and is said to be a code by itself in which reposes a sovereign power, transcending anything elsewhere in the Constitution.
Precedent	The <ORG> submits that (except as stated in the article) there are no limitations on the amending power and denies that there are any implied restrictions.
Precedent	It claims, therefore, that an amendment of the Constitution or of any of its part can never be a justiciable issue if the procedure for amendment has been duly followed.
Precedent	In this claim no exception is made - the Preamble, the Fundamental Rights, the guaranteed remedy to uphold them all of them severally and together are said to be capable of being partially or wholly abrogated by an amendment.
Precedent	Looked at from this point of view the Seventeenth Amendment Act not only must be valid but also beyond the power of the courts to question.
Precedent	The petitioners, on the other hand, conten d that this is to deny the real importance and inviolability of the Fundamental Rights which the Constitution itself, through certain articles, has made paramount even to Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It is these questions which fall for consideration, before we can decide whether the two State Act s are valid or not.
Precedent	153.
Precedent	The same questions were before this <ORG> on two earlier occasions.
Precedent	They arose for the first time immediately after the Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951 was adopted and became the subject of a decision of this <ORG> reported in <CITATION_1706770> ( S.C.R.

Precedent	There <NAME> speaking for <ORG> Mukherjea.

Argument	and himself upholds the First Amendment on the grounds that the power conferred by Part XX is constituent, paramount and sovereign and is, therefore, not subject to Art.
Argument	13(2) which prohibits the making of ordinary laws tending to abridge or take away Fundamental Rights.
Argument	The questions were again before the <ORG> in <CITATION_1308308> , when th e Seventeenth Amendment was impugned.

Precedent	Three opinions were delivered : by <NAME>, C.J.

Precedent	and himself, by <NAME>, J. and by me.
Precedent	I found the reasoning in <NAME> case ( S.C.R.

Precedent	No doubt Art.
Precedent	19 by clauses numbered 2 to 6 allows a curtailment of rights in the public interest.
Precedent	This shows that Part III is not static.
Precedent	It visualises changes and progress but at the same time it preserves the individual rights.
Precedent	There is hardly any measure of reform which cannot be introduced reasonably, the guarantee of individual liberty notwithstanding.
Precedent	Even the agrarian reforms could have been partly carried out without Article 31-A and 31-B but they would have cost more to the public exchequer.

Precedent	89).

RatioOfTheDecision	.
Precedent	.
Precedent	"The Constitution gives so many assurances in Part III that it would be difficult to think that they were the playthings of a special majority.
Precedent	To hold this would mean prima facie that the most solemn parts of our Constitution stand on the same footing as any other provision and even on a less firm ground than one on which the articles mentioned in the proviso stand.
Precedent	The anomaly that Art.
Precedent	226 should be somewhat protected but not Art.
Precedent	32 must give us pause.
Precedent	Article 32 does not erect a shield against private conduct but against state conduct including the legislatures (See Art.
Precedent	12 ).
Precedent	Can the legislature take away this shield ?
Precedent	Perhaps by adopting a liberal construction of Art.
Precedent	368 one can say that.
Precedent	But I am not inclined to play a grammarian's role.
Precedent	As at present advised I can only say that the power to make amendments ought not ordinarily to be a means of escape from absolute constitutional restrictions".
Precedent	154.
Precedent	My opposition (lest one misund erstands its veridical character) appears to be cautious and even timid but this was because it was attended by an uneasy feeling that I might have missed some immanent truth beyond what was said in <NAME> case ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89).
Precedent	The arguments then were extremely brief.
Precedent	After hearing full arguments in this case, which have not added to the reasoning of the earlier cases, I am not satisfied that the reasons are cogent enough for me to accept them.
Precedent	I say it with respect that I felt then, as I do so even more strongly now, that in the two earlier cases, the result was reached by a mechanical jurisprudence in which harmonious construction was taken to mean that unless Art.
Precedent	368 itself made an exception the existence of any other provision indicative of an implied limitation on the amending power, could not be considered.
Precedent	This was really to refuse to consider any argument which did not square with the a priori view of the omnicompetence of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	Such reasoning appears to me to be a kind of doctrinair e conceptualism based on an arid textual approach supplemented by one concept that an amendment of the Constitution is not an exercise of legislative power but of constituent power and, therefore, an amendment of the Constitution is not law at all as contemplated by Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	I am reminded of the words of Justice <NAME> that "we must think things and not words".
Precedent	The true principle is that if there are two provisions in the Constitution which seem to be hostile, juridical hermeneutics requires the <ORG> to interpret them by combining them and not by destroying one with the aid of the other.
Precedent	No part in a Constitution is superior to another part unless the Constitution itself says so and there is no accession of strength to any provision by calling it a code.
Precedent	<NAME>, the great French Jurist (who helped in the making of the Code Napoleon) supplied the correct principle when he said that it is the context of the legal provisions which serves to illustrate the meaning of the different parts, so that among them  and between them there should be correspondence and harmony.
Precedent	155.
Precedent	We have two provisions to reconcile.
Precedent	Article 368 which says that the Constitution may be amended by following this and this procedure, and Art.
Precedent	13(2) which says, the <ORG> shall not make any law which takes away or abridges the rights conferred by Part III and that any law made in contravention of the clause shall, to the extent of the contravention, be void.
Precedent	The question, therefore, is : does this create any limitation upon the amending process ?
Precedent	On the answer to this question depends the solution of all the problems in this case.
Precedent	156.
Precedent	It is an error to view our Constitution as if it were a mere organisational document by which the people established the structure and the mechanism of their Government.
Precedent	Our Constitution is intended to be much more because it aims at being a social document in which the relationship of society to the individual and of Government to both and the rights of the minorities and the backward classes are clearly laid  down.
Precedent	This social document is headed by a Preamble (PREAMBLE -WF, THE PEOPLE OF INDIA, having solemnly resolved to constitute India into a SOVEREIGN DEMOCRATIC REPUBLIC and to secure all its citizens; JUSTICE, social, economical and political; LIBERTY of thought, expression, belief, faith and worship; EQUALITY of status and of opportunity; and to promote among them all; FRATERNITY assuring the dignity of the individual and the unity of Nation: IN OUR CONSTITUENT ASSEMBLY this twenty-sixth day of November, 1949, do HEREBY ADOPT, ENACT AND GIVE TO OURSELVES THIS CONSTITUTION.
Precedent	"), which epitomizes the principles on which the Government is intended to function and these principles are later expanded into Fundamental Rights in Part III and the Directive Principles of Policy in Part IV.
Precedent	The former are protected but the latter are not.
Precedent	The former represent the limits of <ORG> action and the latter are the obligations and the duties of the Government as a good and social Government.
Precedent	157.
Precedent	Why was it necessary to have  the Fundamental Rights at all and make them justiciable ?
Precedent	As we seem to be forgetting our own history so soon let me say that the answer lies there.
Precedent	<ORG> and the birth of <ORG> in 1885 were the direct result of the discriminatory treatment of the Indians in their own country.
Precedent	The demand for the guarantee of Fundamental Rights had unfortunately to be made then to a foreign rule and it appeared in the Constitution of India Bill framed by <ORG> ten years later.
Precedent	All that is valuable to an Individual in civilized society, including free speech, imprisonment only by a competent authority, free state education, etc.
Precedent	were claimed therein.
Precedent	Resolutions of the <ORG> since then reiterated this demand and the securing of Fundamental Rights in any future Constitution became one of the articles of faith.
Precedent	To cut the narration short, the main steps may only be mentioned.
Precedent	Mrs. <NAME>'s Commonwealth of India Bill 1925 with its seven fundamental rights (the pr ecursor of Art.
Precedent	19 ), the Madras Congress Resolution of 1927 - "a constitution on the basis of declaration of rights" - the Nehru Report - "it is obvious that our first care should be to have the Fundamental Rights guaranteed in a manner which will not permit their withdrawal in any circumstances" - the draft article in the Nehru Constitution - "No person shall be deprived of his liberty, nor shall his dwelling or property be entered, requisitioned or confiscated save in accordance with law" - the Independence Resolution of 26th January, 1930 - "We believe that it is the inalienable right of the Indian people, as of any other people, to have freedom and to enjoy the fruits of their toil and have the necessities of life, so that they may have full opportunities of growth" - the Karachi Resolution on Fundamental Rights, Economic and Social Change (1931), the Sapru Report (1945) which for the first time distinguished between justiciable and non-justiciable rights, the suggestion of <ORG> nstitution of <ORG>, and, lastly <ORG>, are just a few of the steps to be remembered.
Precedent	The Fundamental Rights and the Directive Principles were the result.
Precedent	158.
Precedent	Fundamental laws are needed to make a Government of laws and not of men and the Directive Principles are needed to lay down the objectives of a good Government.
Precedent	Our Constitution was not "the cause but the result of political and personal freedom".
Precedent	Since <NAME> had said that "the proclamation in a Constitution or Charter of the right to personal freedom, or indeed of any other right, gives of itself but slight security that the right has more than a nominal existence", (<NAME> : "Law of the Constitution" 10th Edn.
Precedent	p.207) provision had to be made for guaranteeing them and to make them justiciable and enforceable.
