Precedent	12, 13, 32, 136, 141, 144 and 226.
Precedent	<ORG> and finally this <ORG> have been made the <NAME> ges of whether any legislative or executive action on the part of the <ORG> considered as comprehensively as is possible, offends the Fundamental Rights and Art.
Precedent	13(2) declares that legislation which so offends is to be deemed to be void.
Precedent	It is thus that <ORG> cannot today abridge or take away a single Fundamental Right even by a unanimous vote in both the Chambers.
Precedent	But on the argument of the <ORG> it has only to change the title of the same Act to an Amendment of the Constitution Act and then a majority of the total strength and a 2/3 rds majority of the members present and voting in each <ORG> may remove not only any of the Fundamental Rights but the whole Chapter giving them.
Precedent	And this is said to be possible because of Art.
Precedent	368 and its general language which, it is claimed, makes no exception in its text and, therefore, no exception can be implied.
Precedent	It is obvious that if an Act amending the Constitution is treated as a law it must also be subject to the provisions of Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	Since the definition of  the word 'law', makes no exception a strenuous effort is made on the basis of argument and authority to establish that a constituent power does not result in a law in the ordinary sense.
Precedent	Distinction is thus made between laws made ordinarily that is to say, from day to day by ordinary majority and laws made occasionally for the amendment of the Constitution by a slightly enhanced majority.
Precedent	In our Constitution this distinction is not valid in the eye of Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	159.
Precedent	It is not essential, of course, that a difference must always exist in the procedure for the exercise of constituent and ordinary legislative power.
Precedent	One has not to go far to find the example of a country in which constitutional law as such may be made by the same agency which makes ordinary laws.
Precedent	The most outstanding example is that of England about which <NAME> observed :"  "the <ORG> has an acknowledged right to modify the Constitution; as, therefore, the Constitution may undergo perpetual changes, it does not in reality exist;  the <ORG> is at once a legislative and a constituent assembly :" (Introduction to the Study of Law of the Constitution by <NAME>, Tenth Edn.

RatioOfTheDecision	Of course, the dictum of <NAME> that the English Constitution "elle n'existe point" (it does not exist) is far from accurate.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is a vast body of constitutional laws in England which is written and statutory but it is not all found in one place and arranged as a written Constitution usually is.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Act of Settlement (1701), the Act of Union with Scotland (1707), the Act of Union with Ireland (1800), the <ORG> Act (1911), the Representation of the Peoples Acts of 1832, 1867, 1884, 1918, 1928 and 1948, the Ballot Act (1872), the Judicature Acts 1873, 1875 and 1925, the Incitement to Disaffection Act (1934), His Majesty's Declaration of Abdication Act (1936), the Regency Act (1937) and the various Acts setting up different ministries are ex amples of what will pass for constitutional law under our system (The list is taken from <NAME> : The Statute of Westminster and Dominion Status" (4th Edn) p. 8.

Precedent	What then is the real distinction between ordinary law and the law made in the exercise of constituent power ?
Precedent	I would say under the scheme of our Constitution none at all.
Precedent	This distinction has been attempted to be worked out by several authors.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not necessary to quote them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Taking the results obtained by <NAME> (Tagore Law Lectures (1924) p. 83) it may be said that the fact that a Constitution is written as a Constitution is no distinction because in Britain constitutional law is of both kinds and both parts co-exist.
RatioOfTheDecision	The test that the Constitution requires a different kind of procedure for amendment, also fails because in Britain <ORG> by a simple majority makes laws and also amends constitutional statutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our Constitution too, in spite of the claim that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is a code (whatever is meant by the word "code" here), Arts.

RatioOfTheDecision	By this method one of the legislative limbs in a <ORG> can be removed or created.

RatioOfTheDecision	368 is a code and also that any special method of amendment of the Constitution is fundamentally necessary.

RatioOfTheDecision	The next test that the courts must apply the Constitution in preference to the ordinary law may also be rejected on the analogy of the British practice.
RatioOfTheDecision	There, every statute has equal standing.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the only difference can be said to arise from the fact that constitutional laws are generally amendable under a process which in varying degrees, is more difficult or elaborate.
RatioOfTheDecision	This may give a distinct character to the law of the Constitution but it does not serve to distinguish it from the other laws of the land for purposes of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2).
RatioOfTheDecision	Another difference is that in the written constitutions the form and power of Government alone are to be found and not rules of private law as is the case with ordinary laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	But this is also not an invar iable rule.
RatioOfTheDecision	The American Constitution and our Constitution itself are outstanding examples.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are certain other differences of degree, such as that ordinary legislation may be tentative or temporary, more detailed or secondary, while the Constitution is intended to be permanent, general and primary.
RatioOfTheDecision	Because it creates limitations on the ordinary legislative power, constitutional law in a sense is fundamental law, but if the legislative and constituent processes can become one, is there any reason why the result should be regarded as law in the one case and not in the other ?
RatioOfTheDecision	On the whole, therefore, as observed in the American Jurisprudence -  "It should be noticed however that a statute and a constitution, though of unequal dignity are both laws and each rests on the will of the people.

Precedent	A Constitution is law which is intended to be for all time and is difficult to change so that it may not be subject to "impulses of majority" "temporary exc itement and popular caprice or passion".

Precedent	I agree with the authors cited before us that the power of amendment must be possessed by the <ORG>.

Precedent	A Republic must, as says <NAME> (Commentaries on the Constitution of the United <ORG>s (1883) Vol.
Precedent	III pp.

Precedent	The power is also needed to disarm opposition and prevent factions over the Constitution.
Precedent	The power, however, is not intended to be used for experiments or as an escape from restrictions against undue state action enacted in the Constitution itself.
Precedent	Nor is the power of amendment available for the purpose of removing express or implied restrictions against the <ORG>.

Precedent	Here I make a difference between Government and <ORG> which I shall explain presently.

Precedent	But there is nothing to prevent the <ORG> from limiting itself.

Precedent	I (2nd Edn.)

Precedent	Amendment can then be by a fresh constituent body.
Precedent	To attempt to do this otherwise is to attempt a revolution.
Precedent	I do not know why the word "revolution", which I have used before, should evoke in some persons an image of violence and subversion.
RatioOfTheDecision	The whole American Constitution was the result  of a bloodless revolution and in a sense so was ours.
RatioOfTheDecision	The adoption of the whole Constitution and the adoption of an amendment to the Constitution have much in common.
RatioOfTheDecision	An amendment of the Constitution has been aptly called a Constitution in little and the same question arises whether it is by a legal process or by revolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no third alternative.
RatioOfTheDecision	An amendment, which repeals the earlier Constitution, unless legal, is achieved by revolution.
Precedent	As stated in the American Jurisprudence :  "An attempt by the majority to change the fundamental law in violation of self-imposed restrictions is unconstitutional and revolutionary" (Vol.
Precedent	12, Section 25 pp.
Precedent	629-630).
Precedent	168.
Precedent	There are illegal and violent revolutions and illegal and peaceful revolutions.
Precedent	Modification of Constitution can only be by the operation of a certain number of will acting on other wills.
Precedent	The pressure runs through a broad spectrum, harsh at one end and gentle at the other.
Precedent	But whatever the pressure may be, kind or cruel, the revolution is alway s there if the change is not legal.
Precedent	The difference is one of method, not of kind.
Precedent	Political thinking starts from the few at the top and works downward more often than in the reverse direction.
Precedent	It is wrong to think that masses alone, called "the people" after <NAME>, or "the proletariate" after <NAME>, begin a revolutionary change.
Precedent	Political changes are always preceded by changes in thought in a few.
Precedent	They may be outside the Government or in it.
Precedent	It is a revolution nevertheless, if an attempt is made to alter the will of the people in an illegal manner.
Precedent	A revolution is successful only if there is consent and acquiescence and a failure if there is not.
Precedent	Courts can interfere to nullify the revolutionary change because in all cases of revolution there is infraction of existing legality.
Precedent	It is wrong to classify as revolution some thing coming from outside the Government and an illegality committed by the Government against the Constitution as evolution.
Precedent	I am mindful of the observations of Justice <NAME> that -  "We nee d education in the obvious to learn to transcend our own convictions and to leave room for much that we hold dear to be done away with short of revolution, by the orderly change of law" (The Mind and Faith of Justice Holmes p. 390).
Precedent	169.

Precedent	The protection of the Fundamental Rights is necessary so that we may not walk in fear of democracy itself.
Precedent	170.
Precedent	Having assumed the distinction between Government and <ORG> let me now explain what I mean by that distinction and what the force of Art.

Precedent	174.

Precedent	325 of the Constitution of Nicargua, which reads as follows :-  "325.

Precedent	175.
Precedent	In our Constitution the agencies of the <ORG> are controlled jointly and separately and the prohibition is against the whole force of the Sta te acting either in its executive or legislative capacity.
Precedent	The control of the <ORG> is more important than even the <ORG>.
Precedent	In modern politics run on parliamentary democracy the <ORG> attains a position of dominance over the <ORG>.
Precedent	The <ORG>, therefore, can use the <ORG> as a means of securing changes in the laws which it desires.
Precedent	It happened in Germany under <NAME>.

Precedent	176.
Precedent	This is true of our country also regarding administration and legislation.
Precedent	Fortunately, this is avoided at least in so far as the Fundamental Rights are concerned.
Precedent	Absolute, arbitrary power in defiance of Fundamental Rights exist nowhere under our Constitution, not even in the largest majority.
Precedent	The people's representatives have, of course, inalienable and undisputable right to alter, reform or abolish the Government in any manner they think fit, but the declarations of the Fundamental Rights of the citizens are the inalienable rights of the people.
Precedent	The extent of the power of the rulers at any time is measured by the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	It is wrong to think of them as rights within the <ORG>'s giving or taking.
Precedent	Our Constitution enables an individual to oppose successfully the whole community and the <ORG> and cl aim his rights.
Precedent	This is because the Fundamental Rights are so safe-guarded that within the limits set by the Constitution they are inviolate.
Precedent	The Constitution has itself said what protection has been created round the person and property of the citizens and to what extent this protection may give way to the general good.
Precedent	It is wrong to invoke the Directive Principles as if there is some antinomy between them and the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	The Directive Principles lay down the routes of State action but such action must avoid the restrictions stated in the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	Prof. <NAME> (Changing Law in Developing Countries, pp.
Precedent	88, 89), taking the constitutional amendments, as they have been in our country, considered the Directive Principles to be more potent-than the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	That they are not, is clear when one takes the Fundamental Rights with the guaranteed remedies.
Precedent	The Directive Principles are not justiciable but the Fundamental Rights are made justiciable.
Precedent	This gives a judicial control and  check over State action even within the four corners of the Directive Principles.
Precedent	It cannot be conceived that in following the Directive Principles the Fundamental Rights (say for example, the equality clause) can be ignored.
Precedent	If it is attempted, then the action is capable of being struck down.
Precedent	In the same way, if an amendment of the Constitution is law, for the reasons explained by me, such an amendment is also open to challenge under Art.
Precedent	32 , if it offends against the Fundamental Rights by abridging or taking them away.
Precedent	Of course, it is always open to better Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	A law or amendment of the Constitution would offend the Fundamental Rights only when it attempts to abridge or take them away.
Precedent	177.
Precedent	The importance of Fundamental Rights in the world of today cannot be lost sight of.
Precedent	On December 10, 1948, <ORG> adopted the Universal Declaration of Human Rights without a dissent.
Precedent	This draft was made after <ORG> had devoted 85 me etings to it.
Precedent	The Declaration represents the civil, political and religious liberties for which men have struggled through the centuries and those new social and economic rights of the Individual which the <ORG> are increasingly recognising in their Constitutions.
Precedent	Some of these were proclaimed during the French Revolution and are included in the declarations of <ORG> taking pride in the dignity and liberty of the Individual.
Precedent	They are epitomized in the Preamble, and more fully expressed in Parts III and IV of our Constitution.
Precedent	These Declarations wherever found are intended to give a key to social progress by envisaging rights to work, to education and to social insurance.
Precedent	178.
Precedent	The <ORG> of the world are now in the second stage, where Covenants are being signed on the part of the States to respect such rights.
Precedent	United <ORG> Human Rights Commission has worked to produce two drafts - one dealing with civil and political rights and the other with economic, social and cultural rights.
Precedent	The third stage is sti ll in its infancy in which it is hoped to provide for the enforcement of these rights on an international basis.
Precedent	The Regional Charter of the Human Rights under which there is established already <ORG> to investigate and report on violations of Human Rights, is a significant step in that direction.
Precedent	After 1955 <ORG> has become competent to receive complaints from individuals although the enforceability of Human Rights on an international basis is still far from being achieved.
Precedent	If one compares the Universal Declaration with Parts III and IV of our Constitution one finds remarkable similarity in the two.
Precedent	It is significant that our <ORG> was deliberating when <ORG> was deliberating on the Universal Declaration of Human Rights.
Precedent	Both are manifestos of man's inviolable and fundamental freedoms.
Precedent	179.
Precedent	While the world is anxious to secure Fundamental Rights internationally, it is a little surprising that some  intellectuals in our country, whom we may call "classes non classes" after <NAME>, think of the Directive Principles in our Constitution as if they were superior to Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	As a modern philosopher (<NAME>) said such people 'do lip service' to freedom thinking all the time in terms of social justice "with 'freedom' as a by-product".
Precedent	Therefore, in their scheme of things Fundamental Rights become only an epitheton ornans.
RatioOfTheDecision	One does not know what they believe in : the communistic millennium of <NAME> or the individualistic Utopia of <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	To them an amendment of the Fundamental Rights is permissible if it can be said to be within a scheme of a supposed socio-economic reform, however, much the danger to liberty, dignity and freedom of the Individual.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are others who hold to liberty and freedom of the Individual under all conditions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Compare the attitude of <NAME> who would have Communism provided "there was universal freedom of speech, of association, of elections and of <ORG> ent" !
RatioOfTheDecision	To such the liberty and dignity of the Individual are inviolable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of course, the liberty of the individual under our Constitution, though meant to be fundamental, is subject to such restrictions as the needs of society dictate.
Precedent	These are expressly mentioned in the Constitution itself in the hope that no further limitations would require to be imposed at any time.
Precedent	180.
Precedent	I do not for a moment suggest that the question about reasonableness, expediency or desirability of the amendments of the Constitution from a political angle is to be considered by the courts.
Precedent	But what I do say is that the possession of the necessary majority does not put any party above the constitutional limitations implicit in the Constitution.
Precedent	It is obvious that <ORG> in making the Fundamental Rights justiciable was not satisfied with reliance on the sense of self-restraint or public opinion (Sir <NAME> calls it "that great compound of folly, weakness, prejudice, wrong feeling, right feeling, obstinacy and new spaper paragraphs!")
Precedent	on which the majority in <NAME> , case does.
Precedent	This is not an argument of fear.
Precedent	The question to ask is : can a party, which enjoys 2/3rds majority today, before it loses it, amend Art.
Precedent	368 in such wise that a simple majority would be sufficient for the future amendments of the Constitution ?
Precedent	Suppose it did so, would there be any difference between the constitutional and the ordinary laws made thereafter ?
Precedent	181.
Precedent	The liberty of the Individual has to be fundamental and it has been so declared by the people.
Precedent	<ORG> today is not the constituent body as <ORG> was, but is a constituted body which must bear true allegiance to the Constitution as by law established.
Precedent	To change the Fundamental part of the Individual's liberty is a usurpation of constituent functions because they have been placed outside the scope of the power of constituted <ORG>.
Precedent	It is obvious that <ORG> need not now legislate at all.
Precedent	It has spread the umbrella of Art.
Precedent	31-B and has only to add  a clause that all legislation involving Fundamental Rights would be deemed to be within that protection hereafter.
Precedent	Thus the only palladium against legislative dictatorship may be removed by a 2/3rds majority not only in praesanti but defuturo.
Precedent	This can hardly be open to a constituted <ORG>.
Precedent	182.
RatioOfTheDecision	Having established that there is no difference between the ordinary legislative and the amending processes in so far as clause (2) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13 is concerned, because both being laws in their true character, come within the prohibition created by that clause against the <ORG> and that the Directive Principles cannot be invoked to destroy Fundamental Rights, I proceed now to examine whether the English and American precedents lay down any principle applicable to amendments of our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Britain the question whether a constitutional amendment is valid or not cannot arise because the courts are powerless.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG>ary Sovereignty under the English Constitution means that <ORG> enjoys the right to make  or unmake any law whatever and no person or body has any right to question the legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The utmost and absolute despotic power belongs to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can "make, confirm, enlarge, restrain, abrogate, repeal, revise and expand law concerning matters of all possible denominations".
RatioOfTheDecision	What <ORG> does, no authority on earth can undo.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG>, each <ORG> of <ORG>, the constituencies and the law courts have in the past claimed independent legislative powers but these claims are unfounded.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is impossible to compare the Indian <ORG> with the British <ORG> as the former concededly in the ordinary legislation, is subject to judicial review, both on the ground of competence arising from a federal structure and the existence of Fundamental Rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question of competence in the matter of amendment of the Constitution depends upon, firstly, compliance with the procedure laid down in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and, secondly, upon the question whether the process is in any manner restricted by the Fundamental  Rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such questions cannot obviously arise in the British <ORG> (<NAME> gives three supposed limitations on the power of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of these one that language has been used in Acts of <ORG> which implies that one <ORG> can make laws which cannot be touched by any subsequent <ORG>, is not true.
RatioOfTheDecision	The best examples are Act of treaties with Scotland and Ireland but these same Acts have been amended later.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> found this claim to be untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	See <NAME> 'The Law of Constitution' pp.
Precedent	64, 65).
Precedent	183.
RatioOfTheDecision	The example of the Constitution of the United States cannot also serve any purpose although the greatest amount of support was sought to be derived from the decisions of <ORG> and the institutional writings in the United States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power of amendment in the United States Constitution flows from Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V. (Article V. The <ORG>, whenever two-thirds of both houses shall deem it necessary, shall propose amendments to this Constitution, or, on the application of the legislatures o f two-thirds of the several <ORG>s, shall cal a convention for proposing amendments, which, in either case, shall be valid to all intents and purposes, as part of this Constitution, when ratified by the legislatures of three-fourths of the several <ORG>s or by conventions in three-fourths thereof, as the one or the other mode of ratification may be proposed by the <ORG>, provided that no amendment which may be made prior to the year one thousand eight hundred and eight shall in any manner affect the first and fourth clauses in the ninth section of the first article; that no <ORG>, without its consent, shall be deprived of its equal suffrage in the <ORG>").
RatioOfTheDecision	It must be noticed that the power is clearly not made equal to ordinary legislative process.
Precedent	One salient point of difference is that the President is nowhere in this scheme because his negative does not run (<NAME> v. Virginia 3 Dall.
Precedent	378).
Precedent	The amendment is thus not of the same quality as ordinary legislation.
Precedent	184.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> d States has no doubt brushed aside objections to amendments of the Constitution on the score of incompetence, but has refrained from giving any reasons.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the most important of them, which questioned the 18th Amendment, the <ORG> only stated its conclusions.
RatioOfTheDecision	After recalling the texts of the Article under which Amendments may be made and of the 18th Amendment proposed by the <ORG> in 1917 and proclaimed as ratified by the States in 1919, the <ORG> announced :  "4.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is significant, however, that at the time of the 18th Amendment, the arguments were (a) that 'amendment' was limited to the correction of error in the framing of the Constitution, (b) Article V did not comprehend the adoption of additional or supplementary pr ovisions, (c) ordinary legislation could not be embodied in the constitutional amendment, and (d) <ORG> could not propose amendment which pared the sovereign power of the <ORG>s.

Precedent	In 1939 came the case of <NAME> v. <NAME> (307 U.S. 443), which dealt with the Child Labour  Amendment.
Argument	Such a law was earlier rejected by <ORG>.

RatioOfTheDecision	Although there is no clear pronouncement, a great controversy exists whether questions of substance can ever come before the <ORG> and whether there are any implied limitations upon the amendatory power.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the cases above noted, the other articles (particularly the Bill of Rights) were not read as limitations and no limitation outside the amending clause was implied.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the two cases in which the express limitation of Equal Suffrage Clause was involved the <ORG> did not enter the question.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus the 15th and, on its strength, the 19th Amendments were upheld.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <NAME> v. <NAME> (307 U.S. 443), the political question doctrine brought the support of only four Justices and in <NAME> v. <NAME> (369 U.S. 186), the Federal <ORG>s were held to have jurisdiction to scrutinise the fairness of legislative apportionment,  under the 14th Amendment and to take steps to assure that serious inequities were wiped out.
RatioOfTheDecision	The courts have thus entered the 'political thicket'.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question of delimitation of constituencies cannot, of course, arise before courts under our Constitution because of Art.
Precedent	329.
Precedent	192.
Precedent	Baker v. Carr (369 U.S. 186), makes the <ORG> sit in judgment over the possession and distribution of political power which is an essential part of a Constitution.
Precedent	The magical formula of "political question" is losing ground and it is to be hoped that a change may be soon coming.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many of the attacks on the amendments were the result of a misunderstanding that the Constitution was a compact between States and that the allocation of powers was not to be changed at all.

Precedent	<ORG> checks there are only three.

Precedent	It will, of course, be completely out of place in a judgment to discuss the views of the several writers and so I shall confine myself to the observation of <NAME> to whom again and again counsel for the <ORG> turned either for support or inspiration.

RatioOfTheDecision	13(2).
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> brushes aside the argument that this would destroy the very concept of the <ORG> which, as Chief Justice <NAME> had said, was indestructible.

Precedent	Of course, <ORG> has not yet faced an amendment of this character and it has not yet denied jurisdiction to itself.
Precedent	In the United States the Constitution works because, as observed by <NAME>, <ORG> is allowed to do "the work of remolding the Constitution to keep it abreast with new conditions and ne w times, and to allow the agencies expressly endowed with the amending process to act only in extraordinary emergencies or when the general opinion disagrees with the opinion of <ORG>".
Precedent	In our country amendments so far have been made only with the object of negativing <ORG> decisions, but more of it later.
Precedent	195.
Precedent	I have referred to <NAME> although there are greater names than his expounding the same views.
Precedent	I have refrained from referring to the opposite view which in the words of <NAME> has been "strenuously argued by reputable writers" although <NAME> discourteously referred to them in his book.
Precedent	My reason for not doing so is plainly this.
Precedent	The process of amendment in the United States is clearly not a legislative process and there is no provision like Art.
Precedent	13(2) under which "laws" abridging or taking away Fundamental Rights can be declared void.
Precedent	Our liberal Constitution has given to the Individual all that he should have - freedom of speech, of association, of assembly, of religion , of motion and locomotion, of property and trade and profession.
Precedent	In addition it has made the <ORG> incapable of abridging or taking away these rights to the extent guaranteed, and has itself shown how far the enjoyment of those rights can be curtailed.
Precedent	It has given a guaranteed right to the person affected to move the <ORG>.
Precedent	The guarantee is worthless if the rights are capable of being taken away.
Precedent	This makes our Constitution unique and the American precedents cannot be of much assistance.
Precedent	196.
Precedent	The Advocate General of Madras relied upon <NAME> (Mannual Elementaire da Droit Constitutional (Sirey) p. 117).
Precedent	According to <NAME>, a prohibition in the Constitution against its own amendment has a political but not juridical value, and from the juridical point of view, a declaration of absolute constitutional immutability cannot be imagined.
Precedent	The constituent power being supreme, the <ORG> cannot be fettered even by itself.
Precedent	He notices, however, that the Constitution of 1791 limited the power of amendment (revision) for  a certain time and that of 1875 prohibited the alteration of the Republican form of Government.
Precedent	He thinks that this hindrance can be removed by a two step amendment.
Precedent	He concludes that the constituent of today cannot bind the nation of tomorrow and no Constitution can prohibit its amendment in all aspects.
Precedent	197.
Precedent	Of course, the French have experimented with over a dozen Constitutions, all very much alike, while the British have slowly changed their entire structure from a monarchical executive to an executive from <ORG> and have reduced the power of <ORG>.
Precedent	<NAME>, former Prime Minister of England summed up the difference to Ambassador <NAME> thus :  ".....Quand nous faisons une Revolution, nous ne detruisons pas notre maison, nous en conservons avec soin la facade, et, derriere cette facade, nous reconstruisons une nouvelle maison.
Precedent	Vous, Francais, agissez autrement; vous jetez bas le vieil edifice et vous reconstruisez la meme maison avec une autre facade et sous un nom diff erent".
Precedent	(When we make a Revolution we do not destroy an house, we save with care the facade and behind construct a new house.
Precedent	You, Frenchmen, act differently.
Precedent	You throw down the old edifice and you reconstruct the same house with a different facade and under a different name).
Precedent	198.
Precedent	<NAME> agreed that there was a lot of truth in it (Il ya du vrai dans cette boutade) (Recounted by <NAME> in the Preface to <NAME>, L'Empire Britannique, son evolution politique et constitutionnelle p. 6, quoted in whereas : The Statute of Westminster and Dominion Status, p. 9-10).
Precedent	199.
RatioOfTheDecision	But of course to a Frenchman brought up in a legal system in which the Courts do not declare even an ordinary statute to be invalid, the idea of the unconstitutionality of a constitutional amendment does not even occur.
RatioOfTheDecision	France and Belgium have created no machinery for questioning legislation and rely on moral and political sanctions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even an English lawyer and less so an American lawyer find it difficult to understand  how the legality of an amendment of the Constitution can ever be questioned.
RatioOfTheDecision	It appears to them that the procedure for the amendment being gone through there is no one to question and what emerges is the Constitution as valid as the old Constitution and just as binding.
RatioOfTheDecision	The matter, however, has to be looked at in this way.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where the Constitution is overthrown and the <ORG> lose their position under the old Constitution, they may not be able to pass on the validity of the new Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the result of a revolution pure and simple.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where the new Constitution is not accepted and the people have not acquiesced in the change and the courts under the old Constitution function, the courts can declare the new Constitution to be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps even when the people acquiesce and a new Government comes into being, the courts may still declare the new Constitution to be invalid but only if moved to do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only when the courts begin to function under the new Constitution that they cannot consider the vire s of that Constitution because then they owe their existence to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	I agree with <NAME> in these observations taken from his book.
RatioOfTheDecision	He, however, does not include amendments of the Constitution in these remarks and expressly omits them.
RatioOfTheDecision	His opinion seems to indicate that in the case of amendments courts are completely free to see that the prescribed constitutional mode of alteration is complied with and the alteration is within the permissive limits to which the Constitution wishes the amendments to go.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is true of all amendments but particularly of an amendment seeking to repeal the courts' decision and being small in dimension, leaves the courts free to consider its validity.
Precedent	The courts derive the power from the existing terms of the Constitution and the amendment fails if it seeks to overbear some existing restriction on legislation.
Precedent	200.
Precedent	What I have said does not mean that Fundamental Rights are not subject to change or modification.
Precedent	In the most inalienable of such rights a distinction must be made be tween possession of a right and its exercise.
Precedent	The first is fixed and the latter controlled by justice and necessity.
Precedent	Take for example Art.
Precedent	21 :   "No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law".
Precedent	201.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of all the rights, the right to one's life is the most valuable.
RatioOfTheDecision	This article of the Constitution, therefore, makes the right fundamental.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the inalienable right is curtailed by a murderer's conduct as viewed under law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The deprivation, when it takes place, is not of the right which was immutable but of the continued exercise of the right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Take a Directive Principle which is not enforceable at law but where the same result is reached.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to employment is a directive principle.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some countries even view it as a Fundamental Right.
RatioOfTheDecision	The exercise, however, of that right must depend upon the capacity of Society to afford employment to all and sundry.
RatioOfTheDecision	The possession of this right also cannot be confused with its exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	One right here is positive  and can be enforced although its exercise can be curtailed or taken away, the other is a right which the <ORG> must try to give but which cannot be enforced.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution permits a curtailment of the exercise of most of the Fundamental Rights by stating the limits of that curtailment.
RatioOfTheDecision	But this power does not permit the <ORG> itself, to take away or abridge the right beyond the limits set by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must also be remembered that the rights of one individual are often opposed by the rights of another individual and thus also become limitative.
Precedent	The Constitution in this way permits the Fundamental Rights to be controlled in their exercise but prohibits their erasure.
Precedent	202.
Argument	It is argued that such approach makes Society static and robs the <ORG> of its sovereignty.
Argument	It is submitted that it leaves revolution as the only alternative if change is necessary.
Precedent	This is not right.
Precedent	The whole Constitution is open to amendment.
Precedent	Only two dozen articles are outside the reach of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	That too because the Con stitution has made them fundamental.
Precedent	What is being suggested by the counsel for the <ORG> is itself a revolution because as things are that method of amendment is illegal.
Precedent	There is a legal method.
Precedent	<ORG> must act in a different way to reach the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	The <ORG> must reproduce the power which it has chosen to put under a restraint.
Precedent	Just as the French or the Japanese etc.
Precedent	cannot change the articles of their Constitution which are made free from the power of amendment and must call a convention or a constituent body, so also we in India cannot abridge or take away the Fundamental Rights by the ordinary amending process.
Precedent	<ORG> must amend Art.
Precedent	368 to convoke <ORG>, pass a law under item 97 of the First List of Schedule VII to call <ORG> and then that assembly may be able to abridge or take away the Fundamental Rights if desired.
Precedent	It cannot be done otherwise.
Precedent	The majority in <NAME> case , suggested bringing Art.
Precedent	32 under the Proviso to improve pro tection to the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	Article 32 does not stand in need of this protection.
Precedent	To abridge or take away that article (and the same is true of all other Fundamental Rights) a constituent body and not a constituted body is required.
Precedent	<ORG> today is a constituted body with powers of legislation which include amendments of the Constitution by a special majority but only so far as Art.
Precedent	13(2) allows.
Precedent	To bring into existence a constituent body is not impossible as I had ventured to suggest during the hearing and which I have now more fully explained here.
Precedent	It may be said that this is not necessary because Art.
Precedent	368 can be amended by <ORG> to confer on itself constituent powers over the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	This would be wrong and against Art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	<ORG> cannot increase its powers in this way and do indirectly which it is intended not to do directly.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> does not lose its sovereignty but as it has chosen to create self-imposed restrictions through one constituent body, those restrictions c annot be ignored by a constituted body which makes laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	Laws so made can affect those parts of the Constitution which are outside the restriction in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) but any law (legislative or amendatory) passed by such a body must conform to that article.
RatioOfTheDecision	To be able to abridge or take away the Fundamental Rights which give so many assurances and guarantees a fresh <ORG> must be convoked.
RatioOfTheDecision	Without such action the protection of the Fundamental Rights must remain immutable and any attempt to abridge or take them away in any other way must be regarded as revolutionary.
RatioOfTheDecision	203.
RatioOfTheDecision	I shall now consider the amendments of the Fundamental Rights made since the adoption of the Constitution, with a view to illustrating my meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	Part III is divided under different headings.

Precedent	This brings me to the main question in this case.
Precedent	It is : whether the amendments of the part to Property in Part III of the Constitution were legally made or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	To understand this part f the case I must first begin by discussing what property rights mean and how they were safeguarded by the Constitution as i was originally framed.
Statute	"Right to Property" in Part III was originally the subject of one article, namely, Art.
Statute	31.
Statute	Today there are three articles 31, 31-A and 31-B and the Ninth Schedule.
Statute	The original thirty-first article read :  "31.
Statute	Compulsory acquisition of property.
Statute	(1) No person shall be deprived of his property save by authority of law.
Statute	(2) No property, movable or immovable, including any interest in, or in any company owning, any commercial or industrial undertaking, shall be taken possession of or acquired for public pur poses under any law authorising the taking of such possession or such acquisition, unless the law provides for compensation for the property taken possession of or acquired and either fixes the amount of the compensation, or specifies the principles on which, and the manner in which, the compensation is to be determined and given.
Statute	(3) No such law as is referred to in clause (2) made by the <ORG> of the State shall have effect unless such law, having been reserved for the consideration of the President, has received his assent.
Statute	(4) If any Bill pending at the commencement of this Constitution in the <ORG> of a State has, after it has been passed by such <ORG>, been reserved for the consideration of the President and has received his assent, then, notwithstanding anything in this Constitution, the law so assented to shall not be called in question in any court on the ground that t contravenes the provisions of clause (2).
Statute	(5) Nothing in clause (2) shall affect -  (a) the provisions of any ex isting law other than a law to which the provisions of clause (a) apply, or  (b) the provisions of any law which the <ORG> may hereafter make -  (i) for the purpose of imposing or levying any tax or penalty, or  (ii) for the promotion of public health or the prevention of danger to life or property, or  (iii) in pursuance of any agreement entered into between <ORG> or <ORG> and the Government of any other country, or otherwise, with respect to property declared by law to be evacuee property.
Statute	(6) Any law of the <ORG> enacted nor more than eighteen months before the commencement of this Constitution may within three months from such commencement be submitted to the President for his certification; and thereupon, if the President by public notification no certifies, it shall not be called in question in any court on the ground that it contravenes the provisions of clause (2) of this article or has contravened the provisions of sub-section (2) of section 299 of  the Government of India, Act, 1935".
Statute	215.
Statute	The provisions of this article are intended to be read with Art.
Statute	19(1)(f) which reads :  "19(1) All citizens shall have the right -  ........................................  (f) to acquire, hold and dispose of property".
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 19(1)(f) is subject to clause (6) which I have already set out elsewhere and considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ownership and exchange of property are thus recognised by the article.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "property" is not defined and I shall presently consider what may be included in 'property'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whatever the nature of property, it is clear that by the first clause of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31 the right to property may be taken away under authority of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	This was subject t one condition under the original Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31 , namely, that the law must either fix the compensation for the deprivation of specify the principles on which and the manner in which compensation was to be determined and given.
RatioOfTheDecision	This was the heard of the institution of property as understood by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rest  of the article only gave constitutional support against the second clause, to legislation already on foot in the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This created a Fundamental Right in property.
Precedent	The question may now be asked : why was it necessary to make such a Fundamental Right at all ?
Precedent	216.
Precedent	There is no natural right in property and a <NAME> said in his Reflections, Government is not made in virtue of natural rights, which may and do exist in total independence of it.
Precedent	Natural rights embrace activity outside the status of citizen.
Precedent	Legal rights are required for free existence as a social being and the <ORG> undertakes to protect them.
Precedent	Fundamental Rights re those rights which the <ORG> enforces against itself.
Precedent	Looking at the matter briefly but historically, it may be said that the Greeks were not aware of the distinctions for a <NAME> (Das Deutsches Genossenschaftrecht (III, 10) points out they did not distinguish between personality as a citizen and personality as a human being.
Precedent	For them the Individual was merged in the citizen and the  citizen in the <ORG>.
Precedent	There was personal liberty and private law but there was no sharp division between the different kinds of laws.
Precedent	The Romans evolved this gradually, not when <ORG> existed, but when the notion of a Fiscus developed in the <ORG> and the legal personality of the Individual was separated from his membership of the <ORG>.
Precedent	It was then that the <ORG> began to recognize the rights of the Individual in his dealings with the <ORG>.
Precedent	It was <NAME> (De Off.
Precedent	(The Offices) II Ch.
Precedent	XXI (Everyman) p.105), who was the first to declare that the primary duty of the Governor of a <ORG> was to secure to each individual in the possession of his property.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here we may see recognition of the ownership of property as a Fundamental Right.
RatioOfTheDecision	This idea was so engrained in early social philosophy that we find <NAME> opening in his 'Civil Government' (Ch.
RatioOfTheDecision	7) that "Government has no other end but the preservation of property".
RatioOfTheDecision	The Concepts of liberty, equality and religious freedom were well-known.
Precedent	To them was  added the concept of property rights.
Precedent	Later the list included :equalitas, libertas ius securitatis, ius defensionis and ius puniendi.
Precedent	The concept of property right gained further support from <NAME> and <NAME> and <NAME> and <NAME> (<NAME> : Legal Theory (4th Edn.)
Precedent	See pp.
Precedent	373-376).
Precedent	The term property in its pristine meaning embraced only land but it soon came to mean much more.
Precedent	According to <NAME> (The Institution of Property (1936) p. 436) -  "Property is any protected right or bundle of rights (interest or thing) with direct or indirect regard to any external object (i.e.
Precedent	other than the person himself) which is material or quasi material (i.e.
Precedent	a protected process) and which the then and there organisation of Society permits to be either private or public, which is connoted by the legal concepts of occupying, possession or using".
Precedent	217.
Precedent	The right is enforced by excluding entry or interference by a person not legally entitled.
Precedent	The position of the <ORG> vis-a-vis the individual is the subject of Arts.
Precedent	19 an d 31, 31-A and 31-B.
Precedent	218.
Precedent	Now in the enjoyment, the ultimate right may be an interest which is connected to the object through a series of intermediaries in which each 'holder' from the last to the first 'holds of' 'the holder' before him.
Precedent	Time was when there was a lot of 'free property' which was open for appropriation.
Precedent	As <NAME> (The Institution of Property (1936) p. 438) puts it, "all physical manifestations capable of being detected, localised and identified" can be the objects of property.
Precedent	One exception now made by all civilized nations is that human beings are no longer appropriable.
Precedent	If any free property was available then it could be brought into possession and ownership by mere taking.
Precedent	It has been very aptly said that all private property is a system of monopolies and the right to monopolise lies at the foundation of the institution of property.
Precedent	<NAME> (<ORG>; p. 420) in classifying right in rem puts private property along with personal integrity [right against injury to life, body and health (body o r mental), personal liberty (free motion and locomotion)], Society and control of one's family and dependents.
Precedent	An extremely valuable definition of ownership is to be found in the Restatement of the Law of Property where it is said :  "It is the totality of rights as to any specific objects which are accorded by law, at any time and place, after deducting social reservations".
Precedent	219.
Precedent	This is the core from which some rights may be detached but to which they must return when liberated.
Precedent	220.
Precedent	The right to property in its primordial meaning involved the acquisition of a free object by possession and conversion of this possession into ownership by the protection of <ORG> or the ability to exclude interference.
Precedent	As the notion of a <ORG> grew, the right of property was strong or weak according to the force of political opinion backing it or the legislative support of the <ORG>.
Precedent	The English considered the right as the foundation of society.
Precedent	<NAME> (Commentaries) explained it on religious and social grounds claiming  universality for it and called it the right of the English people.
Precedent	<NAME> (Moral Philosophy), although he thought the institution paradoxical and unnatural, found it full of advantages and <NAME> in his famous diatribe against the French Revolution described it as the "sheet-anchor of society".
Precedent	This institution appeared in the Magna Carta, in the American Declaration of Independence and the French Declaration of Rights of Man.
Precedent	Later we find it in many Constitutions described as Fundamental, general and guaranteed (Under the Constitution of Norway the rights (Odels and <NAME> rights) cannot be abolished but if the <ORG> requires the owner must surrender the property and he is compensated).
Precedent	221.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution accepted the theory that Right of Property is a fundamental right.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion it was an error to place it in that category.
RatioOfTheDecision	Like the original Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	16 of the Draft Bill of the Constitution which assured freedom of trade, commerce and intercourse within the territory of India as a fundament al right but was later removed, the right of property should have been placed in a different chapter.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of all the fundamental rights it is the weakest.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in the most democratic of Constitutions, (namely, the West German Constitution of 1949) there was a provision that lands, minerals and means of production might be socialised or subjected to control.
RatioOfTheDecision	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31 , if it contemplated socialization in the same way in India should not have insisted so plainly upon payment of compensation.

Precedent	What was then the theory about Right to Property accepted by <ORG> ?

Precedent	<NAME> (<NAME> : De jure Belli ac Pacis.
Precedent	II c. 2 2 (5) 6.

Precedent	According to him, the acquired right had to give way to eminent domain (ex vi auper-eminentis domini) but there must be public interest (publicautilitas) and if possible compensation.

Precedent	251.
Precedent	My conclusions are :  (i) that the Fundamental Rights are outside the amendatory process if the amendment seeks to abridge or take away any of the rights;  (ii) that <NAME> case (and <NAME> case which followed it) conceded the power of amendment over Part III of the Constitution on an erroneous view of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) and 368;   (iii) that the First, Fourth and Seventh Amendments being part of the Constitution by acquiescence for a long time, cannot now be challenged and they contain authority for the Seventeenth Amendment;  (iv) that this <ORG> having now laid down that Fundamental Rights cannot be abridged or taken away by the exercise of amendatory process in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , any further inroad into these rights as they exist today will be illegal and unconstitutional unless it complies with Part III in general and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) in particular;  (v) that for abridging or taking away Fundamental Rights, a Constituent body will have to be convoked; and  (vi) that  the two impugned Acts, namely, the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act, 1953 (X of 1953) and the Mysore Land Reforms Act, 1961 (X of 1962) as amended by Act XIV of 1965 are valid under the Constitution not because they are included in Schedule 9 of the Constitution but because they are protected by Art.

Fact	Amendment of the Constitution Procedure for amendment of the Constitution  368.
Statute	An amendment of this Constitution may be initiated only by the introduction of a B ill for the purpose in either <ORG> of <ORG>, and when the Bill is passed in each <ORG> by a majority of the total membership of that <ORG> and by a majority of not less than two-thirds of the members of that <ORG> present and voting, it shall be presented to the President for his assent and upon such assent being given to the Bill, - the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill :  Provided that if such amendment seeks to make any change in -  (a) article 54 , article 55 , article 73 , article 162 or article 241 , or  (b) Chapter IV of Part V, Chapter V of Part VI, or Chapter I of Part XI, or  (c) any of the Lists in the Seventh Schedule, or  (d) the representation of States in <ORG>, or  (e) the provisions of this article, the amendment shall also require to be ratified by the <ORG> of not less than one-half of the States by resolutions to that effect passed by those <ORG> before the Bill making provision for such amendment is presented to the Presiden t for assent".
RatioOfTheDecision	257.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention that article 368 prescribes only the procedure of amendment cannot be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article not only prescribes the procedure but also gives the power of amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the procedure of art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is followed, the Constitution "shall stand amended" in accordance with the terms of the bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is because the power to amend is given by the article that the Constitution stands amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proviso is enacted on the assumption that the several articles mentioned in it are amendable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of the proviso is to lay down a stricter procedure for amendment of the articles which would otherwise have been amendable under the easier procedure of the main part.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no other provision in the Constitution under which these articles can be amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	258.

RatioOfTheDecision	259.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some articles are expressed to continue until provision is made by law [see articles 59(3), 65(3), 73(2), 97, 98(3), 106, 135, 142(1), 148(3), 149, 171(2), 186, 187(3), 189(3), 194(3), 195, 221(2), 283(1) and (2), 285, 313, 345, 372(1), 373].

Precedent	268.
Precedent	In Art.
Precedent	13 unless the context otherwise provides 'law' includes any ordinanc e, order, bye-law, rule, regulation, notification, custom or usage having in the territory of india the force of law [ article 13(3)(a)].

Precedent	269.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now the term 'law' in its widest and generic sense includes the Constitution and a constitutional amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in the Constitution this term is employed to designate an ordinary statue or legislative act in contradistinction to the Constitution or a constitutional amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is the basic law providing the framework of government and creating the organs for the marking of the laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	The distinction between the Constitution and the laws is so fundamental that the Constitution is not regarded as a law or a legislative act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution means the Constitution as amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	An amendment in conformity with art.
Precedent	368 is a part of the Constitution and is likewise not a law.
Precedent	270.
Precedent	The basic theory of our Constitut ion is that it cannot be changed by a law or legislative Act.
Precedent	It is because special provision is made by articles 4, 169, Fifth Schedule Part D and Sixth Schedule para 21 that some parts of the Constitution are amendable by ordinary laws.
Precedent	But by express provision no such law is deemed to be a constitutional amendment.
Precedent	Save as expressly provided in articles 4, 169, Fifth Schedule Part D and Sixth Schedule para 21, no law can amend the Constitution, and a law which purports to make such an amendment is void.
Precedent	271.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME> ([1803] 1 Cranch 137, 177 : 2 L. Ed.
Precedent	60, 73), Marshall, C.J., said :  "It is a proposition too plain to be contested, that the Constitution controls any legislative Act repugnant to it; or, that the legislature may alter the Constitution by an ordinary Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Between these alternatives there is no middle ground.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is either a superior paramount law, unchangeable by ordinary means, or it is on a level with ordinary legislative Acts, and, like other Acts, is alterable  when the legislature shall please to alter it.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the former part of the alternative be true, then a legislative Act contrary to the Constitution is not law; if the latter part be true, then written constitutions are absurd attempts, on the part of the people, to limit a power in its own nature illimitable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certainly all those who have framed written constitutions contemplate them as forming the fundamental and paramount law of the nation, and, consequently, the theory of every such government must be, that an Act of the Legislature, repugnant to the Constitution, is void.
RatioOfTheDecision	This theory is essentially attached to a written constitution, and is consequently to be considered, by this court, as one of the fundamental principles of our society".
RatioOfTheDecision	272.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is because a Constitution Amendment Act can amend the Constitution and is not a law that art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 avoids all reference to law making by the <ORG>.

Precedent	368 an ordinary law making procedure.

RatioOfTheDecision	In other respects the amending process under art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is very different from the ordinary legislative process.
RatioOfTheDecision	A constitution amendment Act must be initiated by a bill introduced for that purpose in either <ORG> of Parliament.
RatioOfTheDecision	The bill must be passed in each <ORG> by not less than two thirds of the members present and voting, the requisite quorum in each <ORG> being a majority of its total membership; and in cases coming under the proviso, the amendment must be ratified by the legislature of not less than one half of the States.
RatioOfTheDecision	Upon the  bill so passed being assented to by the President, the Constitution stands amended in accordance with the terms of the bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ordinary legislative process is much easier.
RatioOfTheDecision	A bill initiating a law may be passed by a majority of the members present and voting at a sitting of each <ORG> or at a joint sitting of the <ORG>s, the quorum for the meeting of either <ORG> being one tenth of the total number of members of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The bill so passed on being assented to by the President becomes a law.
Precedent	A bill though passed by all the members of both <ORG>s cannot take effect as a Constitution amendment Act unless it is initiated for the express purpose of amending the Constitution.
Precedent	275.
Precedent	The essence of a written Constitution is that it cannot be changed by an ordinary law.
Precedent	But most written Constitutions provide for their organic growth by constitutional amendments.
Precedent	The main method of constitutional amendments are (1) by the ordinary legislature but under certain restrictions, (2) by the people through a referendum, ( 3) by a majority of all the units of a Federal State; (4) by a special convocation see C.F.
Precedent	Strong Modern Political Institutions, 5th Edition, pp.
Precedent	133-4, 146.
Precedent	Our Constitution has by article 368 chosen the first and a combination of the first and the third methods.
Precedent	276.
Precedent	The special attributes of constitutional amendment under art.

Precedent	13(2) would include a constitutional amendment under art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	277.
Precedent	If a constitutional amendment creating a new fundamental right and incorporating it in Part III were a law, it would not be open to the <ORG> by a subsequent constitutional amendment to abrogate the new fundamental right for such an amendment would be repugnant to Part III.
Precedent	But the conclusion is absurd for the body which created the right can surely take it away by the same process.
Precedent	278.
Precedent	<NAME> relied upon a decision of the Oklaho <ORG> in <NAME> v. <NAME> (88 A.L.R.
Precedent	1068), where it was held that for some purposes the Constitution of a State was one of the laws of the State.
Precedent	But even in America, the term "law" does not ordinarily include the Constitution or a constitutional amendment.
Precedent	In this connection, I will read the following passage in Corpus Juris Secundum, Vol.
Precedent	XVI Title Constitutional Law Art.
Precedent	1 , p.20 :  The term `constitution' is ordinarily employed to designate the organic law in contradistinction to the term `law', which is generally used to designate statutes or legislative enactments.
Precedent	Accordingly, the term `law' under this distinction does not include a constitutional amendment.
Precedent	However, the term `law' may, in accordance with the context in which it is used, comprehend or included the constitution or a constitutional provision or amendment.
Precedent	A statute and a constitution, although of unequal dignity, are both `laws', and rest on the will of the people".
Precedent	279.
Precedent	In our Constitution, the expression "law" does not incl ude either the constitution or a constitutional amendment.
Precedent	For all these reasons we must hold that a constitutional amendment under art.
Precedent	368 is not a law within the meaning of art.
Precedent	13(2).
Precedent	280.

Precedent	Demands for safeguards of the rights embodied in Part III and IV may be traced to the Constitution of India Bill 1895, the Congress Resolutions between 1917 and 1919, Mrs. <NAME>'s Commonwealth of India Bill of 1925, the Report of the Nehru Committee set up under the Congress Resolution in 1927, the Congress Resolution of March 1931 and the Sapru Report of 1945.

RatioOfTheDecision	The objective of the preamble is secured not only by Part III but also by Part IV and art.

Precedent	694-5; in Re Berubari Union & exchange of Enclaves .

Precedent	It is next argued that the people of India in exercise of their sovereign power have placed the fundamental rights beyond the reach of the amending power.

Precedent	89) case that the fundamental rights are amendable.
Precedent	The power to frame the Constitution was vested in <ORG> by s. 8(1) of the Indian Independe nce Act, 1947.
Precedent	The Constitution though legal in its origin was revolutionary in character and accordingly <ORG> exercised its powers of framing the Constitution in the name of the people.
Precedent	The objective resolution of the <ORG> passed on January 22, 1947, solemnly declared that all power and authority of severing independent India, its constituent parts, and organs and the <ORG> were derived from the people.
Precedent	The permeable to the Constitution declares that the people of India adopts, enacts and gives to themselves the Constitution.
Precedent	In form and in substance the Constitution emanates from the people.
Precedent	By the Constitution the people constituted themselves into a republic.
Precedent	Under the Republic all public is derived from the people and is exercised by functionaries chosen either directly or indirectly by the people.
Precedent	The <ORG> can exercise only such powers as are delegated to it under the Constitution.
Precedent	The people acting through <ORG> reserved for themselves certain right s and liberties and ordained that they shall not be curtailed by ordinary legislation.

Precedent	289.
Precedent	It is urged that the word 'amend' imposes the limitation that an amendment must be an improvement of the Constitution.
Precedent	Reliance is placed on the dictum in Livermore v. <NAME> (102 Cal.
Precedent	113-25 L.R.A.
Precedent	312) : "On the other hand, the significance of the term 'amendment; implies such an addition or change within the lines of the original instrument as will effect an improvement, or better carry out the purpose for which it was framed".

Precedent	The decision totally negatived  the contention that "an amendment must be confined in its scope to an alteration or improvement of that which is already contained in the Constitution and cannot change its basic structure, include new grants of power to the federal Government nor relinquish in the <ORG> those which already have been granted to it", see <NAME> on Constitutional Law, Chapter III, Art.
Precedent	5. pp.

Precedent	XVI, title 'Constitutional Law, p. 26 thus : "The term 'amendment' as used in the constitutional article giving <ORG> a power of proposal includes additions to as well as corrections of, matters already treated, and there is nothing there which suggests that it is used in a restricted sense".
Precedent	290.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 368 Indicates that the term "amend" means "change".
RatioOfTheDecision	The proviso is expressed to apply to amendments which seek to make any "change" in certain articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	The main part of art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 thus gives the power to amend or to make changes in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	A change is not nece ssarily an improvement.
RatioOfTheDecision	Normally the change is made with the object of making an improvement, but the experiment may fail to achieve the purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even the plain dictionary meaning of the word "amend" does not support the contention that an amendment must take an improvement, see <ORG> where the word "amend" is defined thus : "4 to make professed improvement (in a measure before <ORG>); formally to alter in detail, though practically it may be to alter its principle so as to thwart it".

Precedent	Having heard lengthy arguments on the question I have come to the conclusion that the validity of the constitutional amendments was rightly upheld in Sri Sankari <NAME> ([1952] S.C.R.

Precedent	On November 4, 1948, he said :  "The second means adopted to avoid rigidity and legalism is the provision  for facility with which the Constitution could be amended.
Precedent	The provisions of the Constitution relating to the amendment of the Constitution divide the Articles of the Constitution into two groups.
Precedent	In the one group are placed Articles relating to (a) the distribution of legislative powers between the center and the States, (b) the representation of the States in <ORG> (c) the powers of the <ORG>.
Precedent	All other Articles are placed in another group.
Precedent	Articles placed in the second group cover a very large part of the Constitution and can be amended by <ORG> by a double majority, namely, a majority of not less than two-thirds of the members of each <ORG> present and voting and by a majority of the total membership of each <ORG>.
Precedent	The amendment of these Articles does not require ratification by the States.

Precedent	The provisions relating to amendment of the Constitution have come in for a virulent attack at the hands of the critics of the Draft Constitution.

RatioOfTheDecision	Compared to them those contained in the Draft Constitution will be found to be the simplest.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Draft Constitution has eliminated the elaborate and difficult procedures such as a decision by a convention or a referendum.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Powers of amendment are left with the Legislatures Central and Provincial.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only for amendments or specific matters - and they are only few, that the ratification of <ORG> is required.
