Precedent	975-6).
Precedent	313.
Precedent	On November 11, 1948, Pandit <NAME> said :  "And remember this, that while we want this Constitution to be as solid and as permanent a structure as we can make it, nevertheless there is no permanence in Constitutions.
Precedent	There should be a certain flexibility.
Precedent	If you make anything rigid and permanent, you stop a Nation's growth, the growth of living vital organic people.
Precedent	Therefore it has to be flexible".
Precedent	(Constituent Assembly Debates Vol.
Precedent	7, p. 322).
Precedent	314.
Precedent	The views of <NAME> echoed by <NAME> and <NAME> were more powerful expressed by <NAME> in 1791 :  "There never did, there never will, and there never can, exist a parliament, or any description of men, or any generation of men, in any country, possessed of the right or the  power of binding and controlling posterity to the 'end of time', or of commanding for ever how the world shall be governed, or who shall govern it; and therefore all such clauses, acts or declarations by which the makers of them attempt to do what they have neither the right nor the power to do, nor take power to execute, are in themselves null and void.
Precedent	Every age and generation must be as free to act for itself in all cases as the ages and generations which preceded it.
Precedent	The vanity and presumption of governing beyond the grave is the most ridiculous and insolent of all tyrannies.
Precedent	Man has no property in man; neither has any generation a property in the generations which are to follow.
Precedent	The parliament of the people of 1688 or of any other period, had no more right to dispose of the people of the present day, or to bind or to control them in any shape whatever, than the parliament or the people of the present day have to dispose of, bind or control those who are to live a hundred or a thousand years hence.
Precedent	Every  generation is, and must be, competent to all the purposes which its occasions require.
Precedent	It is the living, and not the dead, that are to be accommodated.
Precedent	When man ceases to be, his power and his wants cease with him; and having no longer any participation in the concerns of this world, he has no longer any authority in directing who shall be its governors, or how its government shall be organized, or how administered".
Precedent	(See 'Rights of Man' by <NAME>, unabridged edition by <NAME>, pp.
Precedent	3 & 4).
Precedent	315.
Precedent	For the reasons given above, I agree with <NAME>, J. that the writ petitions must be dismissed.
Precedent	316.
Precedent	In the result, the writ petitions are dismissed without costs.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	317.
Precedent	I have perused the judgment of my learned Brother <NAME>, J. and I agree with his conclusion that the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964 is legally valid, but in view of the importance of the constitutional issues raised in this case I would prefer to state my own reasons in a separate judgment.
Precedent	318.
Precedent	In these  petitions which have been filed under Art.
Precedent	32 of the Constitution a common question arises for determination, viz., whether the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964 which amends Art.
Precedent	31-A and 31-B of the Constitution is ultra vires and unconstitutional.
Precedent	319.
Precedent	The petitioners are affected either by the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act, 1954 (Act X of 1953) or by the Mysore Land Reforms Act (Act 10 of 1962) as amended by Act 14 of 1965 which were added to the 9th Schedule of the Constitution by the impugned Act and their contention is that the impugned Act being unconstitutional and invalid, the validity of the two Acts by which they are affected cannot be saved.
Precedent	320.
Precedent	The impugned Act consists of three sections.
Statute	The first section gives its short title.
Statute	Section 2(i) adds a proviso to clause (1) of Art.
Statute	31-A after the existing proviso.
Statute	This proviso reads thus :  "Provided further that where any law makes any provision for the acquisition by the State of any estate and where any land comprised there in is held by a person under his personal cultivation, it shall not be lawful for the <ORG> to acquire any portion of such land as is within the ceiling limit applicable to him under any law for the time being in force or any building or structure standing thereon or appurtenant thereto, unless the law relating to the acquisition of such land, building or structure, provides for payment of compensation at a rate which shall not be less than the market value thereof".
RatioOfTheDecision	321.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 2(ii) substitutes the following sub-clause for sub-clause (a) of clause (2) of Art.
Statute	31-A :-   "(a) the expression 'estate' shall, in relation to any local area, have the same meaning as that expression or its local equivalent has in the existing law relating to land tenures in force in that area and shall also include -  (i) any jagir, inam or muafi or other similar grant and in the <ORG>s of Madras and Kerala, any janmam right;  (ii) any land held under ryotwari settlement;  (ii) any land held or let for purposes of agriculture or  for purposes ancillary thereto, including waste land, forest land, land for pasture or sites of buildings and other structures occupied by cultivators of land, agricultural labourers and village artisans;"  322.
Statute	Section 3 amends the 9th Schedule by adding 44 entries to it.
Statute	323.
RatioOfTheDecision	In dealing with the question about the validity of the impugned Act, it is necessary to consider the scope and effect of the provisions contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 of the Constitution, because the main controversy in the present applications turns upon the decision of the question as to what is the construction of that Article.
Statute	Article 368 reads as follows :  "An amendment of this Constitution may be initiated only by the introduction of a Bill for the purpose in either <ORG> of Parliament, and when the Bill is passed in each house by a majority of the total membership of that <ORG> and by a majority of not less than two-thirds of the members of that <ORG> present and voting, it shall be presented to the President for his assent and upon su ch assent being given to the Bill, the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
Statute	Provided that if such amendment seeks to make any change in -  (a) Article 5 , article 55 , article 73 , article 162 or article 241 , or  (b) Chapter IV of Part V, Chapter V of Part VI, or Chapter I of Part XI, or  (c) any of the Lists in the Seventh Schedule, or  (d) the representation of States in <ORG>, or  (e) the provisions of this article, the amendment shall also require to be ratified by the <ORG> of not less than one-half of the States by resolutions to that effect passed by those <ORG> before the Bill making provision for such amendment is presented to the President for assent".
RatioOfTheDecision	324.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary at this stage to set out briefly the history of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B.
RatioOfTheDecision	These Articles were added to the Constitution with retrospective effect by s. 4 of the Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951.
RatioOfTheDecision	Soon after the promulgation of the Constitution, the political party in po wer, commanding as it did a majority of votes in the several State legislatures as well as in <ORG>, carried out radical measures of agrarian reform in Bihar, Uttar Pradesh and Madhya Pradesh by enacting legislation which may be referred to as Zamindari Abolition Acts.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certain zamindars, feeling themselves aggrieved, attacked the validity of those Acts in courts of law on the ground that they contravened the fundamental rights conferred on them by Part III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> held that the Act passed in Bihar was unconstitutional while <ORG> of Allahabad and Nagpur upheld the validity of the corresponding legislature in Uttar Pradesh and Madhya Pradesh respectively ( <CITATION_219225> (A.I.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	1951 Pat.
RatioOfTheDecision	91) and <NAME> v. U. P. Government) .
RatioOfTheDecision	The parties aggrieved by these respective decisions had filed appeals by special leave before this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, petitions had also been preferred before this <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	32 by certain other zamindars, seeking the d etermination of the same issues.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was at this stage that <ORG>, with a view to put an end to all this litigation and to remedy what they considered to be certain defects brought to light in the working of the Constitution, brought forward a bill to amend the Constitution, which, after undergoing amendments in various particulars, was passed by the requisite majority as the Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951 by which Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B were added to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	That was the first step taken by <ORG> to assist the process of legislation to bring about agrarian reform by introducing Articles 31-A and 31-B.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second step in the same direction was taken by <ORG> in 1955 by amending Art.
Statute	31-A by the Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of this amendment was to widen the scope of agrarian reform and to confer on the legislative measures adopted in that behalf immunity from a possible attack that they contravened the fundamental rights of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other w ords, the amendment protected the legislative measures in respect of certain other items of agrarian and social welfare legislation, which affected the proprietary rights of certain citizens.
Statute	At the time when the first amendment was made, Art.
Statute	31-B expressly provided that none of the Acts and Regulations specified in the 9th Schedule, nor any of the provisions thereof, shall be deemed to be void or ever to have become void on the ground that they were inconsistent with or took away or abridged any of the rights conferred by Part III, and it added that notwithstanding any judgment, decree or order of any <ORG> or tribunal to the contrary, each of the said Acts and Regulations shall subject to the power of any competent legislature to repeal or amend, continue in force.
Statute	At this time, 19 Acts were listed in Schedule 9, and they were thus effectively validated.
Statute	One more Act was added to this list by the Amendment Act of 1955, so that as a result of the second amendment, the Schedule contained 20 Acts which were v alidated.
Precedent	325.
RatioOfTheDecision	It appears that notwithstanding these amendments, certain other legislative measures adopted by different States for the purpose of giving effect to the agrarian policy of the party in power, were effectively challenged.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance the <CITATION_9285> ( Supp.
RatioOfTheDecision	1 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	829), the validity of the Kerala Agrarian Relations Act (IV of 1961) was challenged by writ petitions filed under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	32 , and as a result of the majority decisions of this <ORG>, the whole Act was struck down.
RatioOfTheDecision	The decision of this <ORG> was pronounced on December 5, 1961.
Argument	<CITATION_1980640> , the constitutionality of the Madras Land Reforms (Fixation of Ceiling on Land) Act (No.
Argument	58 of 1961) was the subject-matter of debate, and by the decision of this <ORG> pronounced on March 9, 1964, it was declared that the whole Act was invalid.
Argument	It appears that the Rajasthan Tenancy Act III of 1955 and the Maharashtra Agricultural Lands (Ceiling and Holdings) Act 27 of 1961 had been similarly declared invalid, and in consequence, Parliame nt though it necessary to make a further amendment in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-B so as to save the validity of these <ORG> which had been struck down and of other similar <ORG> which were likely to be challenged.
RatioOfTheDecision	With that object in view, the impugned Act has enacted s. 3 by which 44 <ORG> have been added to Schedule 9.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore clear that the object of the First, Fourth and the Seventeenth Amendments of the Constitution was to help <ORG> to give effect to measures of agrarian reform in a broad and comprehensive sense in the interests of a very large section of Indian citizens whose social and economic welfare closely depends on the pursuit of progressive agrarian policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	326.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first question presented for determination in this case is whether the impugned Act, in so far as it purports to take away or abridge any of the fundamental rights conferred by Part III of the Constitution, falls within the prohibition of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) which provides that "the <ORG> shall not make any law which takes away or abrid ges the rights conferred by this Part and any law made in contravention of this clause shall to the extent of the contravention be void".
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, the argument of the petitioners was that the law to which Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) applies, would include a law passed by <ORG> by virtue of its constituent power to amend the Constitution, and so, its validity will have to be tested by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was contended that "the State" includes <ORG> within Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	12 and "law" must include a constitutional amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was said that it was the deliberate intention of the framers of the Constitution, who realised the sanctity of the fundamental rights conferred by Part III, to make them immune from interference not only by ordinary laws passed by the legislatures in the country but also from constitutional amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, there is no substance in this argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although "law" must ordinarily include constitutional law, there is a juristic distinction between ordinary law made in exercise of legislati ve power and constitutional law which is made in exercise of constituent power.
Precedent	In a written, federal form of Constitution there is a clear and well-known distinction between the law of the Constitution and ordinary law made by the legislature on the basis of separation of powers and pursuant to the power of law-making conferred by the Constitution (See <NAME> on 'Law of the Constitution', Tenth Edn.
Precedent	p. 110, <NAME>, 'Law and the Constitution' pp.
Precedent	62-64, and 'American Jurisprudence', 2nd Edn., Vol.
Precedent	16, p. 181).
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a written Constitution, the amendment of the Constitution is a substantive, constituent act which is made in the exercise of the sovereign power which created the Constitution and which is effected by a special means, namely, by a predesigned fundamental procedure unconnected with ordinary legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The amending power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is hence sui generis and cannot be compared to the law-making power of <ORG> pursuant to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	246 read with List I and III.
RatioOfTheDecision	It follows that the expression " law" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) of the Constitution cannot be construed as including an amendment of the Constitution which is achieved by <ORG> in exercise of its sovereign constituent power, but must mean law made by <ORG> in its legislative capacity pursuant to the powers of law-making given by the Constitution itself under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	246 read with Lists I and III of the 7th Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also clear, on the same line of reasoning, that 'law' in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) cannot be construed so as to include 'law' made by <ORG> under Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	4, 169, 392, 5th Schedule Part D and 6th Schedule para 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The amending power of <ORG> exercised under these Articles stands on the same pedestal as the constitutional amendment made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 so far as Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) is concerned and does not fall within the definition of 'law' within the meaning of this last article.
RatioOfTheDecision	327.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary to add that the definition of 'law' in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(3) does not include in terms a constitutional amendment, though it includes "any Ordinance, order,  bye-law, rule, regulation, notification, custom or usage".
RatioOfTheDecision	It should be noticed that the language of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is perfectly general and empowers <ORG> to amend the Constitution without any exception whatsoever.
RatioOfTheDecision	Had it been intended by the Constitution-makers that the fundamental rights guaranteed under Part III should be completely outside the scope of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it is reasonable to assume that they would have made an express provision to that effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was stressed by the petitioners during the course of the argument that Part III is headed as "Fundamental Rights" and that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	32 " guarantees" the right to move <ORG> by appropriate proceedings for enforcement of rights conferred by Part III.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the expression "fundamental" in the phrase "Fundamental Rights" means that such rights are fundamental vis-a-vis the laws of the legislatures and the acts of the executive authorities mentioned in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	12.
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be suggested that the expression "fundamental" lifts the fundamental rights above t he Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, the expression "guaranteed" in Art.
Precedent	32(1) and 32(4) means that the right to move <ORG> for enforcement of fundamental rights without exhausting the normal channels through <ORG> or the lower courts is guaranteed.
Precedent	This expression also does not place the fundamental rights above the Constitution.
Precedent	328.
Fact	I proceed to consider the next question arising in this case, viz., the scope of the amending power under Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	368 of the Constitution.
RulingByLowerCourt	It is contended on behalf of the petitioners that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 merely lays down the procedure for amendment and does not vest the amending power as such in any agency constituted under that article.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am unable to accept this argument as correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	Part XX of the Constitution which contains only Art.
Fact	368 is described as a Part dealing with the Amendment of the Constitution; and Art.
Fact	368 which prescribes the procedure for amendment of the Constitution, begins by saying that an amendment of this Constitution may be initiated in the  manner therein indicated.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, the expression "amendment of the Constitution" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 plainly and unambiguously means amendment of all the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is unreasonable to suggest that what Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 provides is only the mechanics of the procedure to be followed in amending the Constitution without indicating which provisions of the Constitution can be amended and which cannot.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a restrictive construction of the substantive part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 would be clearly untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The significant fact that a separate Part has been devoted in the Constitution for "amendment of the Constitution" and there is only one Article in that Part shows that both the power to amend and the procedure to amend are enacted in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, the words "the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 clearly contemplate and provide for the power to amend after the requisite procedure has been followed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides, the words used in the proviso unambiguously i ndicate that the substantive part of the article applies to all the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is on that basic assumption that the proviso prescribes a specific procedure in respect of the amendment of the articles mentioned in cls.
RatioOfTheDecision	(a) to (e) thereof.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore it must be held that when Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 confers on <ORG> the right to amend the Constitution the power in question can be exercised over all the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	How the power should be exercised, has to be determined by reference to the question as to whether the proposed amendment falls under the substantive part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , or whether it attracts the procedure contained in the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	329.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was suggested for the petitioners that the power of amendment is to be found in Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	246 and 248 of the Constitution read with item 97 of List I of the 7th Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think that it is possible to accept this argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 246 states that <ORG> has exclusive power to make laws with respect to matters enumerated in L ist I in the Seventh Schedule, and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	248 , similarly, confers power on <ORG> to make any law with respect to any matter not enumerated in the Concurrent List or State List.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the power of law-making in Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	246 and 248 is "subject to the provisions of this Constitution".
RatioOfTheDecision	It is apparent that the power of constitutional amendment cannot fall within these Articles, because it is illogical and a contradiction in terms to say that the amending power can be exercised and at the same time it is "subject to the provisions of the Constitution".
RatioOfTheDecision	330.
RulingByLowerCourt	It was then submitted on behalf of the petitioners that the amending power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is subject to the doctrine of implied limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, it was contended that even if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 confers the power of amendment, it was not a general but restricted power confined only to the amendable provisions of the Constitution, the amendability of such provision being determined by the nature and character of the respective provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued, for inst ance, that the amending power cannot be used to abolish the compact of the Union or to destroy the democratic character of the Constitution guaranteeing individual and minority rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was said that the Constitution was a permanent compact of the States, that the federal character of the States was indissoluble, and that the existence of any of the States as part of the federal compact cannot be put an end to by the power of amendment.
Precedent	It was also said that the chapter of fundamental rights of the Constitution cannot be the subject-matter of any amendment under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It was contended that the preamble to the Constitution declaring that India was a sovereign democratic republic was beyond the scope of the amending power.
Precedent	It was suggested that other basic features of the Constitution were the Articles relating to distribution of legislative powers, the Parliamentary form of Government and the establishment of <ORG> and <ORG> in the various States.
Precedent	I am unable to accept this argument as  correct.
Precedent	If the Constitution makers considered that there were certain basic features of the Constitution which were permanent it is most unlikely that they should not have expressly said in Art.
Precedent	368 that these basic features were not amendable.
Precedent	On the contrary, the Constitution-makers have expressly provided that Art.
Precedent	368 itself should be amendable by the process indicated in the proviso to that Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	This circumstance is significant and suggests that all the articles of the Constitution are amendable either under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 or under the main part of that Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, there is no room for an implication in the construction of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	So far as the federal character of the Constitution is concerned it was held by this <ORG> in <CITATION_603736> , that the federal structure is not an essential part of our Constitution and there is no compact between the States and there is no dual citizenship in India.
Precedent	It was pointed out in that case that there was no constitutional guarantee again st the alteration of boundaries of the <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	By Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	3 the <ORG> is by law authorised to form a new <ORG> by redistribution of the territory of a <ORG> or by uniting two or more <ORG>s or parts of <ORG>s or by uniting any territory to a part of any <ORG>, to increase the area of any <ORG>, to diminish the area of any <ORG>, to alter the boundaries of any <ORG>, and to alter the name of any <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In In Re : The Berubari Union and Exchange of Enclaves , it was argued that the Indo-Pakistan agreement with regard to Berubari could not be implemented even by legislation under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 because of the limitation imposed but he preamble to the Constitution and that such an agreement could not be implemented by a referendum.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument was rejected by this <ORG> and it was held that the preamble could not, in any way, limit the power of <ORG> to cede parts of the national territory.
RatioOfTheDecision	On behalf of the petitioners the argument was stressed that the chapter on fundamental rights was the basic feature of the  Constitution and cannot be the subject of the amending power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued that the freedoms of democratic life are secured by the chapter on fundamental rights and the dignity of the individual cannot be preserved if any of the fundamental rights is altered or diminished.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not possible to accept this argument as correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	The concepts of liberty and equality are changing and dynamic and hence the notion of permanency or immutability cannot be attached to any of the fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Directive Principles of Part IV are as fundamental as the constitutional rights embodied in Part III and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	37 imposes a constitutional duty upon the <ORG>s to apply these principles in making laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference should in particular be made to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	39(b) which enjoins upon the <ORG> to direct its policy towards securing that the ownership and control of the material resources of the community are so distributed as best to subserve the common good.
RatioOfTheDecision	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	38 imposes a duty upon the <ORG> to promote the welf are of the people by securing and protecting as effectively as it may, a social order in which justice, social, economic and political, shall inform all the institutions of the national life.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have already said that the language of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is clear and unambiguous in support of the view that there is no implied limitation on the amending power.
RatioOfTheDecision	In principle also it appears unreasonable to suggest that the Constitution-makers wanted to provide that the fundamental rights guaranteed by the Constitution should never be touched by way of amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	In modern democratic thought there are two main trends - the liberal idea of individual rights protecting the individual and the democratic idea proper proclaiming the equality of rights and popular sovereignty.
RatioOfTheDecision	The gradual extension of the idea of equality from political to economic and social fields in the modern <ORG> has led to the problems of social security, economic planning and industrial welfare legislation.
Precedent	The implementation and harmonisation of these some what conflicting principles is a dynamic task.
Precedent	The adjustment between freedom and compulsion, between the rights of individuals and the social interest and welfare must necessarily be a matter for changing needs and conditions.
Precedent	The proper approach is therefore to look upon the fundamental rights of the individual as conditioned by the social responsibility, by the necessities of the <ORG>, by the balancing of interests and not as pre-ordained and untouchable private rights.
Precedent	331.
Precedent	As pointed out forcefully by <NAME> :  "The struggle for freedom is largely transferred from the plane of political to that of economic rights.
Precedent	Men become less interested in the abstract fragment of political power an individual can secure than in the use of massed pressure of the groups to which they belong to secure an increasing share of the social product.
Precedent	Individualism gives way before socialism.
Precedent	The roots of liberty are held to be in the ownership and control of the instruments of production by the state, the latter using its  power to distribute the results of its regulation with increasing approximation to equality.
Precedent	So long as there is inequality, it is argued, there cannot be liberty.
Precedent	The historic inevitability of this evolution was seen a century ago by <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is interesting to compare his insistence that the democratization of political power meant equality and that its absence would be regarded by the masses as oppression with the argument of Lord Action that liberty and equality are antitheses.
RatioOfTheDecision	To the latter liberty was essentially an autocratic ideal; democracy destroyed individuality, which was the very pith of liberty, by seeking identity of conditions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The modern emphasis is rather toward the principle that material equality is growing inescapable and that the affirmation of personality must be effective upon an immaterial plane.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is found that doing as once likes, subject only to the demands of peace, is incompatible with either international or municipal necessities.
RatioOfTheDecision	we pass from contract to relation, as  we have passed from status to contract.
RatioOfTheDecision	Men are so involved in intricate networks of relations that the place for their liberty is in a sphere where their behavior does not impinge upon that self-affirmation of others which is liberty".
Precedent	(<ORG>, Vol.
Precedent	IX, 445.).
Precedent	332.
Precedent	It must not be forgotten that the fundamental right guaranteed by Art.
Precedent	31 , for instance, is not absolute.
Precedent	It should be noticed that clause (4) of that Article provides an exception to the requirements of Clause (2).
Precedent	Clause (4) relates to Bills of a State Legislature relating to public acquisition which were pending at the commencement of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If such a Bill has been passed and assented to by the President, the <ORG> shall have no jurisdiction to question the validity of such law on the ground of contravention of clause (2) i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	on the ground that it does not provide for compensation or that it has been enacted without a public purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (6) of the Article is another exception to clause (2) and  provides for ouster of jurisdiction of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	While clause (4) relates to Bills pending in the <ORG> <ORG> at the commencement of the Constitution, clause (6) relates to Bills enacted by the <ORG> within 18 months before commencement of the Constitution i.e., Acts providing for public acquisition which were enacted not earlier than July 26, 1948.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the President certifies such an Act within 3 months from the commencement of the Constitution, the <ORG> shall have no jurisdiction to invalidate that Act on the ground of contravention of clause (2) of that Article.
Precedent	similarly, the scheme of Art.
Precedent	19 indicates that the fundamental rights guaranteed by sub-cls.
Precedent	(a) to (g) of clause (1) can be validly regulated in the light of the provisions contained in cls.
Precedent	(2) to (6) of Art.
Precedent	19.
Precedent	In other words, the scheme of Art.
Precedent	19 is two-fold; the fundamental rights of the citizens are of paramount importance, but even the said fundamental rights can be regulated to serve the interests of the general public or other  objects mentioned respectively in cls.
Precedent	(2) to (6) of Art.
Precedent	19.
Precedent	It is right to state that the purposes for which fundamental rights can be regulated which are specified in cls.
Precedent	(2) to (6), could not have been assumed by the Constitution-makers to be static and incapable of expansion.
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be assumed that the Constitution-makers intended to forge a political strait jacket for generations to come.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution-makers must have anticipated that in dealing with socio-economic problems which the legislatures may have to face from time to time, the concepts of public interest and other important considerations which are the basis of cls.
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) to (6), may change and may even expand.
RatioOfTheDecision	As <NAME>, J. has said in <NAME> v. United States (250 U.S. 616, 630) : "the Constitution is an experiment, as all life is an experiment".
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore legitimate to assume that the Constitution-makers intended that <ORG> should be competent to make amendments in these rights so as to meet the challenge of the problems whic h may arise in the course of socio-economic progress and development of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	I find it therefore difficult to accept the argument of the petitioners that the Constitution-makers contemplated that fundamental rights enshrined in Part III were finally and immutably settled and determined once and for all and these rights are beyond the ambit of any future amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Today at a time when absolutes are discredited, it must not be too readily assumed that there are basic features of the Constitution which shackle the amending power and which take precedence over the general welfare of the nation and the need for agrarian and social reform.
Precedent	333.
Precedent	In construing Art.
Precedent	368 it is moreover essential to remember the nature and subject-matter of that Article and to interpret it subjectae materies.
Precedent	The power of amendment is in point of quality an adjunct of sovereignty.
Precedent	It is in truth the exercise of the highest sovereign power in the <ORG>.
Precedent	If the amending power is an adjunct of sovereignty it does not admit of any  limitations.
Precedent	This view is expressed by <NAME> in "Law of the constitution", 10th Edn., at page 148 as follows :  "Hence the power of amending the constitution has been placed, so to speak, outside the constitution, and that the legal sovereignty of the United States resides in the States' governments as forming one aggregate body represented by three-fourths of the several States at any time belonging to the Union".
Precedent	334.
Precedent	A similar view is state by Lord <NAME> in 'The American Commonwealth", Vol.
Precedent	1, ch.
Precedent	XXXII, page 366.
Precedent	<NAME> states as follows in his book.
Precedent	"The Amending of the Federal Constitution" :  "In the last analysis , one is brought to the conclusion that sovereignty in the United States, if it can be said to exist at all, is located in the amending body.
Precedent	The amending body has often been referred to as the sovereign, because it meets the test of the location of sovereignty.
Precedent	As <NAME> has said :  'In all those cases in which, owing to the distribution of governing power, there is d oubt as to the political body in which the Sovereignty rests, the test to be applied is the determination of which authority has, in the last instance, the legal power to determine its own competence as well as that of others'.
Precedent	Applying the criteria of sovereignty which were laid down at the beginning of this chapter, the amending body is sovereignty as a matter of both law and fact.
Precedent	Article Five expressly creates the amending body.
Precedent	Yet in a certain manner of speaking the amending body may be said to exist as a matter of fact since it could proceed to alter Article Five or any other part of the Constitution.
Precedent	While it accrue that the sovereign cannot act otherwise than in compliance with law, it is equally true that it creates the law in accordance with which it is to act".
Precedent	335.
Precedent	In the book "Constitutional Law of the United States", <NAME> says that the doctrine of amendability of the Constitution is based on the doctrine of the sovereignty of the people and that it has no such implied limitatio ns as that an amendment shall not contain a new grant of power nor be in the form of legislation, nor change "our dual form of government nor change the protection of the Bill of Rights, nor make any other change in the Constitution".
Precedent	<NAME> also enunciates the proposition that when a constitutional amendment is adopted "it is done not by the 'general government', but by the supreme sovereign power of the nation i.e., the people, acting through <ORG> or <ORG> conventions" and that "the amending power is equivalent to the Constitution-making power and is wholly above the authority of the Federal Government" - ('Constitutional Problems Under Lincoln', p. 395).
Precedent	The legal position is summarised by <NAME> at page 48 of his treaties "The Law of the American constitution" as follows :  "The result of the National Prohibition Cases (253 U.S. 350) seems to be that there is no limit to the power to amend the Constitution except that a <ORG> may not without its consent be deprived of its equal su ffrage in the <ORG>.
Precedent	To put the case most extremely, this means that by action of two-thirds of both <ORG> and of the legislatures in three-fourths of the States all of the powers of the national government could be surrendered to the States, or all of the reserved powers of the States could be transferred to the federal government.
Precedent	It is only public opinion acting upon these agencies which places any check upon the amending power.
Precedent	But the alternative to this result would be to recognize the power of <ORG> to veto the will of the people expressed in a constitutional amendment without any possibility of the reversal of the court's action except through revolution".
Precedent	336.
Precedent	The matter has been clearly put by <NAME> in Manuel Elementaire De Droit Constitutional (Recueil Sirey) at page 117 as follows :  "Truly speaking no constitution prohibits for ever its amendment or its amendment in all its aspects.
Precedent	But it can prohibit for example, the amendment (revision) during a certain time  (the Constitution of 1791) or it can prohibit the amendment (revision) on this or that point (as in the constitution of 1875) which prohibits amendment of the republican form of Government and the present Constitution follows the same rule.
Precedent	But this prohibition has only a political but no juridical value.
Precedent	In truth from the juridical viewpoint a declaration of absolute constitutional immutability cannot be imagined.
Precedent	The Constituent power being the supreme power in the state cannot be fettered, even by itself.
Precedent	For example, article 95 of our constitution stipulates.
Precedent	"The republican, form of Government cannot be the subject of a proposal for amendment.
Precedent	But juridically the obstacle which this provision puts in the way of an amendment of the republican form of government can be lifted as follows.
Precedent	It is enough to abrogate by way of amendment (revision) the article 95 cited above.
Precedent	After this, the obstacle being removed, a second amendment can deal with the republican form of Government.
Precedent	In practice, this correspo nds to the idea that the constituent assembly of today cannot bind the nation of tomorrow".
Precedent	337.
Precedent	In Re : <ORG> , the argument of implied limitation was advanced by Mr. <NAME> and it was contended that item No.
Precedent	3 of the Indo Pakistan Agreement providing for a division of <ORG> between India and Pakistan was outside the power of constitutional amendment and that the preamble to the Constitution did not permit the dismemberment of India but preserved the integrity of the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	the argument was rejected by this Court and it was held that <ORG> acting under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 can make a law to give effect to and implement the Agreement in question or to pass a law amending Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	3 so as to cover cases of cession of the territory of India and thereafter make a law under the amended Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	3 to implement the Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	338.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is also another aspect of the matter to be taken into account.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the fundamental rights are unamendable and if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 does  not include any such power it follows that the amendment of, say, Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31 by insertion of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B can only be made by a violent revolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was suggested for the petitioners that an alteration of fundamental rights could be made by convening a new <ORG> outside the frame-work of the present <ORG>, but it is doubtful if the proceedings of the new <ORG> will have any legal validity, for the reason is that if the Constitution provides its own method of amendment, any other method of amendment of the Constitution will be unconstitutional and void.
Precedent	For instance, in <NAME> v. <NAME>, as Secretary of State of Ohio (64 L.Ed.
Precedent	871), it was held by <ORG> that Referendum provisions of State Constitutions and statutes cannot be applied in the ratification or rejection of amendments to the Federal Constitution without violating the requirements of Article 5 of such Constitution and that such ratification shall be by the legislat ures of the several states, or by conventions therein, as <ORG> shall decide.
Precedent	It was held in that case that the injunction was properly issued against the calling of a referendum election on the act of the legislature of a state ratifying an amendment to the Federal Constitution.
Precedent	If, therefore, the petitioners are right in their contention that Art.
Precedent	31 is not amendable within the frame-work of the present Constitution, the only other recourse for making the amendment would, as I have already said, be by revolution and not through peaceful means.
Precedent	It cannot be reasonably supposed that the Constitution-makers contemplated that Art.
Precedent	31 or any other article on fundamental rights should be altered by a violent revolution and not by peaceful change.
Precedent	It was observed in <NAME> v. <NAME> (264 Fed.
Precedent	186) :  "If the plaintiff is right in its contention of lack of power to insert the Eighteenth Amendment into the United States Constitution because of its subject-matter, it follows that there is no way to incorporate  it and others of like character into the national organic law, except through revolution.
Precedent	This, the plaintiff concedes, is the inevitable conclusion of its contention.
Precedent	This, is so startling a proposition that the judicial mind may be pardoned for not readily acceding to it, and for insisting that only the most convincing reasons will justify its acceptance".
Precedent	339.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am, therefore, of the opinion that the petitioners are unable to make good their argument on this aspect of the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	340.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was then contended for the petitioners that there would be anomalies if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is interpreted to have no implied limitations.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was said that the more important articles of the Constitution can be amended by the procedure mentioned in the substantive part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 but the less important articles would require ratification by the legislatures of not less than half of the States under the proviso to that Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued that the fundamental rights and also Art.
Precedent	32 could be amended by the majority of two-thirds  of the members of <ORG> but Art.
Precedent	226 cannot be amended unless there was ratification of the legislatures of not less than half of the States.
Precedent	It was pointed out that Arts.
Precedent	54 and 55 were more difficult to amend but not Art.
Precedent	52.
Precedent	Similarly, Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	162 required ratification of the <ORG> but not Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	163 which related to <ORG> to aid and advise the Governor in the exercise of his functions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion the argument proceeds on a misconception.
RatioOfTheDecision	The scheme of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is not to divide the Articles of the Constitution into two categories, viz., important and not so important Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was contemplated by the Constitution-makers that the amending power in the main part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 should extend to each and every article of the Constitution but in the case of such articles which related to the federal principles or the relation of the States with the Union, the ratification of the legislatures of at least half the States should be obtained for any amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was also contended that if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 was construed without any implied limitation the amending power under that Article could be used for subverting the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Both Mr. <NAME> and Mr. <NAME> resorted to the method of reductio ad absurdem in pointing out the abuses that might occur if there were no limitations on the power to amend.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was suggested that <ORG> may, by a constitutional amendment, abolish the parliamentary system of government or repeal the chapter of fundamental rights or divide India into two States, or even reintroduce the rule of a monarch.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is inconceivable that <ORG> should utilise the amending power for bringing about any of these contingencies.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, however, not permissible, in the first place, to assume that in a matter of constitutional amendment there will be abuse of power and then utilise it as a test for finding out the scope of the amending power.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Court has declared repeatedly that the possibility of abuse is not to be used as a test of the existence or extent of a legal power [S ee for example, <CITATION_603736> .
RatioOfTheDecision	In the second place, the amending power is a power of an altogether different kind from the ordinary governmental power and if an abuse occurs, it occurs at the hands of <ORG> and <ORG> representing an extraordinary majority of the people, so that for all practical purposes it may be said to be the people, or at least the highest agent of the people and one exercising sovereign powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore anomalus to speak of 'abuse' of a power of this description.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the last analysis, political machinery and artificial limitations will not protect the people from themselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	The perpetuity of our democratic institutions will depend not upon special mechanisms or devices, nor even upon any particular legislation, but rather upon the character and intelligence and the goods conscience of our people themselves.
Precedent	As observed by <NAME> In <ORG> v. <ORG> (335 U.S. 538, 556) :  "But a democracy need rely on the  courts to save it from its own unwisdom.
Precedent	If it is alert - and without alterness by the people there can be no enduring democracy - unwise or unfair legislation can readily be removed from the statute books.
Precedent	It is by such vigilance over its representatives that democracy proves itself".
Precedent	341.
Precedent	I pass on to consider the next objection of the petitioners that the true purpose and object of the impugned Act was to legislate in respect of land and that legislation in respect of land falls within the jurisdiction of State legislatures under Entry 18 of List II, and the argument was that since the State <ORG> alone can make laws in respect of land, <ORG> had no right to pass the impugned Act.
Precedent	The argument was based on the assumption that the impugned Act purports to be, and in fact is, a piece of land legislation.
Precedent	It was urged that the scheme of Arts.
Precedent	245 and 246 of the Constitution clearly shows that <ORG> has no right to make a law in respect of land, and since the impugned Act is a legislative mea sure in relation to land, it is invalid.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, the argument is based upon a misconception.
RatioOfTheDecision	What the impugned Act purports to do is not to make any land legislation but to protect and validate the legislative measures in respect of agrarian reforms passed by the different <ORG> in the country by granting them immunity from attack based on the plea that they contravene fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned Act was passed by <ORG> in exercise of the amending power conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and it is impossible to accept the argument that the constitutional power of amendment can be fettered by Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	245 and 246 or by the legislative Lists.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was argued for the petitioners that <ORG> cannot validate a law which it has no power to enact.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proposition holds good where the validity of an impugned Act turns on whether the subject-matter falls within or without the jurisdiction of the legislature which passed it.
RatioOfTheDecision	But to make a law which contravenes the Constitution constitutionally valid is  a mater of constitutional amendment, and as such it falls within the exclusive power of <ORG> and within the amending power conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am accordingly of the opinion that the petitioners are unable to substantiate their argument on this aspect of the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	I should like to add that in <NAME> v. <NAME> (258 U.S. 130), in National Prohibition Cases (253 U.S. 350) and in United States v. <NAME> (282 U.S. 716), a similar argument was advanced to the effect that a constitutional amendment was not valued if it was in the form of legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the argument was rejected by <ORG> in all the three cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	342.
RatioOfTheDecision	It remains to deal with the objection of the petitioners that the newly inserted articles 31-A and 31-B require ratification of the State legislatures under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 of the Constitution because these articles deprive <ORG> of the power to issue appropriate writs under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think there is any substance in th is argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned Act does not purport to change the provisions of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and it cannot be said even to have that effect directly or in any substantial measure.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is manifest that the newly inserted articles do not either in terms or in effect seek to make any change in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 of the Constitution.
Statute	Article 31-A aims at saving laws providing for the compulsory acquisition by the <ORG> of a certain kind of property from the operation of article 13 read with other relevant articles in Part III, while article 31-B purports to validate certain specified Acts and Regulations already passe,d which, but for such a provision, would be liable to be impugned under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore not correct to say that the powers of <ORG> to issue writs is, in any way affected.
RatioOfTheDecision	The jurisdiction of the <ORG> remains just the same as it was before.
RatioOfTheDecision	Only a certain category of cases has been excluded from the purview of Part III and the <ORG> can no longer intervene, not because their jurisdiction or  powers have been curtailed in any manner or to any extent, but because there would be no occasion hereafter for the exercise of their power in such cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	As I have already said, the effect of the impugned Act on the jurisdiction of <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 of the Constitution is not direct but only incidental in character and therefore the contention of the petitioners on this point against the validity of the impugned Act must be rejected.
RatioOfTheDecision	343.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well-settled that in examining a constitutional question of this character, it is legitimate to consider whether the impugned legislation is a legislation directly in respect of the subject-matter covered by any particular article of the Constitution or whether it touches the said article only incidentally or indirectly.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1857950> ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	88, 101), Kania, C.J., had occasion to consider the validity of the argument that the preventive detention order resulted in the detention of the applicant in a cell, and so, it contravened his fundamental rig hts guaranteed by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1)(a ), (b ), (c ), (d ), (e) and (g).
RatioOfTheDecision	Rejecting this argument, the learned Chief Justice observed that the true approach in dealing with such a question was only to consider the directness of the legislation and not what will be the result of the detention otherwise valid, on the mode of the detenu's life.
RatioOfTheDecision	On that ground alone, he was inclined to reject the contention that the order of the detention contravened the fundamental rights guaranteed to the petitioner under Art.
Precedent	19(1).
Precedent	At page 100 of the report, <NAME> stated as follows :  "As the preventive detention order results in the detention of the applicant in a cell it was contended on his behalf that the rights specified in Article 19(1)(a ), (b ), (c ), (d ), (e ), and (g) have been infrigned.
Precedent	It was argued that because of his detention he cannot have a free right to speech as and where he desired and the same argument was urged in respect of the rest of the rights mentioned in sub-clauses (b), (c), (d), (e) and (g).
Precedent	Altho ugh this argument is advanced in a case which deals with preventive detention, if correct, it should be applicable in the case of punitive detention also to any one sentenced to a term of imprisonment under the relevant section of the Indian Penal Code .
RatioOfTheDecision	So considered, the argument must clearly be rejected, In spite of the saving clauses (2) to (6), permitting abridgement of the rights connected with each of them, punitive detention under several sections of the Penal Code , i.e., for theft, cheating, forgery and even ordinary assault, will be illegal.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unless such conclusion necessarily follows from the article, it is obvious that such construction should be avoided.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, such result is clearly not the outcome of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article has to be read without any pre-conceived notions.
RatioOfTheDecision	So read, it clearly means that the legislation to be examined must be directly in respect of one of the rights mentioned in the sub-clauses.
RatioOfTheDecision	If there is a legislation directly attempting to control a citizen's f reedom of speech or expression, or his right to assemble peaceably and without arms, etc., the question whether that legislation is saved by the relevant saving clause of article 19 will arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, however, the legislation is not directly in respect of any of these subjects, but as a result of the operation of other legislation, for instance, for punitive or preventive detention, his right under any of these sub-clauses is abridged, the question of the application of article 19 does not arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	The true approach is only to consider the directness of the legislation and not what will be the result of the detention otherwise valid, on the mode of the detenu's life.
RatioOfTheDecision	On that short ground, in my opinion, this argument about the infringement of the rights mentioned in article 19(1) generally must fail.
Precedent	Any other construction put on the article, it seems to me, will be unreasonable".
Precedent	344.
Precedent	It is true that the opinion thus expressed by <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	in the case of <CITATION_1857950> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	88), did not receive the con currence of the other learned Judges who heard the said case.
Precedent	Subsequently, however, in <CITATION_40472258> .
Precedent	( S.C.R.
Precedent	451, 456), the said observations were cited with approval by <ORG>.
Precedent	The same principle was accepted by this <ORG> in <CITATION_1902038> ( S.C.R.
Precedent	12, 129-30), in the majority judgment in <CITATION_514162> , and in <CITATION_1643138> .
RatioOfTheDecision	Applying the same principle to the present case, I consider that the effect of the impugned Act on the powers of the High <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 is indirect and incidental and not direct.
RatioOfTheDecision	I hold that the impugned Act falls under the substantive part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 because the object of the impugned Act is to amend the relevant Articles in Part III which confer fundamental rights on citizens and not to change the power of the High <ORG>s under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226.
RatioOfTheDecision	345.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection I should like to refer to another aspect of the matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question about the validity of the Constitution (First Amendment) Act has been considered by this <ORG> in <CITATION_1706770 > ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89).
RatioOfTheDecision	In that case, the validity of the said Amendment Act was challenged, firstly, on the ground that the newly inserted Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B sought to make changes in Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	132 and 136 in Ch.
RatioOfTheDecision	IV of Part V and Art.
Fact	226 in Ch.
Fact	V of Part VI.
RulingByLowerCourt	The second ground was that the amendment was invalid because it related to legislation in respect of land.
RulingByLowerCourt	It was also urged, in the third place, that though it may be open to <ORG> to amend the provisions in respect of fundamental rights contained in Part III, the amendment made in that behalf would have to be tested in the light of provisions of Art.
Precedent	13(2) of the Constitution.
Precedent	The argument was that the law to which Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) applied would include a law passed by <ORG> by virtue of its constituent power to amend the Constitution, and so, its validity will have to be tested by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	All these arguments were rejected by this court and it was held in that case that the Constitution (First Amendment) Act was legally valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same question ar ose for consideration in <CITATION_1308308> , with regard to the validity of the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that case, the petitioner in their Writ Petition in this <ORG> contended that the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act was constitutionally invalid since the powers prescribed by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 , which is in Ch.
RatioOfTheDecision	V, Part VI of the Constitution, were likely to be affected by the Seventeenth Amendment, and therefore the special procedure laid down under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 should have been followed.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was further contended in that case that the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89) case should be reconsidered.
RatioOfTheDecision	Both the contentions were rejected by this <ORG> by a majority Judgment and it was held that the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act amended the fundamental rights solely with the object of assisting the State Legislatures to give effect to the socio-economic policy of the party in power and its effect on Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 was incidental and insignificant and the impugned Ac t therefore fell under the substantive part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and not attract the proviso to that article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was further held by this <ORG> that there was no justification for reconsidering <NAME> ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89) case.
RatioOfTheDecision	On behalf of the respondents it was submitted by the Additional Solicitor-General that this was a very strong case for the application of the principle of stare decisis.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, this contention must be accepted as correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the arguments urged by the petitioners are to prevail it would lead to the inevitable consequence that the amendments made to the Constitution both in 1951 and in 1955 would be rendered invalid and a large number of decisions dealing with the validity of the acts included in the 9th Schedule which were pronounced by this <ORG> ever since the decision in <NAME> ( S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	89) case was declared, would also have to be overruled.
Precedent	It was also pointed out that <ORG>, the <ORG> and the people have acted on the faith of the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> ari Prasad's ( S.C.R.
Precedent	89) case and titles to property have been transferred, obligations have been incurred and rights have been acquired in the implementation of the legislation included in the 9th Schedule.
Precedent	346.
Precedent	The effect of land reform legislation has been clearly summarised in Ch.
Precedent	VIII of Draft Outline on Fourth Plan as follows :  "Fifteen years ago when the First Plan was being formulated, intermediary tenures like zamindaris, jagirs and inams covered more than 40 per cent.
Precedent	of the area.
Precedent	There were large disparities in the ownership of land held under ryotwari tenure which covered the other 60 per cent.
Precedent	area; and a substantial portion of the land was cultivated through tenants-at-will and share-croppers who paid about one-half the produce as rent.
RatioOfTheDecision	Most holdings were small and fragmented.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides, there was a large population of land-less agricultural labourers.
RatioOfTheDecision	In these conditions the principal measures recommended for securing the objectives of the land policy were the abolition of intermediary tenures , reform of the tenancy system, including fixation of fair rent at one-fifth to one-fourth of the gross produce, security of tenure for the tenant, bringing tenants into direct relationship with the <ORG> and investing in them ownership of land.
RatioOfTheDecision	A ceiling on land holding was also recommended so that some surplus land may be made available for redistribution to the landless agricultural workers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another important part of the programme was consolidation of agricultural holdings and increase in the size of the operational unit to an economic scale through cooperative methods.
RatioOfTheDecision	Abolition of Intermediaries.
RatioOfTheDecision	- During the past 15 years, progress has been made in several directions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The programme for the abolition of intermediaries has been carried out practically all over the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	About 20 million tenants of former intermediaries came into direct relationship with the <ORG> and became owners of their holdings.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> Governments are now engaged in the assessment and payment of compensation.
RatioOfTheDecision	There were some initia l delays but a considerable progress has been made in this direction in recent years and it is hoped that the issue of compensatory bonds will be completed in another two years.
RatioOfTheDecision	Tenancy Reform.
RatioOfTheDecision	- To deal with the problem of tenants-at-will in the ryotwari areas and of sub-tenants in the zamindari areas, a good deal of legislation has been enacted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Provisions for security of tenure, for bringing them into direct relation with the <ORG> and converting them into owners have been made in several <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	As a result about 3 million tenants and sharecroppers have acquired ownership of more than 7 million acres.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ceiling on Holdings.
RatioOfTheDecision	- Laws imposing ceiling on agricultural holdings have been enacted in all the <ORG>s.
Precedent	In the former Punjab area, however, the <ORG> Government has the power to settle tenants on land in excess of the permissible limit although it has not set a ceiling on ownership.
Precedent	According to available reports over 2 million acres of surplus areas in excess of the ceiling limits have been declared or  taken possession of by <ORG>".
Precedent	347.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the principle of stare decisis may not strictly apply to a decision on a constitutional point.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no restriction in the Constitution itself which prevents this <ORG> from reviewing its earlier decisions or even to depart from them in the interest of public good.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the problem of construing constitutional provisions cannot be adequately solved by merely adopting the literal construction of the words used in the various articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is an organic document and it is intended to serve as a guide to the solution of changing problems which the <ORG> may have to face from time to time.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is manifest that in a progressive and dynamic society the character of these problems is bound to change with the inevitable consequence that the relevant words used in the Constitution may also change their meaning and significance.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even so, the <ORG> is reluctant to accede to the suggestion that its earlier decisions should be frequently  reviewed or departed from.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a case the test should be : what is the nature of the error alleged in the earlier decision, what is its impact on the public good and what is the compelling character of the considerations urged in support of the contrary view.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also a relevant factor that the earlier decision has been followed in a large number of cases, that titles to property have passed and multitude of rights and obligations have been created in consequence of the earlier decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have already dealt with the merits of the contention of the petitioners with regard to the validity of the impugned Act and I have given reasons for holding that the impugned Act is constitutionally valid and the contentions of the petitioners are unsound.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even on the assumption that it is possible to take a different view and to hold that the impugned Act is unconstitutional I am of opinion that the principle of stare decisis must be applied to the present case and the plea made by the petitioners for reconsideration  of <NAME> ([1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89) case and the decision in <CITATION_1308308> , is wholly unjustified and must be rejected.
Precedent	348.
Precedent	In Writ Petition No.
Precedent	202 of 1966, it was contended by Mr. <NAME> that the continuance of the Proclamation of Emergency under Art.
Precedent	352 of the Constitution was a gross violation of power because the emergency had ceased to exist.
Precedent	It was also contended that Art.
Precedent	358 should be so construed as to confine its operation only to legislative or executive action relevant to the Proclamation of Emergency.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was submitted that the Mysore State was not a border area and the land reform legislation of that State had no relevant connection with the Proclamation of Emergency and the fundamental rights conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 cannot be suspended so far as the petitions are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think that it is necessary to express any opinion on these points because the Writ Petition must fail on the other grounds which I have already discussed above.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also not necessary for me to express  an opinion on the doctrine of prospective overruling of legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	349.
RulingByPresentCourt	For the reasons already expressed I hold that all these petitions fail and should be dismissed, but there will be no order as to costs.
RulingByPresentCourt	350.
RulingByPresentCourt	Petitions dismissed.
